Liability of the Coercer for Property Damage
Ruling on coercer's liability for property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the owner may claim damages from the coercer and explain the legal reasoning that the coercer is the true cause.

Core Idea
The coercer is the true cause of damage; the coerced person is his tool.

This lesson covers the first part of the passage: the owner's right to claim damages from the coercer when property is destroyed under duress. The principle is that the coercer is the effective cause (mu'aththir), making the coerced person merely an instrument (ala). This rule applies to property damage and distinguishes the coercer's liability from that of the direct actor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine that the owner may claim damages (damān) from the coercer (mukrih). The act is the destruction of property under duress.
The ruling is that the coercer is liable, not the coerced person.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The reasoning is talil: the coercer is the one who destroys the property because he compels the action, and the coerced is like his tool. This is based on the principle of causation (tasabbub).
The matn states: 'because he is the one who destroys his property, and the coerced person is his tool.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
The link is that the coercer's intention and compulsion make him the real destroyer; the coerced lacks volition in the actual damage. Thus liability attaches to the cause, not the immediate actor.
This is analogous to cases where the direct actor is an instrument (ala).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وللمالك أن يضمن لمكره لأنه المتلف لماله والمكره آلة له فيما يصلح آلة . رحمه الله : ( وعلى قتل غيره بقتل لا يرخص) يعني لو أكره على قتل غيره بالقتل لا يرخص له القتل لإحياء نفسه لأن دليل الرخصة خوف التلف والمكره والمكره عليه سواء في ذلك فسقط المكره، ولأن قتل المسلم بغير حق مما لا يستباح لضرورة ما فكذا بالإكراه وهذا نزاع فيه. وأطلق في قوله «غيره فشمل الحر والعبد وعبده وعبد غيره. وفي المحيط : لو أكره بقتله أن يقتل عبده أو يقطع يده لم يسعه ذلك فإن قتل يأثم ويقتل المكره في القتل ويضمن نصف قيمته لأن دمه حرام بأصل الفطرة، ولو أكره بقتل على أن يقتل أباه أو ابنه فقتله لم يحرمه عن الميراث، ولو كان المكره أبا المقتول أو ابنه يحرم عن الميراث لأن المباشر للقتل هو المكره، ولو أكره بقتل على أن يضرب رجلاً بحديدة فضربه وثنى بغير إكراه فمات قتلاً جميعاً لأن إحدى الضربتين بغير إكراه فصارت منقولة إليه والأخرى منقولة إلى المكره، ولو كانت إحدى الضربتين بعصاة غرم عاقلة كل واحد منهما نصف الدية في ثلاث سنين، وإن كان الإكراه بحبس أو قيد فالضمان على الضارب قوداً كان أو دية لأن الإكراه بالحبس لا يعتبر إكراها في حق هذه الأحكام وفيه أيضاً : ولو أكره بقتل على أن يأمل رجلاً بقتل عبده
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the owner may claim damages from the coercer because he is the one who destroys his property, and the coerced person is his tool in what is suitable as a tool. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And regarding killing another with death, it is not permitted) meaning if one is coerced by death to kill another, he is not permitted to kill to save his own life, because the basis of the concession is fear of destruction, and the coercer and the one coerced upon are equal in that, so the coerced person is dropped? And because killing a Muslim without right is not permissible for any necessity, so likewise under duress, and there is a dispute in this. And he said in his statement 'other than him' includes free, slave, his own slave, and another's slave. And in al-Muhit: If one is coerced by death to kill his slave or cut his hand, it is not permissible for him; if he kills, he sins and the coercer is killed in retaliation and he guarantees half his value because his blood is sacred by original disposition. And if coerced by death to kill his father or son, and he kills, he is not deprived of inheritance; but if the coercer is the father of the killed or his son, he is deprived of inheritance because the direct killer is the coercer. And if coerced by death to strike a man with an iron, and he strikes and then a second strike without duress, and he dies, both are considered killers because one strike was without duress, so it is attributed to him, and the other is attributed to the coercer. And if one of the two strikes was with a stick, the 'aahila of each pays half the blood money over three years. And if the duress is by imprisonment or shackles, then the guarantee is on the striker, whether retaliation or blood money, because duress by imprisonment is not considered duress in the context of these rulings. And in it also: If coerced by death to 'amala' a man to kill his slave...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage covers two matn phrases: (1) The owner may claim damages from the coercer because the coercer is the true destroyer, and the coerced person acts merely as his tool. (2) Killing another person under threat of death is not permitted. The commentator explains that the concession of saving one's own life does not extend to killing an innocent person, because the coercer and the victim are equal in terms of life. The prohibition of killing a Muslim without right is absolute. Various scenarios are detailed from al-Muhit: killing or maiming one's own slave under duress is not allowed; if done, the coercer faces retaliation and pays half the value. Killing a father or son does not deprive the coerced killer of inheritance (the coercer is the direct cause), but if the coercer is the father, he is deprived. If a person strikes with a knife under duress and then strikes a second time willingly, both strikes contribute to death, and the liability is divided. If duress is by imprisonment (not fear of death), it is not considered effective duress, and the striker bears full responsibility. The passage ends with a scenario about being coerced to 'amala' (perhaps 'kill') a man's slave, where the text cuts off.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for damages; the coercer must compensate for property destroyed.
مكره (mukrih): The person who compels another to act under duress.
آلة (āla): Tool or instrument; the coerced person is considered a mere tool of the coercer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, who is liable when property is destroyed under duress?
A) The coerced person
B) The coercer
C) Both equally
D) Neither, because duress invalidates liability

Key Takeaway
The coercer is liable for property damage caused by the coerced person because the coercer is the true cause.

Prohibition of Killing Another Under Duress
Ruling on killing another under duress with scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi law prohibits killing an innocent person under duress, even to save one's own life, and apply the ruling to various scenarios.

Core Idea
Killing an innocent person under duress is absolutely prohibited; one must not kill to save oneself.

This lesson examines the second major ruling in the passage: when a person is coerced under threat of death to kill another, he is not permitted to do so. The rationale is that the life of the coerced person and the victim are equal, and killing a Muslim without right is never permissible. The passage also explores numerous applications from al-Muhit, including killing slaves, relatives, and multiple strikes.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
If a person is forced under threat of death to kill another, he may not kill to save his own life. The act remains haram.
The matn says: 'on killing another by death, it is not permitted.'
2
Identify the proof
The proof is twofold: (1) the basis of the concession (rukhsa) is fear of destruction, but here the coercer and the victim are equal in life, so no concession applies; (2) killing a Muslim without right is not permitted for any necessity.
The text states explicitly: 'because killing a Muslim without right is not permissible for any necessity.'
3
Analyze the scenarios
The passage applies the rule to killing one's own slave, a father, a son, and multiple strikes. Determine the ruling in each: in all cases, the coerced is not allowed to kill, and if he does, he sins.
In al-Muhit: killing one's own slave is not allowed; killing a father does not deprive inheritance; etc.
4
Distinguish effective duress
Duress by imprisonment is not considered effective for these rulings; the direct actor bears liability.
The text says: 'if the duress is by imprisonment... the guarantee is on the striker.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وللمالك أن يضمن لمكره لأنه المتلف لماله والمكره آلة له فيما يصلح آلة . رحمه الله : ( وعلى قتل غيره بقتل لا يرخص) يعني لو أكره على قتل غيره بالقتل لا يرخص له القتل لإحياء نفسه لأن دليل الرخصة خوف التلف والمكره والمكره عليه سواء في ذلك فسقط المكره، ولأن قتل المسلم بغير حق مما لا يستباح لضرورة ما فكذا بالإكراه وهذا نزاع فيه. وأطلق في قوله «غيره فشمل الحر والعبد وعبده وعبد غيره. وفي المحيط : لو أكره بقتله أن يقتل عبده أو يقطع يده لم يسعه ذلك فإن قتل يأثم ويقتل المكره في القتل ويضمن نصف قيمته لأن دمه حرام بأصل الفطرة، ولو أكره بقتل على أن يقتل أباه أو ابنه فقتله لم يحرمه عن الميراث، ولو كان المكره أبا المقتول أو ابنه يحرم عن الميراث لأن المباشر للقتل هو المكره، ولو أكره بقتل على أن يضرب رجلاً بحديدة فضربه وثنى بغير إكراه فمات قتلاً جميعاً لأن إحدى الضربتين بغير إكراه فصارت منقولة إليه والأخرى منقولة إلى المكره، ولو كانت إحدى الضربتين بعصاة غرم عاقلة كل واحد منهما نصف الدية في ثلاث سنين، وإن كان الإكراه بحبس أو قيد فالضمان على الضارب قوداً كان أو دية لأن الإكراه بالحبس لا يعتبر إكراها في حق هذه الأحكام وفيه أيضاً : ولو أكره بقتل على أن يأمل رجلاً بقتل عبده
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the owner may claim damages from the coercer because he is the one who destroys his property, and the coerced person is his tool in what is suitable as a tool. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And regarding killing another with death, it is not permitted) meaning if one is coerced by death to kill another, he is not permitted to kill to save his own life, because the basis of the concession is fear of destruction, and the coercer and the one coerced upon are equal in that, so the coerced person is dropped? And because killing a Muslim without right is not permissible for any necessity, so likewise under duress, and there is a dispute in this. And he said in his statement 'other than him' includes free, slave, his own slave, and another's slave. And in al-Muhit: If one is coerced by death to kill his slave or cut his hand, it is not permissible for him; if he kills, he sins and the coercer is killed in retaliation and he guarantees half his value because his blood is sacred by original disposition. And if coerced by death to kill his father or son, and he kills, he is not deprived of inheritance; but if the coercer is the father of the killed or his son, he is deprived of inheritance because the direct killer is the coercer. And if coerced by death to strike a man with an iron, and he strikes and then a second strike without duress, and he dies, both are considered killers because one strike was without duress, so it is attributed to him, and the other is attributed to the coercer. And if one of the two strikes was with a stick, the 'aahila of each pays half the blood money over three years. And if the duress is by imprisonment or shackles, then the guarantee is on the striker, whether retaliation or blood money, because duress by imprisonment is not considered duress in the context of these rulings. And in it also: If coerced by death to 'amala' a man to kill his slave...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage covers two matn phrases: (1) The owner may claim damages from the coercer because the coercer is the true destroyer, and the coerced person acts merely as his tool. (2) Killing another person under threat of death is not permitted. The commentator explains that the concession of saving one's own life does not extend to killing an innocent person, because the coercer and the victim are equal in terms of life. The prohibition of killing a Muslim without right is absolute. Various scenarios are detailed from al-Muhit: killing or maiming one's own slave under duress is not allowed; if done, the coercer faces retaliation and pays half the value. Killing a father or son does not deprive the coerced killer of inheritance (the coercer is the direct cause), but if the coercer is the father, he is deprived. If a person strikes with a knife under duress and then strikes a second time willingly, both strikes contribute to death, and the liability is divided. If duress is by imprisonment (not fear of death), it is not considered effective duress, and the striker bears full responsibility. The passage ends with a scenario about being coerced to 'amala' (perhaps 'kill') a man's slave, where the text cuts off.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Duress or coercion, especially threat of death that overcomes volition.
دم معصوم (dam ma'sūm): Sacred blood of a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) whose killing is prohibited.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for damages, here blood money (diya) or retaliation (qisas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person is coerced under threat of death to kill his father, what is the ruling on inheritance?
A) The killer is deprived of inheritance
B) The killer inherits normally
C) The coercer inherits
D) The killer must pay diya but inherits

Key Takeaway
Killing an innocent under duress is absolutely prohibited; the coercer bears liability, and the coerced must not comply.

Liability of the Coercer for Property Damage
Ruling that the coercer is liable for property damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a person is coerced to destroy property and identify the legal reasoning (talil) that the coerced person is considered a tool of the coercer.

Core Idea
The coercer is the true destroyer; the coerced person is merely his tool.

When someone is forced to break another's property, who bears the liability? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: the coercer, because the coerced person is merely an instrument. This brief rule turns on the concept of āla (tool) and establishes a key principle in tort liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text states that the owner of property may claim compensation (yaḍman) from the coercer. Note that the ruling is about a legal right, not an obligation.
The owner has the option to seek damages from the coercer.
2
Locate the talil (reason)
The reason given: 'because he (the coercer) is the destroyer (mutlif) of his (the owner's) property.' The coercer is legally considered the direct cause of the loss.
The talil is explicit: 'li-annahu al-mutlif li-mālihi'.
3
Understand the tool metaphor
The coerced person is described as a 'tool' (āla) of the coercer. In Hanafi jurisprudence, a tool is a passive instrument; liability for its action is attributed to the user.
This applies when the act is suitable for a tool—i.e., the coerced person has no effective choice.
4
Apply the condition
The phrase 'fīmā yaṣluḥu āla' means this ruling only applies when the coerced act is one where a person can serve as a mere tool, such as physical destruction. Acts requiring intention or personal volition are excluded.
The tool metaphor does not extend to acts like divorce or freeing a slave under coercion.
5
Contrast with direct liability
If the coerced person had meaningful choice or the act required his own intent, he would be the primary actor. Here, the coercer's threat eliminates free choice, so the coercer is the mutlif.
This is a key distinction in Hanafi causation theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمالك أن يضمن لمكره لأنه المتلف لماله والمكره آلة له فيما يصلح آلة.
And the owner may claim compensation from the coercer because he is the destroyer of his property, and the coerced person is [like] his tool in what is suitable as a tool.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This is a ruling from the author stating that when a person is coerced to destroy another's property, the liability falls on the coercer, not the coerced individual. The reason given is that the coercer is the true cause (mutlif) of the loss, and the coerced person acts merely as an instrument (āla) of the coercer. This is based on the Hanafi legal principle that the direct cause (mubāshir) is attributed to the coercer when the coerced person has no valid choice. The phrase 'in what is suitable as a tool' indicates that this applies when the coerced act is one where the person could serve as a mere physical tool, such as destroying property. This is distinguished from acts requiring intention or volition, where the coerced person might be considered the primary actor.

Key Terms
يضمن (yaḍman): He is liable for compensation; the legal obligation to pay damages.
آلة (āla): A tool or instrument; the coerced person is considered a tool of the coercer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone is coerced to break another's window, who is liable for compensation according to the text?
A) The coerced person, because he did the breaking
B) The coercer, because he is the true destroyer
C) Both are equally liable
D) No one is liable because it was under coercion

Key Takeaway
The coercer is liable for property damage caused under coercion; the coerced person is considered a tool.

Killing Under Coercion: The Sin and Its Proof
Explanation of why killing under coercion is sinful

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that killing under coercion is sinful, explain the proof based on the remaining prohibition, and apply the same reasoning to distinguish between male and female cases of coercion to adultery.

Core Idea
Killing under coercion remains sinful because the prohibition is absolute; the actor, not the coercer, bears the sin.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical question: does coercion remove sin? The commentator affirms that the prohibition of murder is not lifted by duress. He then draws an analogy with coercion to adultery, showing a gender-based distinction rooted in the nature of the harm caused.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the hukm: killing under coercion is haram and sinful (ithm). The text opens with 'wa in qatalahu ithm'.
The sin attaches to the direct actor, not the coercer.
2
Trace the proof
The proof is that the prohibition of murder remains absolute; the act is directly performed by the coerced person, so sin is on his liability. The coercer cannot be an instrument for him in this matter.
The dalil is aqli (rational): the act is attributable to the doer.
3
Analyze the analogy to adultery
The text extends the same principle: a man coerced into adultery is not permitted because it involves 'killing the soul through waste' (a child without a father) and corrupts the marital bed. This is analogous to killing.
The harm is similar: irreversible damage to lineage and family.
4
Distinguish the female case
For a woman, overwhelming coercion (ikrah mulji') permits her to commit adultery because her child's lineage is not severed (it remains with her), so the harm is not in the same category as killing.
The distinction is based on the type of harm, not on gender per se.
5
Summarize the rule
The Hanafi principle: coercion does not lift the prohibition of acts that cause irreversible harm to life or lineage; permission is granted only where the harm is lesser and the lineage is preserved.
This illustrates the use of istihsan in balancing harms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن قتله إثم) لأن الحرمة باقية لما ذكرنا وأثم بمباشرته لأن الإثم يكون بذمته والمكره لا يصلح أن يكون آلة له في حقه، وكذا لو أكره على الزنا لا يرخص له لأن فيه قتل النفس بالضياع لأنه يجيء منه ولد ليس له أب ولأن فيه إفساد الفراش بخلاف جانب المرأة حيث يرخص لها بالإكراه الملجئ لأن نسب الولد لا ينقطع فلم يكن في معنى القتل في جانبها بخلاف الرجل ولهذا وجب الإكراه القاصر درء الحد في حقها دون الرجل.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he kills him, it is a sin) because the prohibition remains as we mentioned, and he is sinful by his direct action because sin attaches to his liability, and the coercer is not fit to be an instrument for him in this matter. Similarly, if he is coerced into adultery, he is not permitted because it involves killing the soul through wastage, as a child results from it without a father, and because it corrupts the marital bed. This is unlike the woman's side, where she is permitted under overwhelming coercion because the lineage of the child is not severed; thus it is not in the same category as killing on her side, unlike the man. For this reason, incomplete coercion necessitates warding off the punishment for her but not for the man.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses the legal status of killing under coercion: although the act may be externally compelled, the sin remains on the person who directly commits the murder. The commentator explains that the prohibition of murder is absolute and not lifted by coercion. The analogy with coercion to commit adultery is given: a man coerced into adultery is not permitted because it leads to the harm of a child without a known father and spoils the marital bed. A woman, however, is permitted under overwhelming coercion because her child's lineage is not lost. This distinction rests on the differing harms: for the man, the harm is akin to killing (by causing waste), while for the woman, it is not.

Key Terms
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin attached to the agent despite coercion; the act is prohibited and the doer bears moral responsibility.
الإكراه الملجئ (al-ikrah al-mulji'): Overwhelming coercion that leaves no choice, such as threat of death; under such coercion, certain permissions apply (e.g., for a woman to commit adultery).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a man coerced into adultery not permitted (la yurrakhkhas) under coercion to kill?
A) Because the act is physically impossible
B) Because it leads to the harm of a child without a father and spoils the marital bed
C) Because the coercion is not severe enough
D) Because the man is always considered the aggressor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the sin of killing under coercion falls on the direct agent, and the analogy with adultery shows that permission depends on the type of harm and gender.

Coercion to Kill vs. Apostasy or Eating Carrion: The Hanafi Position
Comparing permissibility of apostasy vs. killing under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why killing under coercion is never permitted in the Hanafi school, while apostasy and eating carrion become permissible under dire necessity, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the killer is executed.

Core Idea
Killing under coercion is never permissible; apostasy and eating carrion are allowed in extremis.

In the Hanafi legal tradition, coercion (ikrah) can lift the prohibition on certain acts when life is at stake, but not all prohibitions are equal. This lesson examines a key passage from al-Muhit that compares three scenarios: coercion to kill, to commit apostasy, or to eat carrion or drink wine. You will see how the school prioritizes the sanctity of life over the obligation to preserve faith or avoid impurity, and why a legal doubt (shubha) may spare the coerced killer from capital punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three coercion scenarios
The passage presents two sets: first, coercion to kill vs. apostasy; second, coercion to kill vs. eating carrion or drinking wine. Note the ruling for each alternative.
2
Understand the principle of necessity (iḍṭirār)
Apostasy and consuming carrion/wine are normally haram, but under dire necessity they become permissible. Killing, however, is never permitted even under coercion. Grasp the hierarchy: preservation of life takes precedence over preservation of faith or avoiding impurity.
The Hanafi school holds that apostasy uttered under duress is not real apostasy; it is a concession to save life.
3
Analyze the dispute on qisas for the coerced killer
If the coerced person kills without committing apostasy (i.e., he chose to kill rather than apostatize), there is disagreement: one view says he is executed because he killed willingly; another view says he is not executed due to the legal doubt (shubha) arising from the prohibition of apostasy—implying that perhaps he should have apostatized instead.
The second view (no execution) is preferred in al-Muhit.
4
Apply the reasoning to the second set of scenarios
When the alternative is eating carrion or drinking wine—both permissible under duress—the killer is executed because he had a lawful alternative. This reinforces that killing is never justified when a non-lethal alternative exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو أكره على أن يقتل رجلاً أو يكفر بالله تعالى وسعه الكفر دون القتل لأن الكفر يرخص في حالة الاضطرار دون القتل فإنه لا يرخص بحال، ولو قتل ولم يكفر المكره دون القتل قياساً لأنه قتل نفساً مختاراً طائعاً ويضمن الدية ماله في في ثلاث سنين إن لم يكن عالماً بأن الكفر يسعه يقتل به، وقيل لا يقتل به لأن الدليل المورث للشبهة قائم وهو حرمة الكفر، ولو أكره على أن يقتل أو يأكل الميتة أو يشرب الخمر فقيل يقتل القاتل دون المكره لأن أكل الميتة وشرب الخمر يرخص حالة الاضطرار.
And in al-Muhit: If he is coerced into killing a man or disbelieving in Allah, disbelief is permissible for him but not killing, because disbelief is permitted in dire necessity, whereas killing is not permitted at all. And if he kills and does not disbelieve, the coerced person is not [liable] for killing by analogy? Actually, he killed a soul willingly and obediently, and he owes blood money from his wealth payable in three years if he was unaware that disbelief was permissible for him; he is killed for it. And it is said he is not killed for it because the evidence that creates doubt exists, which is the prohibition of disbelief. And if he is coerced into killing or eating carrion or drinking wine, then it is said the killer is killed, not the coerced person, because eating carrion and drinking wine are permitted in dire necessity.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit discusses a scenario where a person is coerced into either killing someone or committing apostasy (kufr). The ruling is that apostasy is permissible under duress (due to necessity), but killing is not permissible under any circumstance. If the coerced person kills without committing apostasy, he is considered a willing murderer and must pay diyah (blood money) over three years if he was unaware that apostasy was allowed. There is disagreement on whether he is executed: one view says yes, because he killed intentionally; another says no, because the evidence for apostasy being prohibited creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents qisas. Then, another case: if coerced into killing or eating carrion/drinking wine, the killer is executed (not the coercer) because eating carrion and drinking wine are permitted under duress, so the alternative was lawful, making the killing unjustifiable. The passage highlights the hierarchy of permissible actions under coercion: apostasy and consumption of prohibited substances are allowed in extremis, but taking innocent life is never allowed.

Key Terms
اضطرار (iḍṭirār): Dire necessity that permits normally prohibited acts to save life
شبهة (shubhah): Legal doubt that prevents the application of capital punishment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on killing under coercion according to al-Muhit?
A) It is permissible under extreme duress
B) It is never permitted; the killer is liable for qisas or diyah
C) It is permitted only if the alternative is death
D) It is permitted but makruh

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, killing an innocent person is never justified by coercion, while apostasy and consuming impurities become permissible under dire necessity.

Recourse for Value When Coerced to Manumit a Slave
Explaining the muffered's right to claim the slave's value from the coercer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the muffered's right to recover the slave's value from the coercer and explain why wala’ does not constitute compensation.

Core Idea
Wala’ is not property, so the coercer must compensate for the slave's value.

In this passage, the commentator explains the muffered's right to recover the value of a slave when coerced into manumitting him. The key principle is that destruction (itlaf) is attributed to the coercer, and the muffered is merely an instrument. However, the right is qualified by the muffered's intention, and it hinges on the nature of wala’ as a non-property right. We will explore the reasoning and limits of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The muffered (coerced person) is forced to free his slave; the coercer is the real cause of the destruction of the slave's value.
2
Determine the basis for recourse
The coercer is liable because the destruction is attributed to him; the muffered is his instrument.
This is a qiyas analogy to direct destruction of property.
3
Distinguish liability for manumission vs. destruction
Liability for manumission varies with wealth, but liability for property destruction is strict and does not depend on wealth.
4
Analyze the role of intention
If the muffered intended to comply with the coercion (future manumission), he can recover. If he intended false informing, manumission is effective only judicially (qada’), and he cannot recover.
5
Understand why wala’ is not property
Wala’ is a relational tie like lineage, not a financial asset, so it cannot compensate for the lost value of the slave.
This is supported by the example of witnesses of wala’ who are not liable if they retract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : ( ورجع بقيمته) يعني يرجع المكره على المكره بقيمة العبد لأن الإتلاف قال منسوب إليه والمكره آلة له فيه فيرجع بقيمة العبد عليه موسراً كان أو معسراً لأن ضمان الإتلاف لا يختلف باليسار والإعسار بخلاف ضمان الإعتاق على ما تقدم، ولا سعاية على العبد لأن السعاية إنما تجب عليه للخروج للحرية كما في معتق البعض أو لتعلق حق الغير به كعتق الراهن المرهون وهو معسر أو عتق المريض عبده وعليه دين ولم يخرج من الثلث، ولا المكره على العبد بما ضمن لأنه ضمان وجب عليه بفعله فلا يرجع به على غيره . يرجع وأطلق المؤلف في الرجوع وهو مقيد بما إذا قال أردت بقولي عتقاً مستقبلاً كما طلب مني أو قال لم يخطر ببالي سوى الإتيان بمطلوبه، أما لو قال خطر ببالي الإخبار فأخبرته فيما مضى كاذباً وأردت ذلك لإنشاء الحرية عتق العبد قضاء لا ديانة ولا يضمن المكره المكره شيئاً لأنه عدل عما أكره عليه فكان طائعاً في الإقرار فلا يصدق في دعواه الإخبار كاذباً. فإن قيل: ينبغي أن لا يضمن المكره لأنه أتلف بعوض وهو الولاء والإتلاف بعوض كلا إتلاف . وأجيب بأن الولاء سببه العتق على ملك المولى فكيف المكره معوضاً ولكن لا يكون عوضاً إلا إذا كان العوض مالاً كما إذا أكره على أكل طعام الغير فأكله فلا ضمان على المكره إذ عوضه ما هو في حق حكم المال كما في منافع البضع والولاء ليس بمال لأنه بمنزلة النسب، ألا ترى أن شاهدي الولاء إذا رجعا لا يضمنان. ورد هذا بما إذا أكره المولى على شراء ذي محرم رحم منه فعتق عليه فإن المكره لا يرجع هناك بقيمة العبد على المكره لأنه حصل له عوض صلة ليست بمال كالولاء؛ أما حقيقة كذا في البدائع. ولا يخفى أن الرحم صلة الرحم؛ فظاهر، وأما حكماً فلأنه لم يقل به أحد كما قالوا في منافع البضع عند الدخول.
May Allah have mercy on him: (And he recovers its value) meaning the coerced person (المكره) recovers from the coercer (المكره) the value of the slave, because the destruction is attributed to him and the coerced person is his instrument in it. So he recovers the value of the slave from him, whether the coercer is wealthy or insolvent, because liability for destruction does not differ based on wealth or insolvency, unlike liability for manumission as previously mentioned. And there is no earning (سعاية) upon the slave because earning is only obligatory on him for exiting to freedom, as in the case of a partially freed slave, or due to the attachment of another's right, such as when a mortgaged slave is freed by a debtor or a sick person frees his slave while in debt and it does not come from one-third. And the coerced person does not claim from the slave what he guaranteed, because it is a liability that became obligatory by his own action, so he does not claim it from another. He recovers, and the author (المؤلف) made the recourse unrestricted, but it is restricted to when he says, 'I intended by my word a future manumission as requested from me,' or he says, 'Nothing came to my mind except fulfilling his demand.' However, if he says, 'It came to my mind to inform about the past falsely, and I intended that for creating freedom,' then the slave is freed judicially (قضاء) but not religiously (ديانة), and the coercer does not guarantee anything to the coerced person, because he deviated from what he was coerced into and was voluntary in his acknowledgment, so he is not believed in his claim of false informing. If it is said: It should be that the coercer is not liable because he destroyed with compensation, which is the wala’ (clientage), and destruction with compensation is not destruction. The answer is that wala’ arises from manumission on the property of the master, so how can the coerced person be compensated? But it is only compensation if the compensation is property, as when he is coerced into eating another's food and he eats it, then there is no liability on the coercer because its compensation, in the ruling of property, is like the benefits of the vulva. And wala’ is not property because it is like lineage. Do you not see that if the two witnesses of wala’ retract, they do not guarantee? And this is refuted by the case where the master is coerced into buying a close relative and the relative becomes free upon him: there, the coerced person does not recover the value of the slave from the coercer because he obtained a compensation that is a relational tie not property, like wala’. As for reality, thus in al-Bada’i‘. And it is not hidden that the womb tie is a relational tie; it is obvious. And as for ruling, because no one said it, as they said regarding the benefits of the vulva at the time of consummation.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the muffered's right to recover the value of a slave from the coercer when he was coerced into manumitting. The author of the matn states: 'And he recovers its value.' The commentator says that the muffered (المكره) recovers the value of the slave from the coercer (المكره) because the destruction of property (the slave's value) is attributed to the coercer, and the muffered is merely his instrument. This right holds whether the coercer is wealthy or insolvent, unlike the liability for manumission which varies. The slave himself does not have to earn his freedom (سعاية) because that obligation arises only in specific cases like partial manumission or when debts exist. The muffered cannot claim from the slave what he paid the coercer. However, the author's unrestricted statement about recourse is restricted by the muffered's intention: if he intended future manumission as demanded, he can claim. But if he intended to inform about the past falsely (i.e., pretending to recite a past manumission), then the manumission is effective only judicially (قضاء) but not religiously (ديانة), and he cannot claim from the coercer because he deviated from the coercion. Then an objection is raised: why is the coercer liable? He destroyed the slave but gave compensation in the form of wala’ (clientage). The answer: wala’ is not property, so it is not adequate compensation. The case of eating another's food under coercion is different because the food's replacement is property. Wala’ is akin to lineage, not property. This is supported by the fact that witnesses of wala’ are not liable if they retract. The passage then addresses a refutation: when the master is coerced into buying a relative, the relative becomes free and the master cannot claim value from the coercer because he obtained a relational tie (صلة رحم) as compensation, which is not property.

Key Terms
ولاء (walā’): Clientage; the relationship between a freed slave and his former master, giving the master inheritance rights
سعاية (si‘āyah): Earning; the obligation of a slave to work to pay for his freedom
قضاء (qaḍā’): Judicial ruling; a manumission that is valid in court but not in the divine view
ديانة (diyānah): Religiously; an act that is valid in the sight of Allah but may not be enforced in court

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can the muffered recover the value of the slave from the coercer even though the muffered gained wala’?
A) Wala’ is not property.
B) The muffered did not actually gain wala’.
C) Wala’ is a punishment, not compensation.
D) The coercer must pay twice.

Key Takeaway
The muffered recovers the slave's value from the coercer because wala’ is not property, but only if he intended to comply with the coercion.

Coerced Manumission for Less Than Value and Deferred Payment
Rulings on coerced manumission for a price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effects of coerced manumission when the price is less than the slave's value and when the price is deferred, and identify the master's options for recourse.

Core Idea
Coerced manumission is valid; the master may recover the full value from the coercer, and the slave owes the agreed price via si‘āyah.

This passage from al-Muḥīṭ addresses scenarios where a person is coerced into manumitting his slave for a price that does not match the slave's market value. The Hanafi school holds the manumission valid but gives the master options to seek compensation from the coercer and the slave. Understanding these rulings is crucial for balancing rights in cases of duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The first scenario is manumission for a price less than the slave's value (e.g., 100 instead of 1000). The second is manumission for a deferred price higher than the value (2000 deferred for a year, value 1000).
Both scenarios involve coercion of the owner, not the slave.
2
Validate the manumission
In both cases, the manumission takes effect with full legal force. The slave is freed and the master's ownership is extinguished.
Coercion does not invalidate the act; it only gives rights of recourse.
3
Apply the first option for the master
The master may claim the full market value from the coercer. Then the coercer stands in the master's place and may claim the agreed price from the slave as si‘āyah (earning).
This option restores the master to his original position.
4
Apply the second option for the master
The master may claim only the difference (e.g., 900) from the coercer and take the agreed price (e.g., 100) from the slave directly.
This option is for when the master wants to retain the agreed price.
5
Analyze the deferred payment scenario
If the master was coerced to free for 2000 deferred, he may claim the value (1000) from the coercer and give the surplus (the difference between 2000 and 1000? Actually the text says 'يتصدق بالفصل' – give the excess in charity) or enforce the deferred payment from the slave.
The text is ambiguous; the preferred option is to claim the value and give the excess in charity to avoid riba? Or it may mean he can wait for the payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : ولو أكره على أن يعتق على أقل من قيمته على مائة وقيمته ألف والعبد غير مكره يقع بتمام قيمته، ثم إن شاء ضمن المكره قيمته ثم يرجع هو على العبد بمائة السعاية لأنه بأداء الضمان قام مقام المولى، وإن شاء المولى ضمن المكره تسعمائة ثم يرجع بتسعمائة وأخذ من العبد مائة لأن السيد طائع في التزام المال والمكره يتلف عليه تسعمائة بغير عوض فيأخذ منه . ولو أكره على أن يعتق عبده على ألفين إلى سنة وقيمته ألف ففعل، فإن شاء ضمن المكره قيمته ألف المكره على العبد بألفين إلى سنة ويتصدق بالفصل، وإن شاء اختار وهي ويرجع وهو للحال العتق وكان له ألفان إلى سنة . ولو أكره العبد على قبول العتق على مال لم يلزمه شيء ويضمن للمكره لما بينا عبد بين رجلين أكره أحدهما على عقته فأعتقه جاز والولاء كله للمعتق عندهما، فإن كان المكره موسراً ضمن قیمته بينهما، وإن كان معسراً ضمن نصف قيمته للمكره ويسعى العبد للآخر في نصف قيمته لأن المكره في حق المكره متلف وفي حق الساكت بمنزلة العتق . وعند الإمام يعتق نصيب المكره لا غير ولا ضمان على المكره للساكت
And in al-Muhit: If he is coerced into manumitting for less than its value, e.g., for one hundred while its value is one thousand, and the slave is not coerced, the manumission takes effect with its full value. Then, if he wishes, he may claim the value from the coercer and then claim one hundred from the slave as earning (saya), because by paying the guarantee he stands in the place of the master. And if the master wishes, he may claim nine hundred from the coercer and then claim nine hundred and take one hundred from the slave. If he is coerced into manumitting his slave for two thousand deferred for a year, while its value is one thousand, and he does it, then if he wishes, he may claim from the coercer its value of one thousand... and give charity of the surplus? Actually, unclear. And if he wishes, he chooses... and he claims... and the manumission is immediate and he has two thousand deferred for a year. And if the slave is coerced into accepting manumission for a price, nothing is binding on him, and he (the slave?) guarantees [؟]. And a slave between two men: one of them is coerced into manumitting him, so he manumits him; it is permissible, and all the wala’ belongs to the manumitter according to them (the two). If the coercer is wealthy, he guarantees his value between them; if he is insolvent, he guarantees half of his value to the coercer (the one coerced), and the slave earns for the other half of his value because the coerced man is, in respect of the coercer, a destroyer, and in respect of the silent partner, like a manumitter. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa), only the share of the coerced partner is freed, and there is no guarantee from the coercer to the silent partner.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit presents three special cases of coerced manumission. First, if a man is coerced into manumitting his slave for less than the slave's value (e.g., for 100 while the slave is worth 1000), the manumission takes effect with full value. The master (المكره) then has two options: (1) claim the full value (1000) from the coercer, and then the coercer can claim the agreed 100 from the slave as earning (سعاية); (2) claim 900 from the coercer (the difference) and take the agreed 100 from the slave. The second case: if coerced into manumitting for a deferred price higher than the value, the master can choose to claim the value from the coercer and give the surplus in charity, or enforce the deferred price from the slave? The text is unclear but indicates options. Third, if the slave himself is coerced into accepting manumission for a price, the slave is not liable for the price. Fourth, a joint ownership case: if one of two co-owners is coerced into manumitting the jointly owned slave, the manumission is valid and all wala’ goes to the manumitter according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). If the coercer is wealthy, he must pay the full value (split between both owners); if insolvent, he pays half the value to the coerced co-owner, and the slave must earn (سعاية) the other half for the silent co-owner. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, only the coerced partner's share is freed, and the coercer is not liable to the silent partner. This shows the flexibility in Hanafi fiqh to protect the coerced party while balancing the rights of the slave and other owners.

Key Terms
سعاية (si‘āyah): Earning by the slave to pay for his freedom, used here when the coercer is insolvent
ولاء (walā’): Clientage right of the manumitter over the freed slave

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a master is coerced to free his slave for less than the value, which of the following is an option for the master according to the passage?
A) Void the manumission
B) Claim the full value from the coercer and then the coercer claims the agreed price from the slave
C) Claim the full value from the slave directly
D) Give the slave to the coercer

Key Takeaway
Coerced manumission for a price is valid, but the master has financial options to recover the full value or the deficiency, while the slave is liable only for the agreed price via si‘āyah.

Coerced Manumission in Joint Ownership and When the Slave is Coerced
Rulings on coerced manumission involving slaves and joint ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on coerced manumission when the slave is coerced to accept freedom for a price, and when one co-owner is coerced to free a jointly owned slave, including the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A slave coerced to accept manumission for a price is not liable; in joint ownership, the coerced co-owner's manumission frees the entire slave according to the Sahibayn, but only his share according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

The second part of the passage from al-Muḥīṭ addresses two additional scenarios: when the slave himself is coerced into accepting a conditional manumission, and when one of two co-owners is coerced to manumit their jointly-owned slave. These cases introduce important nuances about liability and the distribution of wala’ and compensation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the slave-coerced scenario
If the slave is coerced into accepting manumission for a price, the slave is not liable for that price. The text implies the slave does not owe the money, and the coercer may be liable.
This protects the slave from being forced into debt.
2
Identify the joint ownership scenario
A slave is owned jointly by two persons. One co-owner is coerced into manumitting the slave, and he does so. The manumission is valid.
The coerced co-owner is the one who performs the act.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's view
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the entire slave is freed. All wala’ (clientage) belongs to the manumitter (the coerced co-owner). The coercer must pay the full value to both owners; if he is insolvent, he pays half to the coerced co-owner, and the slave earns the other half for the silent co-owner.
This view treats the manumission as effective for the whole slave.
4
Apply Imam Abu Hanifa's view
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, only the coerced co-owner's share is freed. The silent partner's share remains in slavery, and the coercer owes nothing to the silent partner.
This view does not free the entire slave.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the status of the slave: fully free versus partially enslaved. Also the distribution of compensation differs.
The mufta bihi is likely the Sahibayn's view as it is presented first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : ولو أكره على أن يعتق على أقل من قيمته على مائة وقيمته ألف والعبد غير مكره يقع بتمام قيمته، ثم إن شاء ضمن المكره قيمته ثم يرجع هو على العبد بمائة السعاية لأنه بأداء الضمان قام مقام المولى، وإن شاء المولى ضمن المكره تسعمائة ثم يرجع بتسعمائة وأخذ من العبد مائة لأن السيد طائع في التزام المال والمكره يتلف عليه تسعمائة بغير عوض فيأخذ منه . ولو أكره على أن يعتق عبده على ألفين إلى سنة وقيمته ألف ففعل، فإن شاء ضمن المكره قيمته ألف المكره على العبد بألفين إلى سنة ويتصدق بالفصل، وإن شاء اختار وهي ويرجع وهو للحال العتق وكان له ألفان إلى سنة . ولو أكره العبد على قبول العتق على مال لم يلزمه شيء ويضمن للمكره لما بينا عبد بين رجلين أكره أحدهما على عقته فأعتقه جاز والولاء كله للمعتق عندهما، فإن كان المكره موسراً ضمن قیمته بينهما، وإن كان معسراً ضمن نصف قيمته للمكره ويسعى العبد للآخر في نصف قيمته لأن المكره في حق المكره متلف وفي حق الساكت بمنزلة العتق . وعند الإمام يعتق نصيب المكره لا غير ولا ضمان على المكره للساكت
And in al-Muhit: If he is coerced into manumitting for less than its value, e.g., for one hundred while its value is one thousand, and the slave is not coerced, the manumission takes effect with its full value. Then, if he wishes, he may claim the value from the coercer and then claim one hundred from the slave as earning (saya), because by paying the guarantee he stands in the place of the master. And if the master wishes, he may claim nine hundred from the coercer and then claim nine hundred and take one hundred from the slave. If he is coerced into manumitting his slave for two thousand deferred for a year, while its value is one thousand, and he does it, then if he wishes, he may claim from the coercer its value of one thousand... and give charity of the surplus? Actually, unclear. And if he wishes, he chooses... and he claims... and the manumission is immediate and he has two thousand deferred for a year. And if the slave is coerced into accepting manumission for a price, nothing is binding on him, and he (the slave?) guarantees [؟]. And a slave between two men: one of them is coerced into manumitting him, so he manumits him; it is permissible, and all the wala’ belongs to the manumitter according to them (the two). If the coercer is wealthy, he guarantees his value between them; if he is insolvent, he guarantees half of his value to the coercer (the one coerced), and the slave earns for the other half of his value because the coerced man is, in respect of the coercer, a destroyer, and in respect of the silent partner, like a manumitter. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa), only the share of the coerced partner is freed, and there is no guarantee from the coercer to the silent partner.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit presents three special cases of coerced manumission. First, if a man is coerced into manumitting his slave for less than the slave's value (e.g., for 100 while the slave is worth 1000), the manumission takes effect with full value. The master (المكره) then has two options: (1) claim the full value (1000) from the coercer, and then the coercer can claim the agreed 100 from the slave as earning (سعاية); (2) claim 900 from the coercer (the difference) and take the agreed 100 from the slave. The second case: if coerced into manumitting for a deferred price higher than the value, the master can choose to claim the value from the coercer and give the surplus in charity, or enforce the deferred price from the slave? The text is unclear but indicates options. Third, if the slave himself is coerced into accepting manumission for a price, the slave is not liable for the price. Fourth, a joint ownership case: if one of two co-owners is coerced into manumitting the jointly owned slave, the manumission is valid and all wala’ goes to the manumitter according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). If the coercer is wealthy, he must pay the full value (split between both owners); if insolvent, he pays half the value to the coerced co-owner, and the slave must earn (سعاية) the other half for the silent co-owner. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, only the coerced partner's share is freed, and the coercer is not liable to the silent partner. This shows the flexibility in Hanafi fiqh to protect the coerced party while balancing the rights of the slave and other owners.

Key Terms
سعاية (si‘āyah): Earning by the slave to pay for his freedom, used here when the coercer is insolvent
ولاء (walā’): Clientage right of the manumitter over the freed slave

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the joint ownership case, what is the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The entire slave is freed
B) Only the coerced owner's share is freed
C) The manumission is invalid
D) The slave must pay for his freedom

Key Takeaway
In coerced manumission, a coerced slave incurs no liability; in joint ownership, the Sahibayn free the entire slave while Imam Abu Hanifa frees only the coerced owner's share.

The Coerced's Recourse for the Value of the Slave
States the ruling on recourse after coerced emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse after coercion in freeing a slave and explain the attribution of liability.

Core Idea
The coercer is the real cause of destruction; the coerced has full recourse for the value.

In Hanafi fiqh, coercion (ikrah) can nullify the legal effects of an act in some respects. This passage addresses a specific scenario where a person is forced to purchase and free a slave. The ruling clarifies who bears the financial loss: the coercer is ultimately liable, and the coerced can recover the slave's value from him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Distinguish between the coerced (al-mukrahu) who performed the act under duress, and the coercer (al-mukrihu) who compelled the act.
The coercer's threat must meet the Hanafi standard of ikrah (death or severe injury).
2
Determine the ruling
The coerced has recourse against the coercer for the market value of the slave (qīma). This is a wajib obligation on the coercer to pay.
The phrase 'yarjiʿu ʿalā al-mukrihi' means he has claim against him.
3
Understand the rationale
The destruction (itlāf) of the slave's value is attributed to the coercer, as the coerced is merely his tool. Thus, the coercer bears liability.
This is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) based on causation.
4
Apply the condition of solvency
The recourse is valid whether the coercer is wealthy (mūsir) or poor (muʿsir), unlike the guarantee of emancipation which depends on ability to pay.
The guarantee for destruction does not vary with financial status.
5
Note the exclusions
The freed slave does not owe saʿāya (work to earn freedom), and the coerced cannot claim from the slave because the guarantee arose from his own action.
Saʿāya is only required in specific cases like partial freeing or third-party rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراء أمة ولدت منه بالنكاح فاشترى فعتقت عليه بشيء لأنه عتق من غير اعتاق. رحمه الله : ( ورجع بقيمته) يعني يرجع المكره على المكره بقيمة العبد لأن الإتلاف قال منسوب إليه والمكره آلة له فيه فيرجع بقيمة العبد عليه موسراً كان أو معسراً لأن ضمان الإتلاف لا يختلف باليسار والإعسار بخلاف ضمان الإعتاق على ما تقدم، ولا سعاية على العبد لأن السعاية إنما تجب عليه للخروج للحرية كما في معتق البعض أو لتعلق حق الغير به كعتق الراهن المرهون وهو معسر أو عتق المريض عبده وعليه دين ولم يخرج من الثلث، ولا المكره على العبد بما ضمن لأنه ضمان وجب عليه بفعله فلا يرجع به على غيره.
A man bought a slave girl who had given birth to him by marriage, so he bought her and she was freed upon him with something because it was emancipation without his own act. May Allah have mercy on him: (And he returns its value), meaning the coerced person returns to the coercer the value of the slave because the destruction is attributed to him and the coerced is his tool; so he returns the value of the slave upon him whether he is wealthy or poor because the guarantee of destruction does not differ with wealth or poverty, unlike the guarantee of emancipation as previously mentioned. And there is no sacyah upon the slave because sacyah is only obligatory upon him for achieving freedom, as in the case of a partially freed slave, or due to the right of another attached to him, like a pawned slave freed by the pawner while he is insolvent, or a sick person's slave freed while he has debt and it does not come out of the third. And the coerced does not return to the slave what he guaranteed because it is a guarantee that became obligatory by his own action, so he does not revert to anyone.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on the consequences of being coerced into freeing a slave. The author explains that when a person is forced to buy and then free a slave, the coerced person must pay the market value to the coercer. The reasoning is that the coercer is the real destroyer of the property, as the coerced is merely his tool. This liability applies whether the coerced is wealthy or poor, because the guarantee for destruction is not affected by one's financial status, unlike the guarantee for emancipation where liability may depend on ability to pay. The passage also clarifies that the freed slave is not required to perform sacyah (earning freedom by work) in this case, because sacyah is only required to achieve freedom for the slave or to protect the rights of third parties, neither of which applies here. Furthermore, the coerced person cannot claim compensation from the slave for what he paid, as the guarantee is a result of his own action.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Compulsion by threat that overcomes the will, legally nullifying the coerced act in some respects.
المكره (al-mukrahu): The person who is coerced to perform an act.
المكرِه (al-mukrihu): The person who exerts coercion.
قيمة (qīma): Market value of the slave.
سعاية (sa‘āya): The slave's work to earn his freedom, required in specific circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does the coerced have recourse against the coercer for the slave's value?
A) Only if the coercer is wealthy
B) Only if the coerced is poor
C) Always, regardless of wealth or poverty
D) Only if the slave performs saʿāya

Key Takeaway
In coercion, the real cause of the act bears the financial loss; the coerced has full recourse for the value of the property.

Return Restricted by the Coerced's Intention
Ruling on coerced emancipation and liability based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule regarding when a coerced person is liable to return the value of a freed slave, based on his intention.

Core Idea
The coerced's intention determines liability: future intent triggers return, past-reporting intent does not.

This passage examines how a coerced person's inner intention affects the legal consequences of his declaration of emancipation. The author clarifies that the ruling on returning the value is conditional upon whether the coerced intended to comply with the coercion or to deviate from it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi fiqh in cases of duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The coerced person's declaration of emancipation is the central act. The ruling specifies when he must return the value to the coercer.
2
Isolate the condition of intention
The author's statement about return being absolute is restricted by the coerced's intention. The return applies only when the coerced intended future emancipation as demanded or had no other thought.
3
Analyze the two scenarios
If the coerced intended future emancipation (complying), return is required. If he intended to lie about a past emancipation to create freedom (deviating), the slave is freed only in court, not religiously, and no return is due.
The second scenario involves voluntary deviation from coercion.
4
Understand the effect in court vs. divine judgment
The slave is freed in court (qaḍāʾ) but not in divine judgment (diyāna). The court enforces freedom, but the coerced is not religiously obligated.
The coerced is not liable because he acted voluntarily.
5
Conclusion on liability
The coerced is not believed in his claim of lying in court because he voluntarily deviated. He bears no liability when he intended to lie about the past.
Thus, intention is the decisive factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق المؤلف في الرجوع وهو مقيد بما إذا قال أردت بقولي عتقاً مستقبلاً كما طلب مني أو قال لم يخطر ببالي سوى الإتيان بمطلوبه، أما لو قال خطر ببالي الإخبار فأخبرته فيما مضى كاذباً وأردت ذلك لإنشاء الحرية عتق العبد قضاء لا ديانة ولا يضمن المكره المكره شيئاً لأنه عدل عما أكره عليه فكان طائعاً في الإقرار فلا يصدق في دعواه الإخبار كاذباً.
The author made the return absolute, but it is restricted to when he said: 'I intended by my statement a future emancipation as demanded from me' or he said: 'Nothing came to my mind except fulfilling his demand.' However, if he said: 'It occurred to me to report, so I informed him in the past as a lie, and I intended that to create freedom', then the slave is freed judicially but not religiously, and the coerced does not guarantee the coercer anything because he deviated from what he was coerced to do, so he was voluntary in his iqrar, and he is not believed in his claim of lying.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage clarifies the condition under which the coerced person must return the value to the coercer. It only applies if the coerced intended to perform the act as demanded (future emancipation). If the coerced claims he intended to report a past event (a lie) and thereby create freedom, the slave is freed in court but not in divine judgment, and the coerced is not liable to return anything because he voluntarily deviated from the coercion. Thus, his claim of lying is not accepted in court.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession; here the coerced person's statement about emancipation.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Judicial ruling; the formal court decision.
ديانة (diyāna): Divine judgment; the ruling according to God's law as opposed to human court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the coerced person not liable to return the value of the freed slave?
A) When he intended future emancipation as coerced
B) When he intended to inform about a past event (lying) to create freedom
C) When the slave is a mahram
D) When the coercer is wealthy

Key Takeaway
The coerced's intention determines liability: only if he intended to comply with the coercion (future emancipation) is he liable to return the value; if he intended to deviate (lie about the past), he is not liable.

Is Wala' a Substitute? The Hanafi Response on Coerced Emancipation
Objection and Hanafi response regarding wala' as substitute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that wala' is not a monetary substitute and apply this principle to determine liability in cases of coerced emancipation.

Core Idea
Wala' is not property (mal) — it is akin to lineage, so it cannot remove liability for destruction.

This passage addresses an objection that the coerced should not be liable because wala' is a substitute. The Hanafi response clarifies that only monetary substitutes (mal) nullify liability. Understanding the distinction between wala' and property is crucial for determining when coercion absolves or imposes liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that since the coerced gives wala' in return for freeing the slave, the destruction is compensated, so no liability.
The objector uses an analogy to coerced food consumption where liability is removed because the food has a monetary value.
2
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that wala' is not a monetary substitute; it is akin to lineage, not property.
Only 'mal' (property) can serve as a valid substitute to absolve liability.
3
Analyze the analogy (qiyas)
The objector's analogy to coerced food consumption fails because the 'illa' (effective cause) is not present: in the food case, the substitute is mal; here it is not.
The Hanafi school performs 'tanqih al-manat' to show the difference.
4
Examine the supporting evidence
The passage provides two pieces of evidence: witnesses of wala' who retract do not guarantee (showing wala' is not property), and the case of coerced purchase of a mahram relative (where the tie of kinship also does not count as mal).
Both examples reinforce that non-monetary substitutes do not remove liability.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the coerced must return the value of the slave because wala' does not count as a substitute.
The ruling is that the coerced is liable (yadman) for the value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ينبغي أن لا يضمن المكره لأنه أتلف بعوض وهو الولاء والإتلاف بعوض كلا إتلاف . وأجيب بأن الولاء سببه العتق على ملك المولى فكيف المكره معوضاً ولكن لا يكون عوضاً إلا إذا كان العوض مالاً كما إذا أكره على أكل طعام الغير فأكله فلا ضمان على المكره إذ عوضه ما هو في حق حكم المال كما في منافع البضع والولاء ليس بمال لأنه بمنزلة النسب، ألا ترى أن شاهدي الولاء إذا رجعا لا يضمنان. ورد هذا بما إذا أكره المولى على شراء ذي محرم رحم منه فعتق عليه فإن المكره لا يرجع هناك بقيمة العبد على المكره لأنه حصل له عوض صلة ليست بمال كالولاء؛ أما حقيقة كذا في البدائع. ولا يخفى أن الرحم صلة الرحم؛ فظاهر، وأما حكماً فلأنه لم يقل به أحد كما قالوا في منافع البضع عند الدخول.
For if it is said: He should not guarantee because he destroyed with a substitute, which is wala', and destruction with a substitute is not destruction. It was answered: Wala' is caused by emancipation while still in the master's ownership, so how can the coerced be compensated? But it is only a substitute if the substitute is wealth, as when he is coerced to eat another's food and eats it, then there is no liability on the coerced because his substitute is what is considered as property, like the usufruct of the vulva. And wala' is not property because it is akin to lineage. Do you not see that the witnesses of wala' when they retract do not guarantee? This was refuted by the case where the master is coerced to buy a mahram relative and she is freed upon him, then the coerced does not return there the value of the slave to the coercer because he obtained a substitute of a tie of kinship which is not property, like wala'. As for the reality, thus in al-Bada'i. And it is not hidden that the tie (rahim) is a tie of kinship; that is apparent, and as for ruling, because no one said it, as they said in the usufruct of the vulva upon entry.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection against the ruling that the coerced must return the value. The objector argues that the coerced should not be liable because he destroyed something (the slave) in exchange for a substitute (wala'), and destruction with an exchange is not considered destruction. The Hanafi response explains that wala' is not a monetary substitute; it is like lineage, not property. For an exchange to remove liability, it must be a monetary value (mal), similar to the case of eating another's food under coercion (where the substitute is the food's value) or the usufruct of the vulva in marriage. Wala' is not mal, as seen when witnesses of wala' retract—they do not pay damages. This is supported by a further example: if a master is coerced to buy his mahram relative (who then becomes free), the coerced does not return the value because he gets a substitute of kinship, which is not property. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Bada'i.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā’): Clientage, the bond of loyalty between the freed slave and the former master.
عوض (‘iwaḍ): Compensation or substitute given in exchange for something.
مال (māl): Property that can be valued and traded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the objection that wala' absolves the coerced of liability?
A) Because wala' is not a valid substitute in any case
B) Because wala' is not considered property (mal)
C) Because the slave is not destroyed
D) Because the coercer is the one who gains wala'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only monetary substitutes (mal) can nullify liability for destruction; wala' is not mal, so the coerced must pay the value of the slave.

Coercion in Manumission: The Murderous Slave and the Dispute with al-Karakhi
Rulings on coercion in manumission with an intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coercion in manumission of a murderous slave and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding manumission on behalf of another under coercion.

Core Idea
Coercion invalidates property destruction but not obligations; manumission for another is not a sale.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines two scenarios of coercion in manumission. The first involves a slave who committed murder, where the coercer is liable for the slave's value. The second involves manumission on behalf of another for a price, triggering a dispute with al-Karakhi over whether the transaction is akin to a coerced sale. The dominant Hanafi view treats the manumission as direct, preserving the original owner's wala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: (1) a slave who killed someone and the owner is coerced to free him, and (2) an owner is coerced to free his slave on behalf of another for a price equal to the slave's value.
Note the different rulings on liability in each case.
2
Analyze the first case
When coercion involves a threat to life (e.g., death threat), the coercer guarantees the slave's value. The owner takes the value and pays the victim's heir. If coercion is by imprisonment or shackles, the owner guarantees the crime without blood-money, and the coercer is not liable.
Distinction: coercion in destruction of property vs. coercion in undertaking an obligation.
3
Analyze the second case
If a man is coerced to free his slave on behalf of another for a price, and the acceptor agrees willingly, the owner may hold either the coercer or the acceptor liable. If he holds the coercer, the coercer may recover from the acceptor. Wala belongs to the original owner (the manumitter).
The wala remains with the one who performed the manumission.
4
Examine al-Karakhi's objection and the Hanafi response
Al-Karakhi argued that the transaction is in the meaning of a sale, and a coerced sale before delivery does not transfer ownership, so the manumission should be on behalf of the acceptor. The response: coercion applied directly to the manumission, not to the implied sale. Indirect effects do not carry coercion.
This is a methodological dispute about how to characterize the transaction—direct vs. implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قتل عبد رجلاً وأكره على عتقه وهو يعلم بالجناية ضمن المكره قيمته ويأخذها المولى فيدفعها إلى ولي الجناية لأنه مضطر في هذا الإعتاق ولو كان الإكراه يحبس أو قيد يضمن المولى الجناية دون الدية ولا يضمن المكره شيئاً لأن هذا الإكراه لا يعد إكراها في حق إتلاف المال ويعتبر إكراها في حق التزام المال. ولو أكره على أن يعتق عبده عن رجل بألف درهم وقيمته ألف فأعتق وقبل المعتق عنه طائعاً، فإن شاء ضمن المكره، وإن شاء ضمن المعتق عنه، فلو ضمن الأول يرجع على المعتق عنه والولاء للمعتق . وقال الكرخي : ينبغي أن يقع العتق عن المعتق عنه لأنه بمعنى البيع وبيع المكره قبل التسليم لا يفيد الملك. وأجيب بأن الإكراه ورد على العتق لا على البيع الذي في ضمن طلب الإعتاق ولو ورد على البيع إنما يرد ضمناً وتبعاً والإكراه لا يؤثر فما ثبت ضمناً وتبعاً ويعتقد في الضمني بما لا يعتقد في القصدي.
If a slave kills a man and the owner is coerced to free him while knowing of the crime, the coercer guarantees his value, and the owner takes it and gives it to the heir of the slain, because he is compelled in this manumission. If the coercion was by imprisonment or shackles, the owner guarantees the crime without blood-money, and the coercer guarantees nothing, because this coercion is not considered coercion regarding destruction of property, but is considered coercion regarding commitment of property. If he is coerced to free his slave on behalf of a man for a thousand dirhams, its value being a thousand, so he frees and the one on whose behalf it was freed accepts willingly, then if he wishes he may hold the coercer liable, and if he wishes he may hold the one on whose behalf it was freed liable. If he holds the first liable, he has recourse against the one on whose behalf it was freed, and the wala belongs to the one who freed. Al-Karakhi said: It is appropriate that the manumission occur on behalf of the one on whose behalf it was freed, because it is in the meaning of sale, and the sale of a coerced person before delivery does not confer ownership. It was answered that coercion applied to the manumission, not to the sale which is implied in the request for manumission, and if it applied to the sale, it would only apply implicitly and dependently, and coercion does not affect what is established implicitly and dependently, and it is considered in the implicit what is not considered in the intended.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains rulings on coercion in manumission scenarios. The first case: a slave commits murder, and the owner is coerced to free him. The coercer must pay the slave's value to the owner, who then pays it to the victim's heir. The reasoning is that the owner is compelled (mudtar) in this manumission. If the coercion is by imprisonment or shackling, the owner is liable for the slave's crime without paying blood-money, and the coercer is not liable because this type of coercion is not considered effective for property destruction but is considered for property obligation. The second case: a man is coerced to free his slave on behalf of another for a price of 1000 dirhams (the slave's value). The victim (the person accepting) is willing. The owner has a choice: he can claim the coercer or the acceptor. If he claims the coercer, the coercer can recover from the acceptor. The wala (clientage) goes to the person who freed (the original owner). Al-Karakhi (a Hanafi scholar) objects: the manumission should be on behalf of the acceptor because it is like a sale, and a coerced sale before delivery does not transfer ownership. The response is that coercion was directed at manumission, not at the sale implied in the request. If it affected the sale, it would be only indirect and dependent, and coercion does not affect what is established indirectly and dependently, unlike what is intended directly. The Hanafi position holds.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion that overcomes a person's will, making them act against their consent. Here distinguished between coercion in destroying property (effective) and in committing to obligation (not effective).
المضطر (al-mudtar): One who is compelled or in dire necessity, here referring to the coerced owner who is forced to free his slave due to coercion.
الولاء (al-wala): Clientage; the legal bond between the manumitter and the freed slave, giving the manumitter inheritance rights in certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a slave who committed murder, and the owner is coerced to free him by threat of death, who is liable for the slave's value?
A) The owner of the slave
B) The coercer
C) The victim's heir
D) The slave himself after freedom

Key Takeaway
Coercion in manumission is effective only for property destruction; for obligations, it does not shift liability, and a coerced manumission on behalf of another remains attributed to the original owner.
