Liability in Coerced Manumission: Imprisonment vs. Threat to Life
Rulings on liability in coerced manumission and tadbir based on type of coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between coercion by imprisonment and threat to life in manumission, and determine who bears liability for the slave's value.

Core Idea
Coercion by imprisonment does not transfer liability to the coercer; the acceptor bears the value.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh: how different types of coercion affect liability when a person is forced to free a slave or make a tadbir. The key distinction is between coercion by imprisonment (which is not considered effective for property destruction) and coercion by threat to life (which is). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly assigning liability to the coercer, the acceptor, or the owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Identify the coerced act (manumission or tadbir), the coercer (mukrih), the acceptor (qabil, the one on whose behalf the act is done), and the owner (mawla).
The acceptor is often the beneficiary who may pay a sum.
2
Classify the coercion type
Determine whether the coercion on the manumitter is by imprisonment or by threat to life. This classification determines liability rules.
Imprisonment is 'habs', threat to life is 'qatl'.
3
Apply the principle of effective coercion
Coercion by imprisonment is not considered effective for destroying property (إتلاف المال), so the coercer is not liable; the acceptor bears the value as a common slave. Coercion by death threat is effective, so the coercer is liable.
The underlying talil: imprisonment does not force the owner to destroy property; he still has a choice.
4
Work through the scenarios
Consider mixed cases: (a) manumitter coerced by death, acceptor by imprisonment, (b) opposite, (c) tadbir cases, (d) owner waives liability. For each, determine who can be sued and who has recourse.
The text covers multiple subcases; we must trace each.
5
Determine final liability and recourse
Conclude who ultimately bears the loss and who can recover from whom. Note that in some cases the owner may choose between suing the coercer or the acceptor.
In tadbir, the 'decrease due to tadbir' (nuqsan al-tadbir) is considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أكره بحبس تجب القيمة على المعتق عنه دون المكره، ولو أكره المعتق بالقتل والمعتق عنه بالحبس فالمعتق عنه غير مكره ولو كان الإكراه على عكس هذا ضمن المكره قيمته للمولى ولم يضمن المعتق عنه شيئاً والولي للمعتق عنه لأن الإكراه الفاعل هو المكره، والإعتاق وإن وجد في ملك المعتق فقد أتلف المكره بالإعتاق عليه حق الاسترداد بغير رضاه ولو أكره على أن يدبر عبده عنه بألف فدبر فالمولى بالخيار إن شاء ضمن المكره قيمته قناً ورجع المكره على قابل التدبير بقيمته مدبراً، وإن شاء ضمن القابل قيمته مدبراً ورجع على المكره بنقصان التدبير ولا يرجع المكره به على القابل. ولو أكره على الإعتاق بحبس أو قيد لم يضمن المكره شيئاً ويضمن القابل قيمته قناً لأن هذا الإكراه غير معتبر في حق إتلاف المال ولو أكره المولى بالقتل والقابل بالحبس ضمن القابل قيمته قناً ولا يرجع على المكره بشيء، فإن ضمن المكره رجع به على القابل. ولو وهب المولى من الكره قيمته أو أبرأه منها كان للمكره أن يرجع على القابل بقيمته، ولو أكره المولى بحبس والقابل بوعيد تلف فللمولى أن يضمن المكره ما نقص بالتدبير ويضمن القابل قيمته مدبراً لما عرف.
If he is coerced by imprisonment, the value is owed by the one on whose behalf the freeing was made, not the coercer. If the one freeing was coerced by threat of death and the one on whose behalf by imprisonment, then the latter is not coerced. If the coercion was opposite, the coercer guarantees his value to the owner and the one on whose behalf guarantees nothing, and wala is for the latter because the effective coercer is the coercer. Even though the manumission occurred in the ownership of the latter, the coercer destroyed his right to reclaim without consent. If he is coerced to make a tadbir on his slave on behalf of another for a thousand, and he does so, the owner may choose: if he wishes he can hold the coercer liable for the slave's value as a common slave, and the coercer can recover from the acceptor the value as a mudabbar; if he wishes he can hold the acceptor liable for the value as a mudabbar, and the acceptor can recover from the coercer the decrease due to tadbir, and the coercer cannot recover from the acceptor. If he is coerced to free by imprisonment or shackles, the coercer guarantees nothing, and the acceptor guarantees the value as a common slave because this coercion is not considered regarding destruction of property. If the owner was coerced by death and the acceptor by imprisonment, the acceptor guarantees the value as a common slave and does not have recourse to the coercer for anything; if the coercer pays, he can recover from the acceptor. If the owner waives the value or discharges the coercer, the coercer can still recover from the acceptor. If the owner is coerced by imprisonment and the acceptor by threat of loss, the owner can hold the coercer for the decrease due to tadbir and hold the acceptor for the value as a mudabbar, as known.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage details how different types of coercion affect liability in manumission and tadbir (making a slave mudabbar). The key distinction: coercion by imprisonment is not considered effective for destroying property (إتلاف المال), while coercion by threat to life is. Therefore, if the manumitter is coerced by imprisonment, the coercer is not liable for the value; the acceptor (the one on whose behalf the freeing was made) bears the value as a common slave. If the manumitter is coerced by threat of death, the coercer is liable. Variations include cases where the manumitter and acceptor face different types of coercion, and the options available to the owner (mawla). The owner can choose to hold either the coercer or the acceptor liable in some cases. The passage also covers tadbir, where the slave is freed upon the owner's death. The intricate rulings aim to balance the effects of coercion with property rights.

Key Terms
التدبير (al-tadbir): A declaration by the owner that his slave will be free after the owner's death (mudabbar).
القابل (al-qabil): The person on whose behalf the manumission or tadbir is made, i.e., the acceptor.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion; forcing someone to act against their will under threat.
إتلاف المال (itlaf al-mal): Destruction of property; causing loss of property value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man is coerced by imprisonment to free his slave, and the acceptor is willing, who is liable for the slave's value?
A) The coercer
B) The acceptor
C) The owner himself
D) No one, the freeing is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the type of coercion determines liability: imprisonment does not make the coercer liable, but threat to life does; the acceptor bears the loss in ineffective coercion.

Divisibility of Manumission and Liability Under Coercion: Imam vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial manumission under coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on partial vs total manumission under coercion and distinguish the rulings based on the divisibility of manumission.

Core Idea
Manumission is divisible according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but indivisible according to the Sahibayn—this determines liability in cases of coercion.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the effect of coercion on manumission. When a person is coerced to free half a slave but frees the whole, or vice versa, the ruling differs between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions. The core issue is whether manumission (itq) can be divided into partial acts. We will explore the rationale behind each position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the two subcases: coerced to free half but frees all; coerced to free all but frees half.
2
Identify Imam's ruling
What does Imam Abu Hanifa rule in each subcase? He says no liability if frees more; if frees less, slave works for value.
Imam's view is based on divisibility.
3
Identify Sahibayn's ruling
What do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule? They hold the coercer liable in both cases.
Sahibayn view manumission as indivisible.
4
Analyze the underlying principle
The dispute hinges on tajazzi' (divisibility) of manumission. Explain how divisibility affects the status of coercion.
If manumission is divisible, coercion to free half does not extend to the other half.
5
Draw the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under Imam, the coercer may have less liability; under Sahibayn, full liability in both scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أكره بقتل على أن يقبل من رجل عتق عبده على ألف وقيمته خمسمائة ورب العبد طائع ففعل كان الولاء للقابل ولا ضمان عليه ولا على المكره لأن قبول العتق عنه بألف يتضمن شراء وقبضاً وإعتاقاً والمشتري مكره في جميع ذلك والمكره لا يضمن شيئاً للمولى، ولو أكره على أن يعتق نصف عبده فأعتق كله لم يضمن عند الإمام، وعندهما يضمن لأن عنده العتق يتجزئ وعندهما لا يتجزئ فالإكراه على إعتاق النصف إكراه على إعتاق الكل . ولو أكره على أن يعتق كله فأعتق نصفه يضمن عندهما، وعند الإمام يسعى في نصف قيمته ويضمن المكره نصف قيمته .اهـ. مختصراً بتأمل. هذا ما تقدم في البيع إذا أكره على الكل فباع النصف كان مكرهاً حيث عللوا بأن بيع النصف أشد ضرراً من بيع الكل وإعتاق الكل أشد ضرراً من عتق النصف ويطلب الفرق .
If he is coerced by death to accept from a man the manumission of his slave for a thousand while its value is five hundred, and the slave's owner is willing, and he does so, then the wala is for the acceptor and there is no liability on him nor on the coercer, because accepting the manumission on his behalf for a thousand includes a purchase, possession, and manumission, and the buyer is coerced in all that, and the coercer does not guarantee anything to the owner. If he is coerced to free half of his slave but he frees all, according to Imam Abu Hanifa he is not liable, but according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is liable, because according to him (Imam) manumission is divisible, while according to them it is not divisible, so coercion to free half is coercion to free all. If he is coerced to free all but he frees half, according to them he is liable, while according to Imam the slave works for half his value and the coercer guarantees half his value. (End) Briefly with reflection. This is what was stated earlier in sales: if coerced to sell all but sold half, he is considered coerced, as they reasoned that selling half is more harmful than selling all, while freeing all is more harmful than freeing half, and a distinction is sought.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage discusses two subcases: First, a person is coerced to accept manumission of a slave on someone's behalf for a price. The ruling: no liability on coercer or acceptor because the entire transaction is under coercion. Second, a key dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn) regarding partial vs. total manumission under coercion. If coerced to free half but frees all, Imam says no liability because manumission is divisible (so he exceeded what was coerced). The Sahibayn say he is liable because manumission is indivisible; coercion to free half implies coercion to free all. If coerced to free all but frees half, the Sahibayn hold him liable, while Imam says the slave works for half value and the coercer pays half value. The commentator then compares with the sale case: coercion to sell all but selling half is considered coercion because selling half is more harmful. But in manumission, freeing all is more harmful than half, so the analogy is inverse. The commentator highlights the apparent inconsistency and mentions that a distinction is sought.

Key Terms
تجزؤ (tajazzi'): Divisibility of a legal act. Here refers to whether manumission can be partial (i.e., freeing half a slave is possible).
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): The slave working to earn his own value to gain freedom, typically when freed partially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person is coerced to free half his slave but frees the whole, what is the ruling?
A) He is liable for the value.
B) No liability.
C) The slave must work for half value.
D) The manumission is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Whether a coerced partial manumission binds the coercer for the whole depends on the divisibility of manumission—a point of dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions.

Compulsion on Future Emancipation and Conditional Acts
Ruling on compulsion for future emancipation and conditional acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on liability when a person is compelled to declare all future slaves free or to suspend emancipation on an unavoidable act.

Core Idea
The compeller is liable only when the compelled act is unavoidable; otherwise the freed slave's value is not recoverable.

This lesson examines two scenarios of compulsion regarding emancipation of slaves. The first involves declaring all future slaves free under duress, while the second involves suspending emancipation on an act that the compelled person cannot avoid. Understanding the different rulings on compeller liability is crucial for applying the correct legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between compulsion to make future slaves free and compulsion to suspend emancipation on an unavoidable act.
The first scenario has no immediate effect; the second involves a conditional emancipation.
2
Determine the legal effect
In both cases, the emancipation occurs (slave is freed) when the act is performed or the slave is acquired.
3
Analyze compeller liability
In the first scenario, there is no liability because the emancipation is attributed to the person's own act. In the second, the compeller must pay the slave's value because the act was unavoidable.
The key distinction is whether the act was avoidable or not.
4
State the underlying reasoning
The compeller is considered coercing only when the act is unavoidable (mulji'); otherwise, the person retains freedom of choice.
The Arabic term 'ملجأ' indicates compulsion that leaves no choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أكره على أن يجعل كل مملوك يملكه حراً في المستقبل ففعل ثم ملك مملوكاً عتق عليه ولا ضمان على المكره لأن العتق حصل باعتبار صنع من جهته. وإن أكره على أن يعلق عتق عبده بفعل لا بد له منه نحو أن يقول إن صليت فعبدي . أو أكلت أو شربت ثم فعل المكره هذه حر الأشياء عتق العبد وغرم المكره قيمته لأنه لا بد له من هذه الأفعال وكان ملجأ. ولو أكره على أن يكفر ففعل لم يرجع بذلك على الذي أكره لأنه أمره بالخروج عن حق لزمه. ولو أكره على عتق عبد عن كفارة ففعل عتق وعلى المكره قيمته لأنه لمن لم يجب عليه أن يعتق عبداً معيناً عن كفارة معينة فهو بالإكراه معتدياً عليه ولا يجزيه عن الكفارة لأنه في معنى العتق بعوض. ولو قال أنا أبرئه عن القيمة حتى يجزئ عن الكفارة لم يجز ذلك لأن العتق نفذ غير مجزئ عن الكفارة والموجود بعد ذلك إبراء عن الدين وهو لا يتأدى به الكفارة. ولو قال اعتقه حين أكرهني وأنا أريد به عن الكفارة ولو أعتقه بإكراه أجزأه عن الكفارة وليس له أن يرجع بقيمة العبد على المكره
And if one is compelled to make every slave he owns free in the future, and he does so, then later acquires a slave, that slave is free, and there is no liability on the one who compelled because the emancipation occurred due to his own act. And if he is compelled to suspend the emancipation of his slave on an act he cannot avoid, such as saying 'if I pray, then my slave is free', or 'eat' or 'drink', and then he does those acts, the slave is freed and the one who compelled pays his value, because he cannot avoid these acts and he coerced. And if one is compelled to do kaffarah (expiation) and he does so, he cannot recover from the one who compelled because he ordered him to exit from a right that was required of him. And if one is compelled to free a slave for kaffarah, he does so, the slave is free and the one who compelled pays his value, because it is as if he was not obligated to free a specific slave for a specific kaffarah, so he is aggressing by compulsion, and it does not suffice for the kaffarah because it is like a sale for compensation. And if he says 'I absolve him of the value so that it suffices for the kaffarah', that is not valid because the emancipation executed was not sufficient for the kaffarah, and what remains is absolution of a debt, and kaffarah is not attained thereby. And if he says 'free him at the time he compelled me, and I intend it for the kaffarah', and he frees him under compulsion, it suffices for the kaffarah, and he cannot recover the value of the slave from the one who compelled.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on compulsion regarding future emancipation and expiation. For future freedom: if one is compelled to declare all future slaves free, it takes effect when he later acquires a slave, and the compeller is not liable because the act is from the person. For conditional emancipation on an unavoidable act (like praying, eating), the slave is freed and the compeller pays because the act was inevitable. For kaffarah: if one is compelled to do an expiatory act, he cannot recover from the compeller because the expiation was his own duty. If he is compelled to free a specific slave for kaffarah, the slave is free but the kaffarah is not fulfilled because it is like a sale; the compeller pays value. Intentions can change the result: if he intended the emancipation to be for kaffarah, it suffices and he cannot recover the value.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Compulsion or duress that overcomes a person's will.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; subject to compulsion rulings.
ملجأ (mulji'): An act that is unavoidable, leaving no choice.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to pay compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling, when is the compeller liable for the value of a slave freed under compulsion?
A) When the emancipation is conditional on a future event
B) When the emancipatory condition is an unavoidable act
C) Whenever the slave is freed as a result of compulsion
D) Only if the slave is freed immediately

Key Takeaway
Compeller liability in emancipation cases hinges on whether the compelled act is unavoidable; for future slaves or avoidable acts, the compeller is not liable.

Compulsion on Kaffarah Emancipation
Ruling on compulsion for kaffarah emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on whether a slave freed under compulsion fulfills kaffarah and when the compeller must pay the value.

Core Idea
Emancipation under compulsion for kaffarah does not fulfill the expiation unless the freed person intended it for kaffarah.

This lesson explores the complex rulings when a person is compelled to free a slave for expiation (kaffarah). The key issues are whether the emancipation counts for the kaffarah and whether the compeller is liable to pay the value. The Hanafi school emphasizes the role of intention (niyyah) in determining the validity of the expiatory act.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between general kaffarah and specific slave emancipation
First scenario: compulsion to perform kaffarah in general (any act) – no recovery from compeller. Second scenario: compulsion to free a specific slave for kaffarah – analyze separately.
General kaffarah is a personal duty; specific emancipation is a property transfer.
2
Analyze the default ruling for specific slave emancipation
If compelled to free a specific slave for kaffarah, the slave is freed, the compeller pays value, but the kaffarah is not fulfilled because it is like a sale (عتق بعوض).
The emancipation is valid but not for expiation.
3
Consider the effect of absolution of value
If the freed person says 'I absolve the compeller of the value so that it suffices for kaffarah', it does not work because the emancipation was already executed without intention for kaffarah.
Absolving a debt does not retroactively change the nature of the act.
4
Identify when the emancipation fulfills kaffarah
If the person states at the time of compulsion that he intends the emancipation for kaffarah, then it does fulfill the kaffarah, and he cannot recover the value from the compeller.
Intention (niyyah) is the determining factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if one is compelled to make every slave he owns free in the future, and he does so, then later acquires a slave, that slave is free, and there is no liability on the one who compelled because the emancipation occurred due to his own act. And if he is compelled to suspend the emancipation of his slave on an act he cannot avoid, such as saying 'if I pray, then my slave is free', or 'eat' or 'drink', and then he does those acts, the slave is freed and the one who compelled pays his value, because he cannot avoid these acts and he coerced. And if he says 'I absolve him of the value so that it suffices for the kaffarah', that is not valid because the emancipation executed was not sufficient for the kaffarah, and what remains is absolution of a debt, and kaffarah is not attained thereby. And if he says 'free him at the time he compelled me, and I intend it for the kaffarah', and he frees him under compulsion, it suffices for the kaffarah, and he cannot recover the value of the slave from the one who compelled.

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on compulsion regarding future emancipation and expiation. For future freedom: if one is compelled to declare all future slaves free, it takes effect when he later acquires a slave, and the compeller is not liable because the act is from the person. For conditional emancipation on an unavoidable act (like praying, eating), the slave is freed and the compeller pays because the act was inevitable. If he is compelled to free a specific slave for kaffarah, the slave is free but the kaffarah is not fulfilled because it is like a sale; the compeller pays value. Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for sins or failed religious obligations; here, freeing a slave is a means.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; subject to compulsion rulings.
إبراء (ibra'): Absolution or waiver of a financial right.
نيّة (niyyah): Intention; crucial for the validity of acts of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a slave freed under compulsion fulfill the kaffarah?
A) Always, because the act is done
B) Only if the freed person intends it for kaffarah at the time of emancipation
C) Only if the compeller pays the value
D) Only if the freed person absolves the compeller of the value

Key Takeaway
For kaffarah under compulsion, intention at the moment of emancipation is decisive; without it, the kaffarah is not fulfilled regardless of subsequent financial arrangements.

Ruling on Coerced Manumission for Kaffarah
Ruling on validity of coerced manumission for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced manumission for kaffarah and identify the condition under which it is valid.

Core Idea
Coerced manumission is valid for expiation if the coercee intends it for his kaffarah.

In Hanafi fiqh, coercion (ikrah) does not automatically invalidate all acts. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that a person coerced into freeing a slave can fulfill his kaffarah obligation if he intends the act for that purpose. This ruling highlights the school's nuanced view of intention and duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the freeing of a slave under coercion (ikrah). The legal category is an act of expiation (kaffarah).
2
State the condition for validity
According to the text, the manumission suffices for kaffarah only if the coerced person intends it for that purpose. Identify the phrase that indicates this condition.
Look at the phrase 'and I intend by it for the kaffarah'.
3
Note the consequence of fulfillment
If the condition is met, the act is valid and the obligation of kaffarah is discharged. The coercer does not acquire any liability over the slave.
The text says 'it suffices for him for the kaffarah'.
4
Recognize the implicit rejection of the opposing view
The Hanafi ruling contrasts with schools like the Shafi'i that generally invalidate coerced contracts. Consider why the Hanafi school differentiates here.
The opposing view might argue lack of consent, but Hanafis prioritize the intended worship act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرجع بقيمة الكفارة والموجود بعد ذلك إبراء عن الدين وهو لا يتأدى به الكفارة. ولو قال اعتقه حين أكرهني وأنا أريد به عن الكفارة ولو أعتقه بإكراه أجزأه عن الكفارة وليس له أن العبد على المكره
He returns with the value of the kaffarah, and the existing after that is a discharge of the debt, and it is not performed by it the kaffarah. And if he said, 'Free him when he coerces me, and I intend by it for the kaffarah,' and if he freed him under coercion, it suffices for him for the kaffarah, and it is not [possible] that the slave be upon the coercer.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This ruling states the validity of manumission under coercion for the purpose of kaffarah (expiation). The author clarifies that if a person is coerced into freeing a slave and explicitly intends it for his kaffarah, the act is valid and fulfills his obligation. The phrase 'and it is not [possible] that the slave be upon the coercer' means the coercer does not acquire ownership or liability over the slave. This is based on the Hanafi principle that coerced acts can be effective, especially when the coercion does not negate the intention entirely.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that compels one to act against his will

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it suffices for his kaffarah
B) No, coercion invalidates the act
C) Yes, but it does not count for kaffarah
D) It depends on the severity of coercion

Key Takeaway
Coerced manumission is valid for kaffarah in Hanafi fiqh if the coercee intends it for that purpose.

Definition and Wisdom of Legal Restriction (Hajr)
Introduction to the chapter on legal restriction (hajr) with linguistic and Shar'i definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shar'i definition of hajr, identify the three primary categories subject to hajr, and explain the wisdom (hikmah) behind legal restriction in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Hajr is a protective compassion, not a punishment: preventing those who cannot manage their property from harming themselves and others.

This passage opens the chapter on al-Hajr (legal restriction). The author first positions hajr after coercion because both involve removing authority, but hajr is gentler. Then he gives the linguistic and legal definitions, lists the categories, and explains the profound wisdom behind this institution—it is an act of compassion (shafaqah) rooted in the recognition of human differences in intellect and desire.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish hajr from coercion
Understand why the chapter follows coercion: both remove authority, but coercion removes authority from one who has full control, while hajr applies to those lacking full capacity.
This sets the context for hajr as a milder measure.
2
Grasp the linguistic definition
The word 'hajr' means prevention. It is used in the Quran for 'for one with a mind' and for the Hijr area of the Kaaba, which is prevented from entering the House.
Linguistic roots reinforce the legal meaning.
3
Memorize the Shar'i definition
Al-Hajr in Shar' is a specific prohibition on a specific person: the minor, the slave, and the insane.
These are the three primary categories.
4
Identify the three additional categories
Also included are the corrupt mufti, the ignorant physician, and the bankrupt merchant—though these are not universally agreed.
These show hajr can apply when professional incompetence or insolvency causes harm.
5
Understand the wisdom (hikmah)
Hajr embodies compassion for Allah's creation, balancing veneration of Allah's commands. It protects those whose desire overcomes intellect, preventing harm.
The human composition of intellect and desire underlies the need for hajr.
6
Examine the proof (dalil)
The Prophet placed legal restriction on Mu'adh ibn Jabal and distributed his property to creditors. This shows hajr is permissible and necessary to prevent harm.
The proof is a Sunnah hadith of the Prophet's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحجر في له اختیار صحيح أورد الحجر عقيب الإكراه لأن في كل واحد منهما سلب ولاية المختار عن الجري على موجب الاختيار إلا أن الإكراه لما كان أقوى تأثيراً لأن فيه سلبها عمن وولاية كاملة بخلاف الحجر والحجر في اللغة المنع من قولك حجر عليه القاضي يحجر حجراً إذا منعه من التصرف في ماله ولهذا سمي الحطيم حجراً لأنه منع من البيت، ومنه قوله تعالى هل في ذلك قسم الذي حجر ﴾ [الفجر: ٥] أي لذي عقل. وفي الشرع عبارة عن مبيع مخصوص في حق شخص مخصوص وهو الصغير والرقيق والمجنون وهذه الثلاثة سبب الحجر، وألحق بهذه الثلاثة ثلاثة أخر المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهلي والمكاري المفلس. ومن محاسن الحجر أن فيه شفقة على خلق الله وهي أحد طرفي الديانة والآخر التعظيم لأمر الله، وتحقيق ذلك أن الله تعالى خلق الورى وفرق بينهم في النهي فجعل بعضهم أولي النهي والرأي ومنهم أعلام الهدى ومصابيح الدجى، وبعضهم مبتلى بأساليب الردى فيما يرجع إلى المعاملات كالمجنون والمعتوه والرقيق والصغير وركب الله في البشر العقل والهوى، وركب في الملائكة العقل دون الهوى، وركب في البهائم الهوى دون العقل، فمن غلب عقله على هوائه كان من أفضل الخلق، ومن غلب هواه على عقله كان أردى من البهائم. ودليله ما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حجر على معاذ وقسم ماله لغرمائه ولأن تصرفه لا يشمل توفير النظر والمصلحة فلذا يحجر عليه . قال رحمه الله : (هو منع عن التصرف
Chapter of Legal Restriction. In it there is correct choice. He presented legal restriction after coercion because in each of them there is removal of the authority of the chooser from acting according to choice, except that coercion is stronger in effect because it removes it from one who has complete authority, unlike legal restriction. Legal restriction in language is prevention; you say: 'The judge placed a restriction (hajr) upon him' meaning he prevented him from disposing of his property. For this reason the Hatim [area of Ka'bah] is called Hijr because it is prevented from the House. And from it is the saying of Allah, the Exalted: 'Is there in that an oath for one who has a mind?' [al-Fajr:5] meaning one with intellect. And in Shar' it is an expression for a specific prohibition regarding a specific person, namely the minor, the slave, and the insane. These three are the cause of legal restriction. And joined to these three are three others: the corrupt mufti, the ignorant physician, and the bankrupt merchant. Among the beauties of legal restriction is that it contains compassion for Allah's creation, which is one of the two sides of religion, the other being veneration of Allah's command. The realization of this is that Allah created people and differentiated them in prohibition, making some possessors of prohibition and opinion, and among them are beacons of guidance and lamps of darkness, and some are afflicted with ways of harm regarding transactions, like the insane, the feeble-minded, the slave, and the minor. And Allah created in humans intellect and desire; in angels intellect without desire; in animals desire without intellect. Whoever's intellect overcomes his desire is among the best of creation, and whoever's desire overcomes his intellect is worse than animals. Its proof is what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, placed legal restriction on Mu'adh and divided his property among his creditors, and because his disposition does not include providing consideration and benefit, therefore he is placed under restriction. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It is prevention from disposition.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on al-Hajr (legal restriction/interdiction). The author first explains why it follows coercion: both involve removal of authority from the agent, but coercion is stronger. He then gives the linguistic definition of hajr as prevention, citing Quran and the term 'Hijr' for the Hatim area. The Shar'i definition is a specific prohibition regarding a specific person: the minor, slave, and insane. Three additional categories are added: a corrupt mufti, an ignorant physician, and an insolvent merchant (though these are not universally agreed). The author then elaborates on the wisdom of hajr, noting that it is a form of compassion (shafaqah) and a means to protect those who cannot manage their affairs, drawing on the distinction between intellect and desire in creation. The proof is a hadith that the Prophet placed restriction on Mu'adh ibn Jabal and distributed his property to creditors. The passage ends with the definition: 'It is prevention from disposition.'

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from disposing of his property due to incapacity
شفقة (shafaqah): Compassion; one of the two sides of religion (the other being veneration of Allah's commands).
حطيم (hateem): The semi-circular area adjacent to the Ka'bah, also called Hijr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A punishment for squandering wealth
C) Only applicable to minors
D) A type of coercion

Key Takeaway
Hajr is a compassionate legal interdiction to protect those who cannot manage their property, grounded in the Prophet's action and the wisdom of differentiating intellect from desire.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability in Coercion Cases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on coercion liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability when a coerced person throws himself into fire or from a height, and apply the Hanafi rules to similar coercion scenarios.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, coercion shifts liability to the coercer, but the Sahibayn differ on the details.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'q presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on three coercion scenarios: throwing oneself into fire, jumping from a mountain under threat of sword, and coerced marriage with deficient mahr. At stake are the principles of qiyas and the scope of the coerced's duty to resist. We will unpack each scenario and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the threat
For each sub-case, note what act the coerced is forced to do (throw himself into fire, jump, marry) and the threat (death by sword or other). Also note the presence of any alleviation (type takhfif).
The presence of alleviation affects Abu Hanifa's ruling on the coerced's choice.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
For the fire scenario: if there is some alleviation, the coerced has a choice; if he dies, the coercer faces qisas. For the mountain/sword scenario: diya on the 'aakilah of the coercer. For the marriage scenario: the guardian can annul or demand proper mahr.
Abu Hanifa uses qiyas: direct killing with a heavy object leads to diya on 'aakilah, so the same applies here.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the coerced must endure the threat and not comply. Their liability differs: for the fire scenario, they say the coerced should not do it; for the mountain scenario, Abu Yusuf says diya on coercer's own wealth, Muhammad says qisas. For marriage, they say the guardian has no right to annul because mahr is the woman's right.
The Sahibayn place greater responsibility on the coerced to resist, shifting liability differently.
4
Analyze the qiyas used by Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa analogizes the coercer's liability to direct killing with a heavy object (muthqal). In that case, the killer's 'aakilah pays diya, not qisas. Similarly here, the coercer's 'aakilah pays diya. But the Sahibayn reject this qiyas and hold the coercer directly liable.
The illa is that the killing is indirect (bi-sabab) and the instrument is not sharp.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is who pays: 'aakilah (Abu Hanifa), coercer's own wealth (Abu Yusuf), or qisas (Muhammad). In the marriage case, whether the guardian can intervene.
These differences affect the burden of blood money and the authority of the guardian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفسه حيث لا يحل له قتلها لأن الأطراف يسلك بها مسلك الأموال في حق صاحب الظرف حتى يحل له قطعها إذا أصابتها أكلة ولو أكره على أن يلقي نفسه في النار أو على الإلقاء من الجبل بالقتل وكان الإلقاء بحيث لا ينجو ولكن فيه نوع تخفيف فله الخيار إن شاء فعل وإن شاء لم يفعل عند الإمام، فلو ألقى نفسه في النار فاحترق فعلى المكره القصاص، وعندهما لا يصبر ولا يفعل. ولو قال له لتلقين نفسك من رأس الجبل أو لأقتلنك بالسيف فألقى نفسه فمات فعند أبي حنيفة تجب الدية على عاقلة المكره لأنه لو باشر لا يجب عليه القصاص لأنه قتل بالمثقل بل فيه الدية على العاقلة فكذا إذا أكره عليه. وعند الثاني تجب الدية على المكره في ماله. وعند محمد يجب القصاص. ولو قال لآخر اقتلني فقتله تجب الدية في ماله الصحيح. ولو أكرهت المرأة على التزويج بمهر فيه غبن فاحش ثم زال الأكراه فرضيت المرأة ولم يرض الولي فللولي الفراق عند أبي حنيفة أو يتم مهر المثل. وقالا : ليس له ذلك لأن المهر خالص حقها حتى تملك إسقاطه والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب.
Himself where it is not permissible for him to kill her because limbs are treated like property in the right of the owner such that it becomes permissible to cut them if gangrene afflicts them. And if he is coerced to throw himself into fire or to throw himself from a mountain with threat of killing, and the throwing is such that he cannot escape but there is some alleviation, then he has the choice: if he wishes he may do it, and if he wishes he may not, according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). If he throws himself into fire and burns, then the coercer is liable for retaliation. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not endure it and does not do it. And if he said to him: 'You must throw yourself from the top of the mountain or I will kill you with a sword', so he threw himself and died, then according to Abu Hanifa the blood money is obligatory on the 'aakilah of the coercer because if he had directly killed him, retaliation would not be obligatory because he killed with a heavy object, but rather the blood money is on the 'aakilah; similarly if he coerced him. According to the second (Abu Yusuf), the blood money is obligatory on the coercer's own wealth. According to Muhammad, retaliation is obligatory. And if he said to another: 'Kill me', and he killed him, the blood money is obligatory on his (the killer's) own wealth. And if a woman is coerced to marry with a mahr involving gross fraud, then the coercion ceases and the woman consents but the guardian does not consent, then the guardian has the right of separation according to Abu Hanifa, or to complete the proper mahr. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not have that because the mahr is exclusively her right such that she has the power to waive it. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on rulings related to coercion (ikrah). It begins by stating a general rule: limbs are treated like property with regard to their owner, so it is permissible to cut them if gangrene sets in, but it is not permissible to kill the person. The main discussion concerns when a person is coerced to throw himself into fire or from a mountain under threat of death. According to Abu Hanifa, if the action is such that there is some chance of survival (alleviation), the coerced has a choice: he may comply or refuse. If he complies and dies, the coercer is liable for retaliation (qisas). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the coerced should not comply and must endure the threat. Another scenario: the coercer threatens to kill with a sword if the coerced does not jump. Abu Hanifa rules that blood money (diya) is on the 'aakilah (agnatic helpers) of the coercer, because if the coercer had killed directly with a heavy object (muthqal), retaliation would not be due but diya on the 'aakilah; similarly here. Abu Yusuf holds diya is on the coercer's own wealth, while Muhammad holds retaliation is due. The passage also covers a case where someone asks another to kill him: the killer pays diya from his own wealth. Finally, a woman coerced into a marriage with grossly deficient mahr: after coercion ends, she consents but the guardian does not. Abu Hanifa gives the guardian the right to annul the marriage or demand proper mahr, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the guardian has no such right because the mahr is exclusively the woman's right which she can waive.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion; compelling someone to act against their will under threat of harm.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for murder or injury.
مهر مثل (mahr mithl): The proper mahr equivalent to what is customary for women of similar status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person is coerced to throw himself from a mountain under threat of death and he dies, who bears the liability?
A) The coerced person's estate
B) The coercer's 'aakilah (agnatic helpers)
C) The coercer's own wealth
D) No liability

Key Takeaway
In coercion leading to death, Hanafi imams differ on whether the coercer bears qisas, diya on 'aakilah, or diya on own wealth.

Definition and Primary Causes of Hajar (Legal Guardianship)
Definition of hajar and its three primary causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of hajar, identify its three primary causes (minor, slave, insane), and classify the types of transactions that are prohibited for each.

Core Idea
Hajar is a legal prohibition on property disposal for the minor, slave, and insane to protect their interests and others' rights.

Hajar (legal guardianship over property) is a key institution in Islamic law that restricts certain individuals from disposing of their wealth. This lesson introduces the definition, the three universally accepted causes, and the wisdom behind this prohibition, based on the opening of Bab al-Hajar in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the placement after Ikrah
Note that hajar is placed after coercion (ikrah) because both involve removing the authority of a competent person, but ikrah is stronger as it removes authority from someone who is fully competent.
2
Learn the linguistic and legal definitions
Linguistically, hajar means prevention; legally, it is a specific prohibition on specific persons. Identify the three persons: minor (saghir), slave (raqiq), and insane (majnun).
3
Identify the dalil (proof)
The proof is the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) placed hajar on Mu'adh and divided his property among his creditors, showing that hajar is permissible to protect creditors.
4
Distinguish between speech and action
Hajar prohibits certain verbal transactions (like sale and purchase) but not all actions. Actions that entail hadd or qisas (like fornication or murder) are still prohibited but the person is exempt from punishment due to lack of intent.
5
Recognize the extensions
Although the matn mentions only three causes, later scholars (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) add the prodigal, debtor, etc. This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فعلاً بصغر ورق وجنون فلا يصح تصرف صبي وعبد بلا إذن ولي وسيد ولا يصح تصرف المجنون المغلوب بحال) يعني يحجر عليه بهذه الأسباب المذكورة. واعترض عليه بأن هذه العبارة تفيد حصر المنع في هذه الثلاثة لأن ذكر الأفراد يفيده وليس كذلك بل يحجر على المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهل والمكاري المفلس بالاتفاق والسفيه والمغفل والمديون على قولهما وعليه الفتوى كما في البزازية فقوله في دليل التعريف بصغر إلى آخره» تفسير زائد وتقييد فاسد فالتعريف فيه قصور من حيث تقييد المطلق وأصل التعريف لمطلق الحقيقة يخلو إما إن أراد أن يعرف المنع المتفق عليه فعله أن يسقط الزيادة أو يزيد ومجانة وجهل وإفلاس ليكون سبباً للمتفق عليه أو يقول بسبب يوجبه، ولا يخفى أن الرق ليس سبباً للحجر في الحقيقة لأنه مكلف محتاج كامل الرأي والعقل وإنما حجر عليه لحق المولى. قوله «لا فعلا أراد فعلاً لم يتعلق به حكم يندرئ بالشبهات، أما إذا كان الفعل يتعلق به حكم يندرئ بالشبهات فهو محجور عليه في حكم ذلك الذي يندرئ بالشبهات كالصبي والمجنون إذا زنى أو قتل فهو محجور عليه بالنسبة لحكم الزنا وهو الحد وبالنسبة لحكم القتل وهو القصاص؛ كذا في الجوهرة. وقوله قولاً نكره في سياق الإثبات وهي تختص عندنا قالوا: المراد بالأقوال هنا ما تردد بين النفع والضرر كالبيع والشراء ويوجب الحجر من الأصل بالإعدام في حكم قول تمحض ضرراً كالطلاق والعتاق في حق الصبي والمجنون دون العبد فإن طلاقه يقع ولم يجب الحرج فيما تمحض نفعاً كقبول الهبة والهدية والصدقة. وقوله «لا فعلا نكرة في سياق النفي فيعم ما تقدم ذكره فإن قيل : الطلاق والعتاق والعفو عن القصاص واليمين والنذر كلها من الأقوال المعتبرة في الشرع والقصد ليس بشرط لاعتبارها شرعاً كما صرحوا به في مواضع لا سيما في مباحث الهزل في الأصول فكيف حكمت بأنها عدم من الصبي والمجنون مع أن القصد ليس بشرط في اعتبارها إذا صدرت مع تمام الأهلية؟ وأجيب بأن من ذكر له قصد وما يقصد وما ذكر ليس له قصد معتبر فافترق الحال ا هـ . قال رحمه الله : (فلا يصح تصرف صبي وعبد بلا إذن ولي وسيد لأن الصبي عديم العقل إذا كان غير مميز وإن كان مميزاً فعقله ناقص لعدم الاعتدال وهو البلوغ فيحتمل فيه الضرر فلا يجوز إلا إذا أذن له المولى فيصح حينئذ لترجح جانب المصلحة للمولى، فإذا أذن له بعد ذلك فيتصرف بأهليته إذا كان بالغاً عاقلاً وإن كان صغير فبأهلية الولي. وفي السراجية: للصغير الذي لا يعقل البيع إذا باع أو اشترى فأجاز الولي لم يصح ولو أذن القاضي للصبي بالتصرف صح تصرفه . قال رحمه الله : (ولا يصح تصرف المجنون المغلوب بحال) يعني لا يجوز تصرفه بحال ولو أجازه الولي لأن صحة العبارة بالتمييز وهو لا تمييز له فصار كبيع الطوطي، وإن كان يجن تارة ويفيق أخرى فهو في حال إفاقته كالعاقل والمعتوه كالصبي العاقل في تصرفاته
Chapter of Hajar. He placed Hajar after Ikrah because in each of them there is removal of the authority of the competent person to act according to his choice, except that Ikrah is stronger in effect because it removes authority from one who has full authority, unlike Hajar. Hajar linguistically is prevention; you say 'the judge placed him under hajar' meaning he prevented him from disposing of his property. Hence the Hijr (enclosure) of the Kaaba is so named because it is prevented from the House. From it is the saying of Allah: 'Is there in that an oath for one of understanding?' (89:5), meaning one of intellect. Legally, it is a specific prohibition for a specific person: the minor, the slave, and the insane. These three are the cause of hajar. Three others are annexed: the corrupt mufti, the ignorant doctor, and the insolvent donkey-driver. Among the beauties of hajar is compassion for creation, which is one pole of religion, the other being reverence for Allah's command. The proof is the narration that the Prophet (peace be upon him) placed hajar on Mu'adh and divided his property among his creditors. He (the author) said: 'It is prevention from disposal in speech, not action, by reason of minority, slavery, and insanity. So the disposal of a minor and a slave without permission of guardian or master is not valid, nor is the disposal of an insane person who is overcome (by insanity) in any state.' Meaning he is placed under hajar by these mentioned causes. An objection was raised that this expression implies restriction of prohibition to these three, but it is not so; rather, hajar is also placed on the corrupt mufti, ignorant doctor, and insolvent donkey-driver by agreement, and on the prodigal, the simpleton, and the debtor according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and on that is the fatwa. So his saying in the definition 'by minority' etc. is an extra interpretation and a corrupt restriction. The definition is deficient in restricting an absolute. And it is not hidden that slavery is not really a cause for hajar because the slave is accountable, needful, and of complete opinion and intellect; he is placed under hajar for the right of the master. His saying 'not action' means an action to which no ruling attached that is repelled by doubts. But if the action has a ruling repelled by doubts, then he is under hajar regarding that ruling, like a minor or insane who commits fornication or murder: he is under hajar regarding the ruling of fornication (hadd) and the ruling of murder (qisas). His saying 'in speech' is a general indefinite in an affirmative context; it is specific according to us. They said: what is meant by 'speech' here is what fluctuates between benefit and harm, like sale and purchase. It necessitates hajar originally. As for a word that is purely harmful, like divorce and manumission for the minor and insane, they are not affected; but for the slave, his divorce takes effect. As for what is purely beneficial, like accepting a gift, present, or charity, there is no prohibition. His saying 'not action' is a general indefinite in a negative context, so it includes what was mentioned. If it is said: divorce, manumission, waiver of qisas, oaths, and vows are all legally recognized statements, and intent is not a condition for their legal validity as stated in places, especially in discussions of jest in usul; so how did you rule that they are nonexistent from the minor and insane, while intent is not a condition when they occur with complete capacity? The answer: The one mentioned (the minor/insane) does not have legally considered intent, so the situation differs. He (the author) said: 'So the disposal of a minor and a slave without permission of guardian or master is not valid' because the minor lacking intellect if he is not discerning; if he is discerning, his intellect is deficient due to lack of maturity (puberty), so harm is possible. It is not permitted unless the master permits, then it is valid due to preponderance of interest for the master. If he permits thereafter, he disposes with his own capacity if he is adult and sane; if he is a minor, then with the capacity of the guardian. In al-Sirajiyyah: For a minor who does not understand sale, if he sells or buys and the guardian ratifies, it is not valid. If the judge permits the minor to dispose, his disposal is valid. He (the author) said: 'Nor is the disposal of an insane person who is overcome (by insanity) valid in any state' meaning it is not permissible in any state even if the guardian ratifies, because the validity of expression depends on discernment, and he has no discernment, so it is like the sale of a parrot. If he is insane at times and lucid at other times, then in his lucid state he is like a sane person, and the imbecile (ma'tuh) is like a discerning minor in his transactions.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Bab al-Hajar in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It provides a comprehensive definition of hajar (legal guardianship/prohibition from property disposal). The author explains why hajar is placed after ikrah (coercion): both involve removing the competent person's authority to act by choice, but ikrah is stronger because it removes authority from one who has full authority. Hajar etymologically means prevention; the Hijr of the Kaaba is so named because it is walled off from the House. The Qur'anic verse 'for one of understanding' uses 'hijr' to mean intellect. Legally, hajar is a specific prohibition on specific persons: the minor (saghir), the slave (raqiq), and the insane (majnun). These three are the original causes. Three additional persons are annexed later: a corrupt mufti, an ignorant doctor, and an insolvent donkey-driver (by agreement). The author then explains the wisdom of hajar: it shows compassion for creation, balancing reverence for Allah's commands. The proof cited is the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) placed hajar on Mu'adh and divided his property. The core definition from the matn is that hajar is prevention from disposal in speech (qawlan) not action (fi'lan), due to minority, slavery, or insanity. This means that the person is prohibited from certain verbal transactions like sale and purchase, but not from purely physical acts that have no legal consequence. However, there are exceptions: actions that entail hadd or qisas (like fornication or murder) are still prohibited even for the minor/insane, but they are exempt from punishment due to lack of intent. The commentary then clarifies that 'speech' here refers to transactions that can be either beneficial or harmful (e.g., sale). Purely harmful speech (like divorce or manumission) from a minor or insane is ineffective, while purely beneficial speech (accepting gifts) is valid. For slaves, divorce takes effect. The passage also addresses an objection: the definition seems to restrict hajar to the three causes, but later scholars add others. The author responds that the definition is only for the universally agreed-upon causes. The passage then elaborates on the rulings for minors (distinguishing between discerning and non-discerning), slaves, and the insane (including those with lucid intervals and the imbecile).

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajar): A legal prohibition from disposing of one's property, imposed on specific persons due to incapacity or for the rights of others.
صغير (saghir): A minor who has not reached puberty; his transactions are invalid without guardian permission.
مجنون (majnun): An insane person whose intellect is overcome; his transactions are invalid even if guardian ratifies, except in lucid intervals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three primary causes of hajar mentioned in the matn?
A) Minor (saghir)
B) Slave (raqiq)
C) Insane (majnun)
D) Prodigal (safih)

Key Takeaway
Hajar is a legal prohibition from property disposal for the minor, slave, and insane (and by extension others), based on the Prophet's precedent with Mu'adh, with the purpose of protecting the individual and others from harm.

Definition of Hajr: Classical Scope and Its Expansion
Expanding and critiquing the definition of hajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hajr, identify its classical causes, and explain why the mufta bihi includes additional causes beyond the three mentioned in the matn.

Core Idea
Hajr restricts verbal dispositions only; its causes extend beyond minority, slavery, and insanity to include folly, ignorance, and bankruptcy.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq critiques the definition of hajr (legal interdiction) as given in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The commentator first notes that the matn's restriction to three causes is too narrow, then explains why slavery is not an inherent cause, and finally distinguishes between categories of verbal acts. We will follow the commentator's analysis to understand both the classic formulation and the relied-upon operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classical definition
The matn defines hajr as 'prevention from disposition verbally, not physically, due to minority, slavery, and insanity.' Note that it specifies three causes.
This is the starting point; the commentator will critique it.
2
Observe the objection to the restricted list
The commentator objects that the matn's language implies restriction to three, but the school also interdicts the foolish mufti, ignorant doctor, bankrupt muleteer (by agreement), and spendthrift, negligent, debtor (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view.
3
Analyze the rationale for each cause
Slavery is not an inherent incapacity; the slave has full intellect but is interdicted to protect the master's rights. Minority and insanity are due to lack of capacity. Understand the different bases.
This explains the commentator's critique of the matn's wording.
4
Classify verbal acts into three categories
The commentator divides verbal dispositions into: (1) mixed benefit-harm (e.g., sale) → interdicted; (2) purely harmful (e.g., divorce, manumission) → not effective from minor/insane, but effective from slave; (3) purely beneficial (e.g., accepting a gift) → allowed without interdiction. Note the role of intent.
This shows the granularity of hajr's application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإكراه باب الحجر هو منع عن التصرف قولاً لا فعلاً بصغر ورق وجنون فلا يصح تصرف صبي وعبد بلا إذن ولي وسيد ولا يصح تصرف المجنون المغلوب بحال عقده ومن منهم وهو يعقله قولاً لا فعلاً بصغر ورق وجنون) يعني يحجر عليه بهذه الأسباب المذكورة. واعترض عليه بأن هذه العبارة تفيد حصر المنع في هذه الثلاثة لأن ذكر الأفراد يفيده وليس كذلك بل يحجر على المفتي الماجن والطبيب الجاهل والمكاري المفلس بالاتفاق والسفيه والمغفل والمديون على قولهما وعليه الفتوى كما في البزازية فقوله في دليل التعريف بصغر إلى آخره» تفسير زائد وتقييد فاسد فالتعريف فيه قصور من حيث تقييد المطلق وأصل التعريف لمطلق الحقيقة يخلو إما إن أراد أن يعرف المنع المتفق عليه فعله أن يسقط الزيادة أو يزيد ومجانة وجهل وإفلاس ليكون سبباً للمتفق عليه أو يقول بسبب يوجبه، ولا يخفى أن الرق ليس سبباً للحجر في الحقيقة لأنه مكلف محتاج كامل الرأي والعقل وإنما حجر عليه لحق المولى. قوله «لا فعلا أراد فعلاً لم يتعلق به حكم يندرئ بالشبهات، أما إذا كان الفعل يتعلق به حكم يندرئ بالشبهات فهو محجور عليه في حكم ذلك الذي يندرئ بالشبهات كالصبي والمجنون إذا زنى أو قتل فهو محجور عليه بالنسبة لحكم الزنا وهو الحد وبالنسبة لحكم القتل وهو القصاص؛ كذا في الجوهرة. وقوله قولاً نكره في سياق الإثبات وهي تختص عندنا قالوا: المراد بالأقوال هنا ما تردد بين النفع والضرر كالبيع والشراء ويوجب الحجر من الأصل بالإعدام في حكم قول تمحض ضرراً كالطلاق والعتاق في حق الصبي والمجنون دون العبد فإن طلاقه يقع ولم يجب الحرج فيما تمحض نفعاً كقبول الهبة والهدية والصدقة. وقوله «لا فعلا نكرة في سياق النفي فيعم ما تقدم ذكره فإن قيل : الطلاق والعتاق والعفو عن القصاص واليمين والنذر كلها من الأقوال المعتبرة في الشرع والقصد ليس بشرط لاعتبارها شرعاً كما صرحوا به في مواضع لا سيما في مباحث الهزل في الأصول فكيف حكمت بأنها عدم من الصبي والمجنون مع أن القصد ليس بشرط في اعتبارها إذا صدرت مع تمام الأهلية؟ وأجيب بأن من ذكر له قصد وما يقصد وما ذكر ليس له قصد معتبر فافترق الحال ا هـ .
Book of Coercion, Chapter of Interdiction: It is prevention from disposition verbally, not physically, due to minority, slavery, and insanity. So the disposition of a minor and a slave without the permission of a guardian and master is not valid, and the disposition of the continuously insane person is not valid under any condition, and whoever among them contracts while understanding, the guardian may validate it or annul it. It means he is interdicted by these mentioned causes. It was objected that this expression implies restriction of prevention to these three because mentioning individuals implies it, but it is not so; rather, the foolish mufti, the ignorant doctor, and the bankrupt muleteer are interdicted by agreement, and the spendthrift, the negligent, and the debtor are interdicted according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the fatwa is upon that, as in al-Bazzaziyya. So his statement in the evidence of the definition 'due to minority etc.' is an excessive interpretation and a corrupt restriction; thus the definition is deficient in restricting the absolute, and the basis of the definition according to the absolute reality is deficient. Either he intended to define the agreed-upon prevention, so he should drop the addition or add folly, ignorance, and bankruptcy to make it a cause for the agreed-upon, or he should say 'due to a cause that necessitates it.' It is not hidden that slavery is not a cause for interdiction in reality because he is a responsible person, in need, complete in opinion and intellect; rather, he is interdicted for the right of the master. His statement 'not physically' means an act to which no ruling is attached that is repelled by doubts. But if an act has a ruling attached that is repelled by doubts, then he is interdicted regarding that ruling which is repelled by doubts, like the minor and insane if they commit adultery or murder: they are interdicted regarding the ruling of adultery, which is the hadd, and regarding the ruling of murder, which is retaliation; thus in al-Jawhara. His statement 'verbally' is an indefinite in the context of affirmation, and it is specific according to us. They said: what is intended by 'verbal' here is that which fluctuates between benefit and harm, like sale and purchase, and it necessitates interdiction from the origin. As for a statement that is purely harmful, like divorce and manumission regarding the minor and insane, but not the slave, for his divorce occurs and interdiction is not necessary [؟]. As for what is purely beneficial, like accepting a gift, present, and charity. His statement 'not physically' is indefinite in the context of negation, so it includes what was mentioned earlier. For if it is said: divorce, manumission, pardon for retaliation, oath, and vow are all verbal acts considered in the Shariah, and intent is not a condition for their validity in Shariah, as they explicitly stated in places, especially in the discussions of jest in Usul; so how did you rule that they are nonexistent from the minor and insane, even though intent is not a condition for their consideration when issued with complete capacity? The answer: the person mentioned (i.e., the minor/insane) does not have intent that is considered, while the one in the objection (i.e., the sane adult) does have intent, so the situation differs. End quote.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of hajr (legal interdiction) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn defines hajr as preventing verbal dispositions (not physical acts) due to three causes: minority, slavery, and insanity. The commentator first objects that this definition is too narrow because the Hanafi school also interdicts other individuals by agreement (foolish mufti, ignorant doctor, bankrupt muleteer) and according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (with fatwa on their view) also the spendthrift, negligent, and debtor. Thus the matn's restriction is flawed. The commentator then explains that slavery is not an inherent cause for interdiction—slaves have full capacity but are interdicted to protect the master's right. Next, he clarifies 'not physically': physical acts that entail capital or corporal punishments (like murder or adultery) do incur liability because the act itself is real and cannot be undone. However, verbal acts are divided into three categories: (1) those with mixed benefit and harm (like sale) are interdicted; (2) purely harmful acts (divorce, manumission) are effective from a slave but not from a minor or insane person because they lack valid intent; (3) purely beneficial acts (accepting gifts) are allowed. The passage ends with a question: how can divorce, manumission, etc., be considered 'nonexistent' from the minor/insane when Shariah does not require intent for such acts? The answer distinguishes between a sane adult (whose intent is considered) and the minor/insane (who lack valid intent).

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal prevention from disposition (تصرف) in financial matters due to a legal cause.
التصرف (al-tasarruf): Disposition; here specifically verbal acts (contracts, statements) that have legal effect.
صغر (sighar): Minority; lack of physical and mental maturity.
رق (riqq): Slavery; being owned by another.
جنون (junun): Insanity; loss or severe impairment of intellect.
قول (qawl): Verbal statement; here the focus is on contracts and legal declarations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (the mufta bihi), which of the following is NOT a cause for legal interdiction (hajr)?
A) Minority
B) Slavery
C) Insanity
D) Being a physician (without ignorance)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hajr is broader than the three classical causes; the operative ruling includes interdiction for foolishness, ignorance, and bankruptcy, and the scope of interdiction is limited to verbal acts that are not purely beneficial.

Liability for Damages by Interdicted Persons
Ruling with rational proof and intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability of interdicted persons for property damage and explain the intra-madhhab dispute on enforcement timing.

Core Idea
Actions cannot be undone; liability arises from the act itself, but enforcement waits until ability.

This passage addresses a fundamental question: Are minors, the insane, and slaves liable for property they destroy? The Hanafi school answers with a rational proof: actions are physical realities that cannot be negated. However, enforcement of liability is postponed until the person has the capacity to pay. The text also records a dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on whether authorization by the owner affects immediate liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that if interdicted persons (minors, insane, slaves) destroy something, they are liable. Determine the legal ruling: daman (liability) is established.
This is the headline ruling.
2
Locate the rational proof
The author provides a dalil aqli: 'it is impossible to make killing not killing or cutting not cutting.' Actions are physical facts that cannot be undone, so liability is unavoidable.
This distinguishes actions from statements, which can be invalidated.
3
Understand the basis of liability: dhimma
Every human is born with a dhimma (legal personality) capable of bearing obligations. This establishes ahliyyat al-wujub, but payment (ada') is not demanded until ability (qudra), just like an insolvent debtor.
The text draws analogies to the insolvent and the sleeper.
4
Examine the intra-madhhab dispute
Imam Muhammad holds that if the owner authorized (by giving a deposit), the child or slave is not immediately liable. Imam Abu Yusuf says they are liable but enforcement is delayed for the slave until freedom and for the child until interdiction ends.
In the absence of authorization, both agree on immediate liability.
5
Apply the conditions
Liability requires: (1) an act of destruction, (2) property that is valuable and protected, and (3) absence of permission from the owner. If permission exists, liability is nullified.
This is not explicitly stated but derived from the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن أتلفوا شيئاً ضمنوا لأنهم غير محجور عليهم في الأفعال إذ لا يمكن أن يجعل القتل غير القتل والقطع غير القطع فاعتبر في حقه فثبت عليه موجبه لتحقق السبب ووجود أهلية الوجوب وهي الذمة لأن الإنسان يولد وله ذمة صالحة لوجوب الحق إلا أنه لا يطالب بالأداء إلا عند القدرة كالمعسر لا يطالب بالدين إلا إذا أيسر وكالنائم لا يطالب بالحال بالأداء إلا إذا استيقظ؛ هكذا قاله الشارح، فظاهره أن الوجوب يتأخر إلى البلوغ والعتق وفي الحدادي يضمن كما يضمن الحر البالغ العاقل، فظاهره أنه يضمن في العناية : جنين ابن يوم لو انقلب على قارورة إنسان فكسرها يجب عليه الضمان في فلو أن الصبي أو المجنون أو العبد استهلكوا مالاً ضمنوا المال في الحال. وفي التتارخانية : إذا أودع صبياً أو عبداً مالاً فاستهلكه لا يضمن الصبي ولا العبد في الحال على قول محمد. وقال أبو يوسف : يضمن إلا أن العبد يؤاخذ بعد العتق والصبي يؤاخذ بعد زوال الحجر لأنه لما أودعهم سلطهم عليه، وفي الأول لم يسلطهم فيضمن في الحال الصبي في ماله والعبد يدفعه المولى أو يفديه.
He (the author) said: (And if they destroy something, they are liable) because they are not interdicted in actions, since it is impossible to make killing not killing or cutting not cutting. So it is considered regarding them (the actions) and the liability is established because the cause is realized and there is capacity for obligation (dhimma). A human is born with a dhimma capable of bearing obligations, but he is not demanded to pay unless able, like the insolvent who is not demanded debt until wealthy, and the sleeper who is not demanded performance until awake. The commentator says this, so apparently the obligation is delayed until puberty and emancipation. In al-Hadadi, it is stated that such a person is liable like a free adult sane person, so apparently he is liable at once. In al-Inayah: a one-day-old fetus that rolls onto a person's bottle and breaks it, he must compensate. So if a child, insane, or slave consumes property, he is liable for the property immediately. In al-Tatarhaniyyah: if someone deposits money with a child or slave and he consumes it, the child and slave are not liable immediately according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf says they are liable, but the slave is taken to account after emancipation and the child after interdiction is removed, because the depositor authorized them; in the first case they did not authorize, so they are liable immediately – the child from his wealth and the slave either the master pays or ransoms him.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains that interdicted persons (minors, insane, slaves) are liable for damages caused by their actions because actions are physical realities that cannot be negated. Unlike statements, actions cannot be ignored. The basis of liability is the dhimma (legal personality) which exists from birth, but enforcement is delayed until they have the ability to pay (puberty, emancipation). There is an intra-madhhab dispute: according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, if someone entrusts property to a child or slave and that property is consumed, there is no immediate liability because the depositor authorized them. According to Abu Yusuf, they are liable but enforcement is postponed for the slave until freedom and for the child until the end of interdiction. The text also cites a case of a fetus causing damage, indicating liability.

Key Terms
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal personality or capacity to bear obligations; every human is born with a dhimma suitable for obligation of rights.
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): Capacity to have rights and obligations; the text says it exists in minors due to dhimma.
محجور عليهم في الأفعال (mahjur 'alayhim fi al-af'al): They are not interdicted in actions; actions are considered valid for liability purposes because they are physical facts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why are interdicted persons such as minors liable for damages caused by their actions?
A) Because they have full legal capacity like adults.
B) Because actions are physical events that cannot be made nonexistent.
C) Because the guardian forces them to pay immediately.
D) Because they intended to cause harm.

Key Takeaway
Interdicted persons are liable for damages because actions are irreversible; liability is immediate in principle but enforcement is deferred until financial capacity.

Slave's Acknowledgment: Hadd/Qisas vs Property
Explains the slave's acknowledgment rulings with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's acknowledgment of hadd or qisas, explain why it binds immediately, and distinguish it from acknowledgment of property.

Core Idea
A slave's acknowledgment of hadd/qisas binds immediately because these are rights of humanity, not property.

In Hanafi law, a slave's legal capacity is limited due to ownership, but some matters are intrinsic to humanity. This lesson explores why a slave's confession to a hadd or qisas crime is accepted immediately, while a property acknowledgment awaits emancipation. The key is the distinction between the slave's humanity and his property status. The Quranic verse 'man is a witness against himself' (75:14) serves as the primary proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of acknowledgment
Recognize that the passage distinguishes between acknowledgment of property (mal) and acknowledgment of hadd or qisas. For property, the ruling is deferred; for hadd/qisas, it is immediate.
2
Understand the rationale for immediate binding
The text states that the slave remains on original freedom (asl al-hurriyya) regarding hadd and qisas because they are from the distinctive characteristics of humanity (khawass al-insaniyya). The slave is owned only as property, not as a human in these matters.
Contrast with property: property acknowledgments affect the master's rights, hence deferred.
3
Analyze the objection from the hadith
The objection cites the hadith 'The slave and the mukatab own nothing except divorce.' The text responds by saying that acknowledgment of hadd/qisas is an acknowledgment of freedom, not property, so the hadith does not apply.
The indefinite 'thing' in a prohibition context implies generality, but it is limited by the context of property.
4
Apply the Quranic proof
The verse 'But man is a witness against himself' (75:14) is used to validate the slave's acknowledgment. The wajh al-dalalah is that a person's own statement is accepted against himself, even if he is a slave.
This proof confirms that acknowledgment is valid and effective.
5
Examine the master's inability to acknowledge
The master cannot acknowledge hadd/qisas on behalf of the slave because the slave retains freedom in these matters. The master's acknowledgment would be an admission against another (the slave), which is not accepted.
This reinforces that the slave's own acknowledgment is the operative one.

Source Text

البلوغ والعتق وفي الحدادي يضمن كما يضمن الحر البالغ العاقل فظاهره أنه يضمن في العناية جنين ابن يوم لو انقلب على قارورة إنسان فكسرها يجب عليه الضمان في فلو أن الصبي أو المجنون أو العبد استهلكوا مالاً وكذا إذا ثبت بالإقرار يجب في ماله ولو أقر لرجل بمال ثم صلح بأن صار أهلاً وقال أقررت بها باطلاً لم يلزمه وإن قال كان حقاً يلزمه وإن قال كان باطلاً لم يوجد منه إقرار بعد الصلاح فلا يلزمه وكذا الصبي المحجور البينة على ذلك والفرق أن في الأول أقر أن الاستهلاك وجد منه وادعى الإذن والتسليط وأنكر رب المال ذلك لما قال أقرضتك فيكون القول قول المحجور عليه وعلى رب المال البينة بخلاف الثانية قال رحمه الله
