122 Book of Coercion. Chapter: the slave in his own right not in the right of his master. So if he acknowledges property, it becomes binding after freedom. And if he acknowledges a hadd or qisas, it becomes binding. The present situation supports it. He said in the present situation regarding performance unless he wakes up; thus said the commentator. Its apparent meaning is that obligation is postponed to puberty and emancipation. And in al-Hadadi, he is liable as a free adult sane person is liable. Its apparent meaning is that he is liable. In al-‘Inaya: a one-day embryo if it turns over on a person's bottle and breaks it, liability is obligatory. For if a minor, insane, or slave consume property, they are liable for the property immediately. And in al-Tatarakhaniyyah: if one deposits money with a minor or slave and he consumes it, neither the minor nor the slave is liable immediately according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: he is liable, except that the slave is held accountable after emancipation and the minor after removal of interdiction because when he deposited with them, he empowered them. In the first case, he did not empower them, so the minor is liable immediately from his property, and the slave is liable and the master pays or ransoms him. He (the author) said: the acknowledgment of the minor and insane is not effective because the validity of statements in the law is dependent on legal capacity, which is absent in them. So even if a legal ruling like hadd is attached to their acknowledgment, it is not considered, except in the sense of destruction, so liability is obligatory. It is not said that this is known from his statement 'as a statement' because we say it is by way of guarantee, and explicit mention is more emphatic, so he mentioned it. He said: the acknowledgment of a slave is effective in his own right, not in the right of his master. So if he acknowledges property, it becomes binding after emancipation because it is an acknowledgment against another, namely the master, since he and what is in his hand are the master's property, and a person's acknowledgment against another is not accepted. When he is emancipated, the impediment is removed, so it becomes binding due to the existence of the cause of capacity. The apparent wording is that the acknowledgment is effective absolutely, whether he remains silent afterwards or says it is invalid or true. For this reason, it is said in al-Muhit: if he acknowledges consuming a deposit, then becomes sound and qualified to acknowledge, and he acknowledges that he consumed it during his unsoundness, he is not liable for intentionality, unlike if he acknowledges murder in this manner, where it becomes binding on his property, as if that was witnessed from him. The difference is that the consumption of the deposit was not proven by eyewitnessing, and by evidence he is not believed according to Muhammad, so likewise if proven by acknowledgment. As for murder, if it occurred from him and was witnessed, the blood money is obligatory on the ‘Aqila, and likewise if proven by acknowledgment, it becomes binding on his property. And if he acknowledges a debt to a man, then becomes sound and says 'I acknowledged it falsely', it is not binding on him. And if he says 'it was true', it becomes binding. And if he says 'it was false', no acknowledgment occurred from him after soundness, so it is not binding. Likewise, a minor under interdiction if he acknowledges consuming a person's property without his permission, then after puberty says 'it was true' or 'false'. And if he says to a man after soundness: 'you lent me during my unsoundness', and the other says: 'no, during your soundness and I consumed it', the saying is of the owner of the money unless the interdictor produces evidence to the contrary. The difference is that in the first, he acknowledged that the consumption occurred from him and claimed permission and empowerment, and the owner of the money denied that because he said 'I lent you', so the saying is of the interdictor, and the owner of the money must produce evidence, unlike the second. He said: and if he acknowledges a hadd or qisas, it becomes binding immediately because he remains on original freedom regarding them, since they are from the distinctive characteristics of humanity, and he is not owned from the aspect that he is a human, but from the aspect that he is property. For this reason, the master's acknowledgment of them against him is not valid because he remains on original freedom regarding them. If it is said: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The slave and the mukatab own nothing except divorce' and the text carries 'nothing' as an indefinite in the context of prohibition, so it is general, implying that he does not own acknowledgment of hadd and qisas. We say: because he remains on original freedom regarding them, his acknowledgment of them is an acknowledgment of freedom, not of servitude. And because the saying of Allah Almighty: 'But man is a witness against himself' [Qiyamah 14] implies that his acknowledgment is valid, so it is effective. Or it is said that it is narrated on other than this case to remove contradiction.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the rulings on liability and acknowledgment by persons with limited legal capacity: minors, the insane, and slaves. It begins by stating that a slave's acknowledgment is effective in his own right but not against his master; acknowledgment of property becomes binding after emancipation, while acknowledgment of hadd or qisas is binding immediately. The commentary then discusses various scenarios: a minor or slave who consumes a deposit is not liable immediately according to Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf holds them liable immediately, with the slave's accountability deferred to after emancipation and the minor after removal of interdiction. The matn states that the acknowledgment of a minor and insane is not effective because they lack legal capacity; however, if their action constitutes destruction (itlaf), liability arises. The discussion then focuses on the slave's acknowledgment: it is effective in his own right, and acknowledgment of property becomes binding after freedom. The text distinguishes between acknowledgment of consuming a deposit (not binding during unsoundness) and acknowledgment of murder (binding immediately) because murder is established by witness. It also covers cases where the person becomes sound and reaffirms or retracts his acknowledgment. Finally, the passage addresses the slave's acknowledgment of hadd or qisas: it is binding immediately because the slave retains original freedom regarding these rights that are peculiar to humanity. The master cannot acknowledge them on behalf of the slave. The objection from the hadith that the slave owns nothing is answered: the acknowledgment of hadd/qisas is an acknowledgment of freedom, not property. The Quranic verse 'but man is a witness against himself' supports the validity of the slave's acknowledgment.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission of a right or liability, here specifically by a slave.
قود (qisas): Retributive punishment, typically for murder or injury.
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave's acknowledgment of hadd or qisas binding immediately according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the slave is fully competent in criminal matters.
B) Because the slave remains on original freedom regarding these rights.
C) Because the master acknowledges on his behalf.
D) Because the hadith about the slave not owning property is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
A slave's acknowledgment of hadd or qisas binds immediately because these are human rights, not property.

Acknowledgment and Liability of Minors and the Insane
Explains rulings on liability and acknowledgment of minors and insane

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a minor's or insane person's acknowledgment is not effective, identify when they become liable for property destruction, and distinguish between the rulings of deposit consumption and murder in this context.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment by a minor or insane person is void due to lack of legal capacity, but liability arises from their actions if they cause destruction.

This lesson examines the legal capacity of minors and the insane in Hanafi law. Their statements (including acknowledgment) are not recognized because they lack the mental capacity required for legal speech. However, if they physically cause damage to someone's property, they become liable for compensation. The passage also highlights a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding liability for consuming a deposit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for minor/insane acknowledgment
The matn states: 'The acknowledgment of the minor and insane is not effective because the validity of statements in the law is dependent on legal capacity, which is absent in them.'
2
Understand the exception for liability (damān)
Even though their acknowledgment is invalid, if their act constitutes destruction (itlaf), they are liable. Liability arises from the act, not the statement.
The text says: 'except in the sense of destruction, so liability is obligatory.'
3
Analyze the deposit case dispute
If a minor or slave consumes a deposit, Muhammad holds no immediate liability because the depositor voluntarily entrusted them. Abu Yusuf holds immediate liability, but enforcement is deferred until the minor reaches majority or the slave is freed.
The difference hinges on empowerment (taslit).
4
Distinguish between deposit consumption and murder
Later in the passage, it is explained that if a person acknowledges consuming a deposit during unsoundness, he is not liable, but if he acknowledges murder, he is liable immediately. The difference: murder is proven by eyewitness, while deposit consumption is not.
This distinction applies to those who regain capacity and acknowledge.

الحرية في حقهما فإن قيل قال لا يملك العبد والمكاتب شيئاً إلا الطلاق والنص يحمل شيئاً نكرة في سياق النهي فتعم فيقتضي أن لا يملك الإقرار بالحدود والقصاص قلنا لما بقي على أصل الحرية في حقهما يكون إقراره بهما إقراراً بالحرية لا بالعبدية ولأن قوله تعالى بل الإنسان على نفسه بصيرة [القيامة ١٤] يقتضي أن يصح إقراره فينفذ أو يقال إن أنه روي على غير هذه الصورة دفعاً للتعارض
122 Book of Coercion. Chapter: the slave in his own right not in the right of his master. So if he acknowledges property, it becomes binding after freedom. And if he acknowledges a hadd or qisas, it becomes binding. The present situation supports it. He said in the present situation regarding performance unless he wakes up; thus said the commentator. Its apparent meaning is that obligation is postponed to puberty and emancipation. And in al-Hadadi, he is liable as a free adult sane person is liable. Its apparent meaning is that he is liable. In al-‘Inaya: a one-day embryo if it turns over on a person's bottle and breaks it, liability is obligatory. For if a minor, insane, or slave consume property, they are liable for the property immediately. And in al-Tatarakhaniyyah: if one deposits money with a minor or slave and he consumes it, neither the minor nor the slave is liable immediately according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: he is liable, except that the slave is held accountable after emancipation and the minor after removal of interdiction because when he deposited with them, he empowered them. In the first case, he did not empower them, so the minor is liable immediately from his property, and the slave is liable and the master pays or ransoms him. He (the author) said: the acknowledgment of the minor and insane is not effective because the validity of statements in the law is dependent on legal capacity, which is absent in them. So even if a legal ruling like hadd is attached to their acknowledgment, it is not considered, except in the sense of destruction, so liability is obligatory. It is not said that this is known from his statement 'as a statement' because we say it is by way of guarantee, and explicit mention is more emphatic, so he mentioned it. He said: the acknowledgment of a slave is effective in his own right, not in the right of his master. So if he acknowledges property, it becomes binding after emancipation because it is an acknowledgment against another, namely the master, since he and what is in his hand are the master's property, and a person's acknowledgment against another is not accepted. When he is emancipated, the impediment is removed, so it becomes binding due to the existence of the cause of capacity. The apparent wording is that the acknowledgment is effective absolutely, whether he remains silent afterwards or says it is invalid or true. For this reason, it is said in al-Muhit: if he acknowledges consuming a deposit, then becomes sound and qualified to acknowledge, and he acknowledges that he consumed it during his unsoundness, he is not liable for intentionality, unlike if he acknowledges murder in this manner, where it becomes binding on his property, as if that was witnessed from him. The difference is that the consumption of the deposit was not proven by eyewitnessing, and by evidence he is not believed according to Muhammad, so likewise if proven by acknowledgment. As for murder, if it occurred from him and was witnessed, the blood money is obligatory on the ‘Aqila, and likewise if proven by acknowledgment, it becomes binding on his property. And if he acknowledges a debt to a man, then becomes sound and says 'I acknowledged it falsely', it is not binding on him. And if he says 'it was true', it becomes binding. And if he says 'it was false', no acknowledgment occurred from him after soundness, so it is not binding. Likewise, a minor under interdiction if he acknowledges consuming a person's property without his permission, then after puberty says 'it was true' or 'false'. And if he says to a man after soundness: 'you lent me during my unsoundness', and the other says: 'no, during your soundness and I consumed it', the saying is of the owner of the money unless the interdictor produces evidence to the contrary. The difference is that in the first, he acknowledged that the consumption occurred from him and claimed permission and empowerment, and the owner of the money denied that because he said 'I lent you', so the saying is of the interdictor, and the owner of the money must produce evidence, unlike the second. He said: and if he acknowledges a hadd or qisas, it becomes binding immediately because he remains on original freedom regarding them, since they are from the distinctive characteristics of humanity, and he is not owned from the aspect that he is a human, but from the aspect that he is property. For this reason, the master's acknowledgment of them against him is not valid because he remains on original freedom regarding them. If it is said: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The slave and the mukatab own nothing except divorce' and the text carries 'nothing' as an indefinite in the context of prohibition, so it is general, implying that he does not own acknowledgment of hadd and qisas. We say: because he remains on original freedom regarding them, his acknowledgment of them is an acknowledgment of freedom, not of servitude. And because the saying of Allah Almighty: 'But man is a witness against himself' [Qiyamah 14] implies that his acknowledgment is valid, so it is effective. Or it is said that it is narrated on other than this case to remove contradiction.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the rulings on liability and acknowledgment by persons with limited legal capacity: minors, the insane, and slaves. It begins by stating that a slave's acknowledgment is effective in his own right but not against his master; acknowledgment of property becomes binding after emancipation, while acknowledgment of hadd or qisas is binding immediately. The commentary then discusses various scenarios: a minor or slave who consumes a deposit is not liable immediately according to Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf holds them liable immediately, with the slave's accountability deferred to after emancipation and the minor after removal of interdiction. The matn states that the acknowledgment of a minor and insane is not effective because they lack legal capacity; however, if their action constitutes destruction (itlaf), liability arises. The discussion then focuses on the slave's acknowledgment: it is effective in his own right, and acknowledgment of property becomes binding after freedom. The text distinguishes between acknowledgment of consuming a deposit (not binding during unsoundness) and acknowledgment of murder (binding immediately) because murder is established by witness. It also covers cases where the person becomes sound and reaffirms or retracts his acknowledgment. Finally, the passage addresses the slave's acknowledgment of hadd or qisas: it is binding immediately because the slave retains original freedom regarding these rights that are peculiar to humanity. The master cannot acknowledge them on behalf of the slave. The objection from the hadith that the slave owns nothing is answered: the acknowledgment of hadd/qisas is an acknowledgment of freedom, not property. The Quranic verse 'but man is a witness against himself' supports the validity of the slave's acknowledgment.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Interdiction preventing a person from disposing of property, here applied to minors, insane, slaves, and prodigals.
استهلاك (istihlak): Consumption or destruction of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a minor's acknowledgment not effective, but he can still be liable for property damage?
A) Acknowledgment requires legal capacity, but property damage is a physical act that creates liability independent of speech.
B) The acknowledgment is effective but cannot be enforced until adulthood.
C) The minor's guardian can acknowledge on his behalf, so his own acknowledgment is not needed.
D) Property damage is treated as a crime, and minors can be criminally liable.

Key Takeaway
Minors and insane persons are not bound by their words but are liable for their destructive actions.

Interdiction of the Prodigal (Safih): The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interdiction of the prodigal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the interdiction of the safih and explain the proofs from Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A prodigal (safih) is not interdicted according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn rule for interdiction to protect wealth.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute: whether a person who wastes wealth (safih) should be placed under interdiction (hajr). The source, from al-Bahr al-Raiq, presents Imam Abu Hanifa's view that the safih remains free to transact because he is rational, and the opposing view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) that the judge may interdict to protect his wealth. You will learn the proofs each side uses and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the position of Imam Abu Hanifa: he does not place the safih under interdiction. Cite his core reasoning: the safih is rational (aql kamil) and absolute (mutlaq) in his capacity.
The key distinction: the safih retains intellect, unlike the mentally incompetent (majnun or safih bi al-asl).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn): they hold that the judge should interdict the safih to prevent waste of wealth.
Their view is based on protecting the safih's own interests and those of dependents.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Sahibayn's evidence: Quran 2:282 (guardianship over the incompetent) and the hadith that the Prophet interdicted Mu'adh al-Ghiyathiyyah.
The verse is considered nas (explicit) by them, establishing general guardianship over the safih.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that the Quranic verse refers to incompetence in understanding contracts, not mere wastefulness. The hadith of Ma'adh is interpreted as specific to that case, not general. He adds the hadith where the Prophet did not interdict a man who deceived in sales, only advising him to say 'no deception.'
Abu Hanifa also uses qiyas: the safih is like the rational adult (rashid) who is not interdicted.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, a safih's transactions (e.g., gifts, sales) are valid and he is not restricted. According to the Sahibayn, the judge may interdict, making his subsequent dispositions subject to guardianship.
Further sub-issues: whether interdiction requires a judge's ruling or arises automatically from safah itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : لا بسفه يعني لا السفة عند الإمام وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد يحجر عليه للإمام ما روى أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ذكر له رجل يخدع ذي البيع فقال من بايعت فقل لا خلابة رواه البخاري ومسلم وفي رواية غيرهما قيل له احجر عليه ولأنه عاقل كامل العقل ألا ترى أنه مطلق فلا يحجر عليه كالرشيد ولهما قوله تعالى فإن كان الذي عليه الحق سفيهاً أو ضعيفاً أو لا يستطيع أن يمل هو فليملل وليه بالعدل [البقرة ٢٨٢] وهذا نص في إثبات الولاية على السفيه وما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حجر على معاذ الغياثية والمراد بالفساد هنا السفه وهو خفة تعتري الإنسان فتحمله على العمل بخلاف موجب الشرع والعقل مع قيام العقل وقد غلب في عرف الفقهاء على تبذير وإتلاف على خلاف مقتضى الشرع والعقل ا هـ وفي الأصل والحجر بسبب الفساد والسفه فهو نوعان أحدهما لخفة في العقل وكان سببه القلب لا يهتدي إلى التصرفات فيحجر عليه القاضي على قولهما والثاني أن يكون سفيهاً مضيعاً لماله إما في الشر بأن يجمع أهل الشر والفساد في داره ويطعمهم ويسقيهم ويصرف في النفقة ويفتح باب الجائزة والعطاء عليهم أو في الخيرات بأن ماله جميع في بناء مسجد وأشباهه فيحجر القاضي عند صاحبيه صيانة لماله واتفقا على أن الحجر عليه بالدين لا يثبت إلا بقضاء القاضي واختلفوا في الحجر الفساد والسفه قال أبو يوسف لا يثبت إلا بقضاء القاضي وعندهما يثبت بنفسه السفه ولا يتوقف على قضاء القاضي قال في المحيط القضاء بالحجر ليس بقضاء بل فتوى لعدم شرائط القضاء وهي الدعوى والإنكار حتى لو وجد الدعوى والأنكار بأن وهب السفيه ماله من إنسان وسلم إليه وصار فقيراً تجب نفقته على محارمه فيرفعوا أمرهم إلى القاضي وأخبروه بأنه يفني ماله سفهاً وطلبوا منه الحجر عليه فالقاضي يحضر السفيه والموهوب له فادعى عليه من وجبت عليه النفقة أن ماله في يد هذا الرجل فأمره يرده عليه فقضى القاضي بالرد عليه يفسد قضاء ا هـ وفي التهذيب وإذا وجد شرط الدعوى وقضاء القاضي صار متفقاً عليه فلا تنفذ تصرفاته بعد القضاء عندهما والإمام أيضاً ا هـ وفي المنتقى لو حجر عليه قاض فرفع ذلك إلى قاض آخر وأطلقه جاز إطلاقه لأن الحجر من الأول فتوى لتقدم شرطه كما تقدم قال صاحب الهداية ولو قضى القاضي فنفس القضاء مختلف فيه فلا بد من إمضاء قاض آخر حتى يلزم لأن الاختلاف إذا وقع في نفس البحر الرائق ج ٨/ م١٠
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Not due to prodigality' meaning not due to prodigality according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he is placed under interdiction. Imam (Abu Hanifa) has what was narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was told of a man who deceived in sales and he said: 'To whomever you sell, say: no deception.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim. In another narration, it was said to him: place him under interdiction. And because he is rational and of complete intellect; do you not see that he is absolute? So he is not placed under interdiction like the rational one. And for them (the two Sahibayn) is the saying of Allah: 'Then if the one who owes the debt is incompetent, weak, or cannot dictate himself, let his guardian dictate in justice' [Baqarah 282]. This is an explicit text for establishing guardianship over the incompetent. And what was narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) placed interdiction on Mu'adh al-Ghiyathiyyah? The meaning of fasad here is safah (prodigality), which is a lightheadedness that befalls a person, causing him to act contrary to legal and rational dictates while still having intellect. It has become prevalent in the terminology of jurists as wasteful and destructive spending contrary to Sharia and reason. End. In al-Asl: interdiction due to corruption and prodigality is of two types: one due to lightness in intellect, caused by the heart not being guided to transactions; the judge places him under interdiction according to them (the Sahibayn). The second: he is prodigal wasting his wealth, either in evil by gathering evil and corrupt people in his house, feeding and giving them, spending on entertainment and opening the door of gifts and charity to them, or in good deeds such as spending all his wealth on building a mosque and the like. Then the judge places him under interdiction according to the two companions to protect his wealth. They agreed that interdiction due to debt is established only by judgment of the judge. They differed regarding interdiction due to corruption and prodigality: Abu Yusuf said it is established only by judgment of the judge; while according to them (Muhammad and Abu Yusuf?) it is established by the prodigality itself and does not depend on the judge's judgment. It is said in al-Muhit: the adjudication of interdiction is not a judgment but a fatwa for lack of conditions of judgment, which are claim and denial. For if the prodigal gifts his wealth to someone and becomes poor, his relatives owed maintenance raise the matter to the judge and inform him that he is wasting his wealth prodigally and request his interdiction. The judge summons the prodigal and the donee, and the one owed maintenance claims that the wealth is in that man's hand, and he orders him to return it; if the judge adjudicates its return, his adjudication is invalid. End. In al-Tahdhib: if the condition of claim and the judge's judgment exist, it becomes agreed upon; his dispositions after the judgment are not effective according to them and also according to Imam. End. In al-Muntaqa: if a judge places him under interdiction and it is raised to another judge who releases him, his release is valid because the interdiction from the first was a fatwa due to the precedence of its condition as mentioned. The author of al-Hidayah said: if the judge adjudicates, then the adjudication itself is disputed, so another judge must enforce it to make it binding because the difference occurs in the very... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the classical Hanafi dispute on whether a prodigal (safih) is placed under interdiction. Imam Abu Hanifa held that a prodigal is not interdicted because he is rational and of complete intellect, citing the hadith about the man who deceived in sales—the Prophet did not order interdiction but advised saying 'no deception'—and analogy with the rational person who is not interdicted. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argued for interdiction, relying on the Quranic verse that establishes guardianship over the incompetent (safih) and on the report that the Prophet placed Ma'ath al-Ghiyathiyyah under interdiction. The passage defines safah as a form of lightheadedness causing wasteful spending contrary to Sharia and reason, despite the person being of sound mind. It then distinguishes two types of interdiction due to fasad/safah: one due to lack of intellect (where the judge intervenes according to the Sahibayn) and one due to wasting wealth in evil or excessive charity (where the judge may intervene to protect wealth). The text also notes that interdiction due to debt is established only by a judge's ruling. Abu Yusuf held that interdiction for fasad/safah also requires a judge's ruling, while the Sahibayn (or Muhammad) held it is established by the safah itself. Al-Muhit explains that an actual adjudication of interdiction is more like a fatwa unless proper legal proceedings (claim and denial) are followed. The passage concludes with reference to al-Hidayah that if a judge does adjudicate, it remains subject to dispute and requires another judge's enforcement.

Key Terms
سفيه (safih): A prodigal who wastes wealth in violation of Sharia and reason while retaining intellect.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction preventing a person from disposing of property, established by judge's ruling or by nature of the person's condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The Quran prohibits interdiction.
B) The safih is rational and of complete intellect.
C) The Sahibayn agreed with him.
D) The Prophet forbade interdiction entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a legitimate internal dispute on the interdiction of the prodigal; understanding both positions is essential for accurate fatwa.

Defining Safah and the Types of Hajr
Defining safah and types of hajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define safah in Hanafi terminology and distinguish the two types of hajr due to safah.

Core Idea
Safah is a cause for interdiction, but the school differs on whether it requires a judge's decree.

In this lesson, we examine the foundation of hajr bi al-safah: what exactly is safah, and what forms of interdiction does the Hanafi school recognize? The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines safah and categorizes the types of hajr before delving into the well-known intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of safah
Safah is a lightness that overcomes a person, causing actions contrary to shari'ah and reason while still sane. In juristic custom, it means wasteful spending and destruction of wealth.
This definition distinguishes the safih from the insane or the minor.
2
Recognize the two types of hajr due to safah
The first type is due to intellectual deficiency (khiffa fi al-aql) where the person cannot conduct transactions properly. The second is due to wasteful spending (tabdhir wa itlaf) in evil or even excessive good deeds.
Both types require a judge's decree according to the Sahibayn.
3
Note the agreement and disagreement
The Sahibayn agree that hajr due to debt requires a decree, but differ on whether hajr due to safah itself establishes interdiction without decree.
Abu Yusuf requires a decree; Muhammad holds safah itself suffices.
4
Understand the foundation for the dispute
The text then provides proofs for each position, setting the stage for the detailed discussion in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الحال لا بسفه يعني لا السفة عند الإمام. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد يحجر عليه. للإمام ما روى أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ذكر له رجل يخدع ذي البيع فقال: من بايعت فقل لا خلابة. رواه البخاري ومسلم وفي رواية غيرهما قيل له احجر عليه ولأنه عاقل كامل العقل ألا ترى أنه مطلق فلا يحجر عليه كالرشيد ولهما قوله تعالى فإن كان الذي عليه الحق سفيهاً أو ضعيفاً أو لا يستطيع أن يمل هو فليملل وليه بالعدل [البقرة: ٢٨٢] وهذا نص في إثبات الولاية على السفيه وما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حجر على معاذ الغياثية والمراد بالفساد هنا السفه وهو خفة تعتري الإنسان فتحمله على العمل بخلاف موجب الشرع والعقل مع قيام العقل وقد غلب في عرف الفقهاء على تبذير وإتلاف على خلاف مقتضى الشرع والعقل ا هـ . وفي الأصل والحجر بسبب الفساد والسفه فهو نوعان : : أحدهما لخفة في العقل وكان سببه القلب لا يهتدي إلى التصرفات فيحجر عليه القاضي على قولهما والثاني أن يكون سفيهاً مضيعاً لماله إما في الشر بأن يجمع أهل الشر والفساد في داره ويطعمهم ويسقيهم ويصرف في النفقة ويفتح باب الجائزة والعطاء عليهم، أو في الخيرات بأن ماله جميع في بناء مسجد وأشباهه فيحجر القاضي عند صاحبيه صيانة لماله. واتفقا على أن الحجر عليه بالدين لا يثبت إلا بقضاء القاضي، واختلفوا في الحجر الفساد والسفه ؛ قال أبو يوسف : لا يثبت إلا بقضاء القاضي. وعندهما يثبت بنفسه السفه ولا يتوقف على قضاء القاضي قال في المحيط القضاء بالحجر ليس بقضاء بل فتوى لعدم شرائط القضاء وهي الدعوى والإنكار حتى لو وجد الدعوى والأنكار بأن وهب السفيه ماله من إنسان وسلم إليه وصار فقيراً تجب نفقته على محارمه فيرفعوا أمرهم إلى القاضي وأخبروه بأنه يفني ماله سفهاً وطلبوا منه الحجر عليه فالقاضي يحضر السفيه والموهوب له فادعى عليه من وجبت عليه النفقة أن ماله في يد هذا الرجل فأمره يرده عليه فقضى القاضي بالرد عليه يفسد قضاء ا هـ . وفي التهذيب : وإذا وجد شرط الدعوى وقضاء القاضي صار متفقاً عليه فلا تنفذ تصرفاته بعد القضاء عندهما والإمام أيضاً ا هـ. وفي المنتقى : لو حجر عليه قاض فرفع ذلك إلى قاض آخر وأطلقه جاز إطلاقه لأن الحجر من الأول فتوى لتقدم شرطه كما تقدم. قال صاحب الهداية ولو قضى القاضي فنفس القضاء مختلف فيه فلا بد من إمضاء قاض آخر حتى يلزم لأن الاختلاف إذا وقع في نفس القضاء لا يلزم ولا يصير مجمعاً عليه حتى يمضيه قاض آخر بخلاف ما إذا كان الاختلاف موجوداً قبل القضاء فإنه بالقضاء الأول وجد شرطه فيكون مجمعاً عليه ا هـ . قال الشارح: وفيه نظر فإن محمداً يقول بأنه يصير محجوراً بنفس السفه قبل قضاء القاضي. وفي الأصل: الحجر بسبب السفه يقارن الحجر بالدين من ثلاثة وجوه الأول أن الحجر على السفيه لمعنى في ذاته أما الحجر بسبب الدين فلحق الغرماء. الثاني المحجور عليه بسبب السفه إذا أعتق عبداً ووجب عليه السعاية فإذا أدى لا يرجع به على المولى بعد زوال الحجر والمقضي عليه بالإفلاس إذا أعتق عبداً وبما في يده وجبت عليه السعاية، فإذا أدى يرجع بما أدى على المولى بعد زوال الحجر الثالث المحجور عليه بالدين يزول إقراره بعد زوال الحجر، وكذا حال قيام الحجر فيما يحدث في المال والمحجور عليه بالسفه لا يجوز إقراره لا في حال الحجر ولا بعد زوال الحجر لا في المال القائم ولا الحادث. وإذا صار السفيه مصلحاً لماله هل يزول الحجر من غير قضاء القاضي؟ فعند أبي يوسف لا يزول إلا بالقضاء، وعند محمد يزول من غير قضاء. وفي نوادر هشام عن محمد السفيه المحجور إذا زوج ابنته الصغيرة أو أخاه الصغير لم يجز . وفي البزازية والفتوى على قولهما.
At present, not due to foolishness, meaning there is no interdiction at Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he is interdicted. For Imam is what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was told about a man who deceives in transactions and said: 'Whoever you sell to, say: no deception.' Bukhari and Muslim narrated it, and in another narration it was said to him: 'Interdict him.' And because he is a sane person of complete intellect, do you not see that he is free, so he should not be interdicted like the prudent one. And for them is His statement: 'But if the one obligated is foolish or weak or unable to dictate himself, then let his guardian dictate with justice' [Al-Baqarah: 282] — this is a text establishing guardianship over the foolish person. And what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) interdicted Mu'adh al-Ghayyathiyya. The meaning of 'corruption' here is foolishness, which is a lightness that overcomes a person, causing him to act contrary to what the law and reason require, while still having reason. It has become prevalent in the terminology of jurists to mean wasteful spending and destruction contrary to the requirements of law and reason. End. In al-Asl: Interdiction due to corruption and foolishness is of two types: one due to lightness in intellect, the cause being the heart not guiding to proper transactions, so the judge interdicts according to the two companions. The second is when he is foolish, wasting his wealth either in evil matters by gathering evil and corrupt people in his home, feeding them, spending on them, and opening the door of gifts and grants, or in good deeds by spending all his wealth on building a mosque and the like, so the judge interdicts him according to the two companions to protect his wealth. They agreed that interdiction due to debt is only established by a judge's decree, and they differed about interdiction due to corruption and foolishness. Abu Yusuf said: it is only established by a judge's decree. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is established by the foolishness itself and does not depend on a judge's decree. It is said in al-Muhit: The decree of interdiction is not a decree but a fatwa, due to the absence of the conditions of a decree, which are a claim and denial. Even if there is a claim and denial, such as if the foolish person gives his wealth as a gift to someone and hands it over, becoming poor, so his expenses become obligatory on his relatives; they raise their matter to the judge and inform him that he is wasting his wealth foolishly and ask him to interdict him. The judge summons the foolish person and the donee, and the one on whom the expense is obligatory claims that the wealth is in this man's hand, so he orders him to return it, and the judge gives a decree for its return, that decree is invalid. End. In al-Tahdhib: If the condition of a claim and the judge's decree are present, it becomes agreed upon, so his transactions are not valid after the decree according to both, and also according to Imam. End. In al-Muntaqa: If a judge interdicted him, and then it was raised to another judge and he released him, his release is valid because the interdiction from the first was a fatwa due to the precedence of its condition as mentioned. The author of al-Hidayah said: If the judge decrees, the decree itself is disputed, so another judge must ratify it for it to be binding, because if the dispute is in the decree itself, it is not binding and does not become unanimous until another judge ratifies it, unlike if the dispute existed before the decree, then by the first decree its condition is found, so it becomes unanimous. End. The commentator said: There is a flaw in this, because Muhammad says that he becomes interdicted by the foolishness itself before the judge's decree. In al-Asl: Interdiction due to foolishness resembles interdiction due to debt in three aspects: First, interdiction of the fool is due to a meaning in himself, while interdiction due to debt is for the sake of creditors. Second, the interdicted due to foolishness, if he emancipates a slave and earning becomes obligatory upon him, then if he pays, he does not seek reimbursement from the master after the removal of interdiction, whereas the one decreed to be bankrupt, if he emancipates a slave and there is wealth in his hand, earning becomes obligatory, and if he pays, he seeks reimbursement from the master after removal of interdiction. Third, the interdicted due to debt, his acknowledgment is removed after the removal of interdiction, and similar during the interdiction concerning newly acquired wealth, but the interdicted due to foolishness, his acknowledgment is not valid neither during interdiction nor after its removal, nor in existing wealth nor in newly acquired. And if the fool becomes prudent in his wealth, does the interdiction cease without a judge's decree? According to Abu Yusuf, it does not cease except by a decree; according to Muhammad, it ceases without a decree. In the Nawadir of Hisham from Muhammad: The interdicted fool, if he marries off his young daughter or young brother, it is not valid. In al-Bazzaziyya: The fatwa is on their statement (the two companions).
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the chapter of interdiction (hajr). It opens by addressing the phrase 'not due to foolishness' in the matn, meaning that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a person is not interdicted simply because of foolishness (safah). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree and hold that such a person should be interdicted. The passage first presents the proof for Abu Hanifa: a hadith where the Prophet advised saying 'no deception' to a deceiving merchant, but did not order interdiction, and another narration where it was suggested to interdict but the Prophet did not explicitly do so. Abu Hanifa also argues that a fool is of complete intellect and is free, so he should not be interdicted like a prudent person. The Sahibayn counter with Quran 2:282, which establishes guardianship over a foolish person, and a hadith where the Prophet interdicted Mu'adh. The passage then defines safah as a lightness that causes a person to act against law and reason, and in juristic custom it refers to wasteful spending. It then describes two types of hajr due to corruption and safah: one due to intellectual lightness (the person does not know how to conduct transactions) and the other due to wasting wealth (either in evil or excessive good deeds). For the first type, the Sahibayn require a judge's decree; for the second, they also require it. The text then explains that hajr due to debt definitely requires a judge's decree. The central dispute is whether hajr due to safah is established by the mere existence of safah or only by a judge's decree. Abu Yusuf requires a judge's decree, while Muhammad holds that it is established by the foolishness itself without a decree. The passage further discusses the nature of the judge's decree: it is not a true judicial decree but a fatwa, because the conditions of a proper case (claim and denial) are absent. However, if a proper claim is made and the judge decrees, then all agree that transactions after the decree are invalid. If a decree is issued but disputed, it is not binding until ratified by another judge. The commentator notes a flaw: Muhammad already considers the person interdicted before any decree. The passage then lists three differences between hajr due to safah and hajr due to debt: (1) the reason for hajr is intrinsic vs. for creditors; (2) the effect of emancipation and earning; (3) the validity of acknowledgments (iqrar) — the fool's acknowledgment is never valid, while the debtor's acknowledgment becomes valid after hajr is lifted. Finally, it addresses whether the interdiction ceases when the fool becomes prudent: Abu Yusuf says only by a judge's decree, Muhammad says automatically. The fatwa is according to the Sahibayn (on their statement).

Key Terms
سفه (safah): A lightness that overcomes a person, causing him to act contrary to religious and rational requirements while still being sane; in juristic terminology, wasteful spending or destruction of wealth.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing a person from disposing of his property due to a deficiency (such as safah or debt).
رشد (rushd): Prudence and sound judgment in financial matters, the opposite of safah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT included in the definition of safah according to the text?
A) Acting contrary to the requirements of shari'ah
B) Having a lightness that overcomes the person
C) Being insane and lacking reason entirely
D) Wasteful spending and destruction of wealth

Key Takeaway
Safah is a legal cause for interdiction, defined as a lightness causing wasteful actions contrary to shari'ah and reason, and it gives rise to two types of hajr that both require a judge's decree according to the Sahibayn.

12 min
The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Need for a Judge's Decree in Hajr bi al-Safah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on requirement of qadi's decree for hajr bi al-safah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the need for a judge's decree in hajr bi al-safah, and apply the fatwa position.

Core Idea
Interdiction for foolishness requires a judge's decree according to the relied-upon fatwa, but Muhammad holds it is self-establishing.

Now that we have defined safah and the types of hajr, we turn to the central dispute: is a judge's decree necessary to establish interdiction due to safah, or does the mere existence of safah suffice? The passage details the proofs for each view, the nature of the decree, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the three positions
Abu Hanifa: no interdiction at all simply due to safah. Abu Yusuf: interdiction requires a judge's decree. Muhammad: the person becomes interdicted by the very fact of safah, without a decree.
These are the three views within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the proofs for each
Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith 'Whoever you sell to, say: no deception' and the argument that the fool is sane and free. The Sahibayn rely on Quran 2:282 and the hadith of Mu'adh's interdiction.
Both arguments are dalil naqli.
3
Understand the nature of the judge's decree
The decree of interdiction is considered a fatwa, not a true qada, because the conditions of a judicial case (claim and denial) are often absent. However, if a proper case is brought and the judge decrees, it becomes binding.
Another judge may ratify the decree to make it binding despite disagreement.
4
Examine the practical consequences
If hajr is established by safah alone (Muhammad), all transactions before a decree are invalid. If decree is required (Abu Yusuf), they remain valid until the decree. The fatwa follows the Sahibayn, meaning a decree is necessary for full effect.
5
Note the differences between hajr due to safah and hajr due to debt
The text lists three differences: (1) reason for hajr (intrinsic vs. creditors), (2) effect of emancipation and earning reimbursement, and (3) validity of acknowledgment (iqrar).
These differences highlight the distinct nature of each type of interdiction.

في الحال لا بسفه يعني لا السفة عند الإمام. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد يحجر عليه. للإمام ما روى أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ذكر له رجل يخدع ذي البيع فقال: من بايعت فقل لا خلابة. رواه البخاري ومسلم وفي رواية غيرهما قيل له احجر عليه ولأنه عاقل كامل العقل ألا ترى أنه مطلق فلا يحجر عليه كالرشيد ولهما قوله تعالى فإن كان الذي عليه الحق سفيهاً أو ضعيفاً أو لا يستطيع أن يمل هو فليملل وليه بالعدل [البقرة: ٢٨٢] وهذا نص في إثبات الولاية على السفيه وما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام حجر على معاذ الغياثية والمراد بالفساد هنا السفه وهو خفة تعتري الإنسان فتحمله على العمل بخلاف موجب الشرع والعقل مع قيام العقل وقد غلب في عرف الفقهاء على تبذير وإتلاف على خلاف مقتضى الشرع والعقل ا هـ . وفي الأصل والحجر بسبب الفساد والسفه فهو نوعان : : أحدهما لخفة في العقل وكان سببه القلب لا يهتدي إلى التصرفات فيحجر عليه القاضي على قولهما والثاني أن يكون سفيهاً مضيعاً لماله إما في الشر بأن يجمع أهل الشر والفساد في داره ويطعمهم ويسقيهم ويصرف في النفقة ويفتح باب الجائزة والعطاء عليهم، أو في الخيرات بأن ماله جميع في بناء مسجد وأشباهه فيحجر القاضي عند صاحبيه صيانة لماله. واتفقا على أن الحجر عليه بالدين لا يثبت إلا بقضاء القاضي، واختلفوا في الحجر الفساد والسفه ؛ قال أبو يوسف : لا يثبت إلا بقضاء القاضي. وعندهما يثبت بنفسه السفه ولا يتوقف على قضاء القاضي قال في المحيط القضاء بالحجر ليس بقضاء بل فتوى لعدم شرائط القضاء وهي الدعوى والإنكار حتى لو وجد الدعوى والأنكار بأن وهب السفيه ماله من إنسان وسلم إليه وصار فقيراً تجب نفقته على محارمه فيرفعوا أمرهم إلى القاضي وأخبروه بأنه يفني ماله سفهاً وطلبوا منه الحجر عليه فالقاضي يحضر السفيه والموهوب له فادعى عليه من وجبت عليه النفقة أن ماله في يد هذا الرجل فأمره يرده عليه فقضى At present, not due to foolishness, meaning there is no interdiction at Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he is interdicted. For Imam is what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was told about a man who deceives in transactions and said: 'Whoever you sell to, say: no deception.' Bukhari and Muslim narrated it, and in another narration it was said to him: 'Interdict him.' End. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the chapter of interdiction (hajr). It opens by addressing the phrase 'not due to foolishness' in the matn, meaning that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a person is not interdicted simply because of foolishness (safah). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree and hold that such a person should be interdicted. The passage first presents the proof for Abu Hanifa: a hadith where the Prophet advised saying 'no deception' to a deceiving merchant, but did not order interdiction, and another narration where it was suggested to interdict but the Prophet did not explicitly do so. Abu Hanifa also argues that a fool is of complete intellect and is free, so he should not be interdicted like a prudent person. The Sahibayn counter with Quran 2:282, which establishes guardianship over a foolish person, and a hadith where the Prophet interdicted Mu'adh. The passage then defines safah as a lightness that causes a person to act against law and reason, and in juristic custom it refers to wasteful spending. It then describes two types of hajr due to corruption and safah: one due to intellectual lightness (the person does not know how to conduct transactions) and the other due to wasting wealth (either in evil or excessive good deeds). For the first type, the Sahibayn require a judge's decree; for the second, they also require it. The text then explains that hajr due to debt definitely requires a judge's decree. The central dispute is whether hajr due to safah is established by the mere existence of safah or only by a judge's decree. Abu Yusuf requires a judge's decree, while Muhammad holds that it is established by the foolishness itself without a decree. The passage further discusses the nature of the judge's decree: it is not a true judicial decree but a fatwa, because the conditions of a proper case (claim and denial) are absent. However, if a proper claim is made and the judge decrees, then all agree that transactions after the decree are invalid. If a decree is issued but disputed, it is not binding until ratified by another judge. The commentator notes a flaw: Muhammad already considers the person interdicted before any decree. The passage then lists three differences between hajr due to safah and hajr due to debt: (1) the reason for hajr is intrinsic vs. for creditors; (2) the effect of emancipation and earning; (3) the validity of acknowledgments (iqrar) — the fool's acknowledgment is never valid, while the debtor's acknowledgment becomes valid after hajr is lifted. Finally, it addresses whether the interdiction ceases when the fool becomes prudent: Abu Yusuf says only by a judge's decree, Muhammad says automatically. The fatwa is according to the Sahibayn (on their statement).

Key Terms
سفه (safah): A lightness that overcomes a person, causing him to act contrary to religious and rational requirements while still being sane; in juristic terminology, wasteful spending or destruction of wealth.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing a person from disposing of his property due to a deficiency (such as safah or debt).
قضاء (qada): A judge's authoritative decree that settles a dispute; in this context, the decree of interdiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa cited in the text, what effect does a judge's decree of interdiction have on transactions conducted before the decree?
A) They become invalid from the moment of the decree.
B) They remain valid because the decree is not retroactive.
C) They are invalid only if the person was Safih at the time.
D) They are valid only with the judge's approval.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on hajr bi al-safah is resolved by the fatwa that a judge's decree is necessary to establish interdiction, but the person becomes interdicted only after the decree, not by safah alone.

The 25-Year Age Limit: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Property Delivery
Intra-Hanafi dispute on property delivery to a non-prudent adult

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the delivery of property to a non-prudent person, and explain the evidence each side uses.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa sets a maximum age of 25 for withholding property; the Sahibayn require actual rushd indefinitely.

This passage presents an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on when a person who reaches puberty but remains imprudent may control his property. Imam Abu Hanifa sets a fixed age limit of 25 years, while the Sahibayn condition it on the observation of rushd. Understanding this dispute reveals how different proofs—Quranic verses, a hadith, and practical reasoning—lead to different rulings within the same school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa
Clearly articulate that for Abu Hanifa, property is withheld until age 25 regardless of rushd, and transactions before that are valid.
This is the key rule for this lesson.
2
Identify the opposing view of the Sahibayn
State that the Sahibayn require rushd to be observed before property is given, and deem transactions invalid until then.
This is the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf to analyze.
3
Follow each side's proof
Examine the Quranic verses (4:2 and 4:6), the hadith of Ibn Umar, and the natural reasoning cited by Abu Hanifa.
Note the different interpretations of the same texts.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the conditional clause
Understand the usul principle that a conditional clause does not imply negation when the condition is absent, as used by Abu Hanifa to counter the Sahibayn's reliance on Quran 4:6.
This principle is central to the debate.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Identify how the two positions differ in real cases: e.g., a 30-year-old imprudent person, validity of transactions before a judge's interdiction.
This helps see why the dispute matters in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He (the author) said: 'If he reaches puberty without being prudent, his wealth is not given to him until he reaches twenty-five years, and his transaction before that is valid, and his wealth is given to him if he reaches that period being insolvent, and this is according to Imam. According to them (the two companions), it is not given to him until prudency is observed from him, and his transaction is never valid because of His statement: 'If you observe from them prudency, then give them their wealth' [An-Nisa: 6] — He made giving conditional upon the existence of prudency, so it is not allowed before. For Imam is His statement: 'And give the orphans their wealth' [An-Nisa: 2] — and the intended meaning is after puberty. And because the state of puberty may not separate him from foolishness due to the effect of childhood, so we set it at twenty-five years. And it is narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'The intellect of a man reaches its completion when he reaches twenty-five years.' And the natural scientists said: When he reaches twenty-five years, he may become a grandfather, because the earliest a boy reaches puberty is twelve years, then he has a child after six months, then that child reaches puberty at twelve years and has a child after six months, thus he becomes a grandfather. The second verse contains a conditional clause, and according to our principle, a conditional clause does not necessitate non-existence when the condition is absent, as known in its place. And the branching (of rulings) is not applicable according to Imam's statement but is applicable according to theirs. If he sells, his sale is not valid even if there is a benefit; the ruler may permit it because he is a responsible sane person. And it is valid in what harms him, like emancipation and divorce. And if he sold before the judge's interdiction upon him, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa because the fool is not interdicted; rather, evidence is sought from his transactions, and that is probable because it may be due to foolishness or it may be a trick to attract the hearts of mujahideen, so if it is ambiguous, its ruling is not established except by the judge's decree, unlike insanity, minority, and imbecility. According to Muhammad, it is not valid because the cause of interdiction...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation of a person who reaches puberty (bulugh) but does not display prudence (rushd). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such a person's wealth is withheld until he reaches the age of 25, and his transactions before that age are valid (even if he is not prudent). He is given his wealth at 25 even if he is still imprudent and insolvent. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they say the wealth is not given until prudency is clearly observed, and his transactions are never valid until that point. They cite Quran 4:6, which conditions giving wealth on observing rushd. Imam Abu Hanifa relies on Quran 4:2, which commands giving orphans their wealth, understood to mean after puberty, and he argues that the effect of childhood may persist beyond puberty, so a maximum age of 25 is set based on a narration from Ibn Umar that the intellect is completed at 25, and also based on the natural possibility of becoming a grandfather by that age (as explained by natural scientists). The text explains that according to Hanafi usul, a conditional clause does not imply non-existence when the condition is absent (i.e., the absence of rushd does not necessarily mean the wealth cannot be given at some point). The passage then discusses transactions before a judge's interdiction: according to Abu Hanifa, such transactions are valid because the fool is not interdicted without a decree, and his actions are ambiguous—they could be foolish or a permissible trick. So they are valid until a judge decrees otherwise. According to Muhammad, they are not valid because the cause of interdiction (safah) exists. The text ends abruptly but the point is clear.

Key Terms
رشد (rushd): Prudence in financial matters; the quality that allows a person to manage his property wisely.
سفيه (safih): A person who lacks rushd; one who is foolish or imprudent with wealth.
بلوغ (bulugh): Puberty; the age at which a person becomes legally responsible for religious duties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The property remains withheld until he becomes prudent, even after 25.
B) The property is given to him at age 25 regardless of his prudence.
C) The property is given to him only if he becomes prudent before 25.
D) The property is given to him at age 25 only if he is solvent.

Key Takeaway
The delivery of property to a non-prudent person after puberty is subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa sets a fixed age of 25, while the Sahibayn condition it on the observation of rushd.

The Safih's Property: Fixed Age or Conditional Prudence?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the safih's property handover

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the age of property handover for a safih and contrast the Quranic proofs used by each side.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa sets age 25; Sahibayn require prudence – both rely on different Quranic verses and usul principles.

The Hanafi school has a well-known internal difference on when the property of a prodigal (safih) who reaches puberty must be handed over. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states Abu Hanifa's view that a fixed age of 25 is the limit, while the sharh reveals the opposing position of the Sahibayn who insist on actual prudence (rushd). This lesson unpacks the proofs behind each view and the practical consequences that follow.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and the ikhtilaf
Identify the ruling from the matn (Abu Hanifa) and the conflicting opinion of the Sahibayn as presented in the sharh. Note the key difference: fixed age vs. conditional prudence.
The matn presents Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position, but the sharh records the dispute.
2
Locate the dalil for each side
Find the Quranic verses cited by each party. The Sahibayn rely on verse 4:6 (conditional on prudence). Abu Hanifa relies on verse 4:2 (general command) and additional evidence from Ibn Umar and natural science.
Both verses are from Surah al-Nisa.
3
Analyse the usul principle behind Abu Hanifa's argument
Understand Abu Hanifa's key usul principle: a conditional clause (shart) does not imply the negation of the ruling when the condition is absent. He argues that verse 4:6 does not prohibit handing over before prudence; it only states when it becomes obligatory.
This is a fundamental Hanafi principle: al-ta'liq bi al-shart la yūjib al-‘adam 'ind 'adam al-shart.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the safih's transactions before age 25 are valid and property is handed over at 25 even if he remains poor. Under the Sahibayn, transactions are invalid and property is withheld indefinitely until prudence is shown.
This drastically affects the legal position of a prodigal.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
Note that the sharh does not give a clear tarjih, but later fatwa collections (e.g., al-Bazzaziyya) tend to favour the Sahibayn's requirement of prudence in related issues.
The mufta bihi may vary by context; modern practice often requires actual capacity assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بلغ غير رشيد لم يدفع له ماله حتى يبلغ خمساً وعشرين سنة ونفذ تصرفه قبله ويدفع إليه ماله إن بلغ المدة معسراً وهذا عند الإمام، وعندهما لا يرفع إليه حتى يؤنس منه الرشد ولا يجوز تصرفه فيه أبداً لقوله تعالى فإن آنستم منه رشداً فادفعوا اليهم أموالهم [النساء : ٦] علق الدفع بوجود الرشد فلا يجوز قبله. وللإمام قوله تعالى وآتوا اليتامى أموالهم [النساء : ٢] والمراد منه بعد البلوغ ولأن حال البلوغ قد لا يفارقه السفه باعتبار أثر الصباء فقدرناه بخمس وعشرين سنة. وما روي عن ابن عمر ا أنه قال : ينتهي لب الرجل إذا بلغ خمساً وعشرين سنة. وقد قال أهل الطبائع : إذا بلغ خمساً وعشرين سنة فقد بلغ رشده لأنه يصير فيه جداً لأن أدنى ما يبلغ فيه الغلام اثنا عشر سنة فيولد له ولد لستة أشهر ثم الولد يبلغ اثني عشر سنة فيولد له ولد لستة أشهر فقد صار بذلك جداً. والآية الثانية فيها تعليق الشرط والتعليق بالشرط لا يوجب العدم عند عدم الشرط على أصلنا على ما عرف في موضعه. والتفريع لا يتأتى على قول الإمام ويتأتى على قولهما.
If he reaches maturity but not prudent, his wealth is not given to him until he reaches twenty-five years, and his disposition before that is effective, and his wealth is given to him if he reaches that period poor. This is according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not given to him until prudence is perceived from him, and his disposition is never valid because of His statement 'If you perceive from them prudence, then give them their wealth' [4:6], which attaches the giving to the existence of prudence, so it is not permissible before it. For Imam (Abu Hanifa) there is His statement 'And give the orphans their wealth' [4:2], which is intended after maturity. And because the state of maturity may not separate from foolishness due to the effect of childhood, so we set it at twenty-five years. And it is narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'The intellect of a man reaches its peak when he reaches twenty-five years.' And the natural scientists said: 'When he reaches twenty-five years, he has reached his reason because he becomes a grandfather at that time. For the least a boy reaches (puberty) is twelve years, then he has a child after six months, then the child reaches twelve years and has a child after six months, so he becomes a grandfather by that.' And the second verse has a conditional clause, and conditional clauses do not necessitate non-existence when the condition is absent according to our principle, as known in its place. And the branching (of rulings) does not apply according to Imam's opinion but applies according to theirs.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the core ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the safih (prodigal) who reaches puberty but is not yet prudent ('ghayr rashid'). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa: the property is withheld until age 25, regardless of whether he shows prudence. If he reaches 25 while poor, the property is given to him. His transactions before that age are valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: the property is not given until prudence is observed, and his transactions are never valid. The proof for the Sahibayn is the verse 4:6 which conditions the handover on the perception of prudence. Abu Hanifa argues using verse 4:2 which commands giving orphans their wealth after puberty, and he sets a maximum age of 25 based on a narration from Ibn Umar and the opinion of natural scientists. He argues that the conditional clause does not prohibit in the absence of the condition, according to Hanafi usul. The dispute is practical: the Sahibayn's position makes the safih's transactions invalid indefinitely, while Abu Hanifa's position limits the interdiction to age 25.

Key Terms
سفيه (safih): A person who is not prudent (ghayr rashid) in managing his wealth, i.e., a prodigal or foolish person who wastes money.
رشد (rushd): Prudence, maturity of judgment, ability to manage one's financial affairs properly.
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from freely disposing of his wealth due to incapacity (e.g., foolishness, insanity, minority).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) When he shows prudence

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a legitimate internal difference on whether a safih's property must be handed over at a fixed age or only upon proven prudence, based on different readings of Quranic verses and usul principles.

Four Exceptions: How the Mahjur Differs from a Minor
Enumeration of four exceptions where a mahjur differs from a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four exceptional rulings where a mahjur (interdicted person) differs from a minor and identify which personal acts of the mahjur are considered valid.

Core Idea
A mahjur is like a minor financially, but his manumission, tadbir, divorce, and marriage are valid; the guardian cannot transact on his behalf.

In Hanafi fiqh, an interdicted person (mahjur) is generally treated like a minor regarding financial restrictions. However, this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq highlights four specific differences that change how we rule on guardianship and personal status acts. Understanding these exceptions is critical for correctly applying rules of interdiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage begins with the principle: al-mahjur 'alayhi bi-manzilat al-sabi (the interdicted person is like a minor). Recognize that this sets a baseline of similarity regarding financial incapacity.
The minor is completely without legal capacity for financial transactions and personal status acts, but the mahjur has partial capacity.
2
Extract the First Exception: Guardian's Disposal
The first listed exception: 'One is that the guardian's disposal of the minor's property is valid, but of the mahjur's property is void.' This means the mahjur is not under the same guardianship; his property cannot be managed by a guardian without his consent.
This implies the mahjur himself must authorize transactions, unlike the minor whose guardian acts fully.
3
Extract the Second Exception: Personal Acts
The second exception: manumission (i'taq), tadbir (declaring a slave free upon death), divorce (talaq), and marriage (nikah) of the mahjur are valid, whereas these acts from a minor are invalid. This shows the mahjur retains legal capacity for personal status matters.
These four acts are counted as separate exceptions, making the total of four.
4
Analyze the Tadbir Refinement
The passage quotes al-Muhit: if the mahjur makes tadbir of his slave, it is valid. During his life, the slave does not compensate for the tadbir. After death, the slave works to pay his value as a mudabbar. However, the later shaykhs add: if the mahjur's peers consider such bequests wasteful, the slave works; if it is customary and within one-third, the slave does not work.
This refinement shows how custom (urf) can affect the ruling.
5
Memorize the Four Exceptions
Use a mnemonic: (1) Guardian's disposal on mahjur's property is void; (2) Mahjur's manumission is valid; (3) Mahjur's tadbir is valid; (4) Mahjur's divorce and marriage are valid. Commit these to memory as the four differences.
The tadbir refinement is an elaboration, not a separate exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى : المحجور عليه بمنزلة الصبي إلا في أربعة : أحدها أن تصرف الوصي في مال الصبي جائز وفي مال المحجور عليه باطل. الثاني إعتاق المحجور عليه وتدبيره وطلاقه ونكاحه جائز من مال الصبي لا تجوز . قال في المحيط : وإذا دبره عبده صح ولا يسعى في نقصان التدبير ما دام حياً، وإذا مات يسعى في قيمته مدبراً . قال :مشايخنا : هذا إذا كان أهل لصلاح يعدون هذه الوصية إسرافاً، فإن كانوا لا يعدونها إسرافاً بل معهوداً حساً لا يسعى في قيمته إذا كان يخرج من الثلث ا هـ.
Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The interdicted person is like a minor except in four [matters]. First: The guardian's disposal of the minor's property is valid, but in the property of the interdicted person it is void. Second: The manumission, tadbir, divorce, and marriage of the interdicted person are valid; from the property of a minor they are not valid. He said in al-Muhit: If he makes a tadbir of his slave, it is valid, and [the slave] does not work to make up for the deficiency of the tadbir as long as he (the master) is alive; when he dies, he works for his value as a mudabbar. He said: Our shaykhs said: This is if [the mahjur] is from people of righteousness who consider this bequest wasteful; if they do not consider it wasteful but it is customary, he does not work for his value if it [the value] comes out of the one-third. End quote.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling enumerating four exceptions where a mahjur (interdicted for foolishness) differs from a minor. Imam Muhammad states the general principle: the mahjur is like a minor regarding financial restrictions, but four specific rules are different. First: the guardian's actions (like selling) are valid on the minor's property but invalid on the mahjur's property – the mahjur himself is authorized to transact once he attains discretion? Actually the text says 'تصرف الوصي في مال الصبي جائز' meaning the guardian's actions on behalf of the minor are valid, but on the mahjur's property they are void. Second: the mahjur's own manumission, tadbir (declaring a slave to be free upon death), divorce, and marriage are valid, whereas a minor's are not. The text then quotes from al-Muhit a detail about tadbir: if the mahjur makes tadbir of his slave, it is valid, and the slave does not have to work to compensate for the tadbir during the mahjur's lifetime. When the mahjur dies, the slave works to pay his value as a mudabbar. The shaykhs add that this applies if the mahjur's peers consider such bequests wasteful; otherwise, if it is customary, the slave does not work for the value if it is within one-third of the estate.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (mahjur 'alayhi): An adult who has been interdicted by a judge due to foolishness (safah), restricting his financial transactions but not his personal status acts.
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by a master that his slave will be free upon the master's death.
وصي (wasiy): Guardian of a minor or executor of a will, not the same as the guardian of a mahjur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Selling property without guardian consent.
B) Divorce and marriage.
C) Accepting a gift.
D) Making a will beyond one-third.

Key Takeaway
The mahjur (interdicted person) is treated like a minor in financial restrictions, but four personal acts—manumission, tadbir, divorce, and marriage—are valid from the mahjur, and his guardian cannot transact on his property.

Interdiction Due to Fisq: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on whether a fasiq is interdicted like a safih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not interdict a fasiq and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A fasiq is not interdicted because rushd (financial competence) not piety is the criterion for property control.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute on whether a person who is sinful (fasiq) but financially competent can be legally restricted from handling his wealth. Abu Hanifa argues that interdiction is only for protecting property, not for punishment. We will examine the proofs and the practical difference between the schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds: a fasiq is not interdicted like a safih. The purpose of interdiction for safah is protection of property, but a fasiq manages his property well.
The text says 'عنده' referring to Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a fasiq should be interdicted as a punishment (zajran wa 'uqubatan) for his sin.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i view is based on the idea that fisq is a serious violation and the community should restrict the fasiq's financial freedom to deter sin.
The passage does not elaborate, but it is implied.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds by citing Quran 4:6, which conditions the return of property on 'rushd' (discernment). Rushd is defined here as financial competence, not religious piety. Additionally, a disbeliever (kafir) is not interdicted, so fisq alone cannot be a basis.
The verse: 'If you find in them discernment, give them their property.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi, a fasiq who is financially competent may freely transact. In Shafi'i, such a fasiq would be interdicted, meaning his transactions are restricted or void if he violates the interdiction.
This difference affects judges and guardians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه فسق وهو معطوف على قوله «لا بسفه». وقال حجر عليه بسبب الله تعالى: (وفسق) يعني لا يحجر عليه بالفسق كالسفه زجراً له وعقوبة له وعندهما الحجر على السفيه صيانة لماله والفاسق مصلح لماله فيدخل تحت قوله تعالى فإن آنستم منهم رشدا فادفعوا إليهم أموالهم [النساء : ٦] لأن رشداً نكرة فتعم فتتناوله الآية إذ الرشد المذكور في الآية المراد به الإصلاح في المال لا الدين لأن الكافر لا يحجر عليه والفسق الأصلي والطارئ سواء.
He (the author) said: 'fisq' and it is conjoined to his saying 'not due to safah'. And he said: 'He is interdicted due to Allah's command: and fisq' meaning he is not interdicted due to fisq like safah, as a deterrent and punishment for him. According to him (Abu Hanifa), interdiction of the safih is to protect his property, and the fasiq is a caretaker of his property, so he falls under His statement: 'If you find in them discernment, give them their property' (4:6) because 'discernment' is indefinite and thus general, so it includes him, since the discernment mentioned in the verse means good management of wealth, not religion, because the disbeliever is not interdicted, and original fisq and acquired fisq are the same.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an intra-madhhab dispute and also an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The author comments on the matn which said 'لا يحجر عليه بالفسق' meaning a person is not interdicted due to fisq (sinfulness) alone, unlike safah (foolishness/wastefulness). The text explains that Abu Hanifa's reason is that the purpose of interdiction for safah is to protect the person's property, but a fasiq (even if sinful) manages his property well, so he is not interdicted. The verse (4:6) speaks of 'rushd' (discernment) which refers to financial competence, not religious piety; hence a fasiq who is financially prudent is not interdicted. The text adds that original fisq (from birth?) and acquired fisq are the same in this ruling. The disagreement with Imam Shafi'i is implied: Shafi'i holds that a fasiq is interdicted as a punishment. The Hanafi response is based on the verse and the principle that the disbeliever is not interdicted, so fisq alone does not justify it.

Key Terms
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness, transgression against religious law.
رشد (rushd): Discernment or competence in managing wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a fasiq not interdicted?
A) Because he is still capable of managing his property.
B) Because interdiction is only for punishment, and fisq is not serious enough.
C) Because a fasiq is considered to have rushd (financial discernment), per Quran 4:6.
D) Because all sinners are automatically interdicted according to the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, interdiction is based on financial incompetence (safah), not moral sinfulness (fisq).
