Authorized Slave: Crime Meets Debt
Scenarios of debt and jinaaya timing for an authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the legal effects of debt and jinaaya on an authorized slave and determine the master's liability based on the timing of the crime and debt.

Core Idea
Debt attaches to the slave's value; jinaaya attaches to the slave's body—so debt restricts disposal but jinaaya does not.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out critical distinctions in Hanafi law regarding an authorized slave (ma'dhun) who both commits a crime and has debts. The rulings hinge on the timing of the crime relative to the debt, and on the nature of the obligation—whether it lies in the slave's dhimma (debt) or the master's dhimma (jinaaya). Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly attributing liability and ensuring creditors' rights are protected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Obligations
Recognize that an authorized slave can have two distinct types of obligations: debt (dayn) arising from trade, and liability for a crime (jinaaya) causing harm to a third party.
Debt is owed by the slave in his dhimma; jinaaya creates an obligation in the master's dhimma to hand over the slave or pay ransom.
2
Understand Their Loci
Debt attaches to the slave's monetary value (māliyyatuhu), while jinaaya attaches to the slave's body (i.e., the right to demand his handover) and does not encumber his value.
This explains why the master can sell or gift the slave after a crime but not after a debt arises.
3
Apply the Timing Rule for Master's Liability
If the crime precedes the debt, the master is liable to the crime victims because their right established on a slave free of debt, and the master's later act (incurring debt) encumbered the slave after their right. If the debt precedes the crime, the master is not liable because the victims received the slave as he already was—encumbered.
The practical outcome: when crime first, master pays the slave's value to victims; when debt first, victims get only the slave as is, no additional claim on master.
4
Handle Confessions by the Master
The master's confession to a crime is not accepted if a known debt exists (unless he pays the debt), because debt prevents the master from giving ownership of the slave to anyone else without creditors' consent. But if the slave already has a known crime, the master's confession to a second crime is accepted because jinaaya does not restrict disposal.
This distinction flows from the difference in the nature of the two obligations.
5
Determine Distribution When Both Exist
If both debts and crimes exist, the slave is sold, and the proceeds are distributed according to the priority of the rights. If debts arose between two crimes, each debt has its own ruling, and the crime victims get compensation from the master accordingly.
The exact distribution depends on the sequence; the text gives examples with half-value claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الجناية نقضه وفقهه أن دين العبد واجب في ذمة العبد لا في ذمة المولى وتعلق بماليته والمالية في العبد والحق الثابت في العين عجز المالك من تمليكه من غيره لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير كحق المرتهن في الرهن فأما موجب جناية العبد تجب في ذمة المولى وهو الدفع أو الفداء إلا أنه يتعلق بالعبد وهو دفعه ولا يتعلق بماليته، وإذا كان موجب الجناية يتعلق بذمة المولى فلا يحجزه عن التصرف فيه لأنه تصرف في محل خالص له لا حق للغير فيه إلا أنه إذا استهلكه ضمن قيمته لأن العبد محل إقامة حقهم وهو الدفع فصار كنصاب الزكاة وجبت فيه الزكاة ولا يحجر المالك عن التصرف فيه وإذا استهلكه ضمن فكذا هذا. ولو قتل رجلاً عمداً وعليه دين فصالح المولى على أن جعل العبد لأصحاب الجناية بحقهم لم يجز وليس لهم أن يقتلوه وقد سقط القصاص ويباع في الدين، فإن فضل شيء كان لصاحب الجناية وإلا فلا شيء له على أحد أبداً لأن تملك المولى العبد من ولي القصاص بالصلح لو صح يؤدي إلى إبطال حق الغرماء وفي المحيط : محجور اشترى ثوباً ولم يعلم مولاه بذلك حتى باع العبد
He handed him over for the crime, then a debt occurred to him; he is sold for the debt, and the crime victims take back his value from the master, because their right was established in a slave free of debt, and it only became occupied by the master's action after their right was established, so he becomes liable. Unlike if the slave committed the crime after the debt occurred and the master handed him over to the crime victims, then after handing over the slave became liable for the creditors' debt: the crime victims do not claim anything from the master, because what reached them is the amount of their right. Because their right was in a slave occupied by debt; he is sold for it, and it reached them likewise. Similarly, if he authorized him and no debt occurred until he killed a man by mistake, then a debt of one thousand dirhams occurred to him, then he handed him over for the crime and he was sold for both debts: the last crime victim claims from the master half his value, his share of the remaining debt, because if both debts had occurred before the crime, the crime victims would not claim from the master anything. If both debts occurred between the crimes, the crime victims claim from the master the entire value of the slave. If one occurred before the crime and the other after, each debt has its own ruling. He confesses against his slave to a crime then to another crime, hands him over to them half-half, then the first crime victim claims from the master half his value if the heirs contradict each other, because the confession to the second crime is a confession of ownership of the slave to the crime victims of the first crime. The validity of making the slave owned by the first crime victims does not prevent the validity of confession to the second crime. By confession to the crime he does not become choosing ransom nor liable for the slave's value, because he is not helpless to hand over, and he can hand over the entire slave to the first crime victims. His authorized slave has a known debt, or the master confessed to it, then he confessed against him to a crime: he is not believed unless he pays his debt. If he had a known crime and the master confessed against his slave to a second crime, his confession is valid. The difference is that the slave's debt prevents the master from giving the slave to others except with the creditors' consent; do you not see that if he sold or gave him as a gift, they could invalidate it? So likewise it prevents the validity of confession that entails ownership to others. As for the slave's crime, it does not prevent the master from giving the slave to others; do you not see that if he sold or gave him as a gift to another, it is valid and the crime guardian cannot invalidate it? The understanding is that the slave's debt is owed by the slave in his dhimma, not in the master's dhimma, and it attaches to his monetary value, which is in the slave. The right established in the property prevents the owner from giving it to others because it entails annulling the right of others, like the right of the mortgagee in a mortgage. As for the effect of the slave's crime, it is owed in the master's dhimma – it is handing over or ransom – except that it attaches to the slave, i.e., handing him over, and does not attach to his monetary value. When the effect of the crime attaches to the master's dhimma, it does not prevent him from acting in it because it is acting in a place that is purely his with no right of others. But if he destroys it, he is liable for its value, because the slave is the locus of establishing their right, i.e., handing over. So it becomes like the nisab of zakat, the zakat is due, and the owner is not prevented from acting in it. If he destroys it, he is liable; similarly here. If he kills a man intentionally and he has a debt, and the master settles by giving the slave to the crime victims of their right, it is not valid. They may not kill him, and retaliation is dropped. He is sold for the debt; if something remains, it belongs to the crime victim; otherwise, nothing is due from anyone ever, because the master making the slave owned by the retaliation guardian through settlement, if valid, would lead to annulling the right of the creditors. And in al-Muhit: A restricted slave bought a garment without his master's knowledge until the master sold the slave.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings concerning an authorized slave (ma'dhun) who commits a crime (jinaaya) while having debts. The key is the timing: if the debt arose after the crime, the master is liable to the crime victims because their right attached to the slave when he was free of debt. If the debt preceded, the master is not liable because the victims got what they were entitled to—a slave already encumbered by debt. The text also distinguishes between debt and jinaaya: debt is in the slave's dhimma and attaches to his value, restricting the master's disposal; jinaaya creates an obligation in the master's dhimma (to hand over or ransom) and attaches to the slave's body, not his value, so the master can still sell or gift him. If the master confesses to a crime when a known debt exists, the confession is not accepted; but if the slave has a known crime and the master confesses to another crime, it is accepted. The passage ends with a ruling that a settlement giving the slave to the crime victims in a murder case is invalid if it harms creditors.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade.
جناية (jinaaya): A crime causing injury to a third party, here specifically murder or bodily harm.
غرماء (ghuramā'): Creditors who hold a debt claim against the slave or master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An authorized slave commits a crime, then a debt arises. The master hands him over to the crime victims. What is the master's liability to the crime victims?
A) The master must pay the slave's value to the victims because the crime preceded the debt.
B) The master owes nothing because the debt cancels the crime liability.
C) The master must pay only half the slave's value.
D) The master must hand over the slave but is not liable for any value.

Key Takeaway
The master's liability for an authorized slave's crime depends on whether the crime occurred before or after debts, due to the different natures of debt (attaching to value) and jinaaya (attaching to body).

The Definition and Essential Elements of Ghasb (Usurpation)
Introduces ghasb with its definition, elements, and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi technical definition of ghasb, identify its rukn and shart, and distinguish between the two types of ghasb based on knowledge.

Core Idea
Ghasb is the wrongful removal of rightful possession over valuable property; it is haram and requires restitution.

This lesson opens the Book of Usurpation (Kitab al-Ghasb) and sets the foundation for understanding one of the most serious prohibitions in Islamic law. We will unpack the linguistic and technical definitions, the essential pillar and condition, the ruling, and the Quranic proof. Mastering this definition will allow you to correctly classify acts of unlawful possession and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context and linguistic meaning
The chapter follows the discussion on permission to trade because both involve possession, but ghasb is the opposite: one is lawful, the other unlawful. Linguistically, ghasb means taking by force or coercion, even extending to persons.
This contrast helps anchor the gravity of ghasb.
2
Analyze the technical definition
Ibn Nujaym defines ghasb as 'removing the rightful possession by establishing wrongful possession over a valuable, inviolable property that is transferable.' Each element (yad muhaqqa, yad mubtila, mal mutaqawwim muhtaram, qabil lil-naql) excludes certain cases.
Focus on the four qualifiers: removal, establishment, type of property, and transferability.
3
Identify the rukn and shart
The rukn (pillar) is the act of removing rightful possession and establishing wrongful possession itself. The shart (condition) is that the usurper be capable of transferring the property, implying physical or legal ability.
The rukn is the core action; the shart ensures feasibility.
4
Understand the ruling and types
The hukm (ruling) is that ghasb is haram and obligates the usurper to return the property if it exists, or its mithl/qimah if destroyed. Two types: with sin (knowingly) and without sin (ignorantly).
Ignorance removes the sin but not the obligation to compensate.
5
Connect to the proof
The dalil is Qur'an 18:79, where the king takes every ship 'ghasban' – condemning the act. This proves the prohibition, and the Hanafi school relies on it as a clear textual evidence.
The verse is given zanni al-dalala because it is a story, but the word ghasb directly indicates the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب كتاب الغصب هو إزالة اليد المحقة بإثبات اليد المبطلة في مال متقوم محترم قابل للنقل والاستخدام مالكاً. ما كتاب الغصب أورد الغصب بعد الإذن في التجارة لوجهين: أحدهما أن الغصب من أنواع التجارة حتى صح إقرار المأذون به ولم يصح بدين المهر من أنواع التجارة دون الثاني إذ المغصوب دام قائماً بعينه لا يكون الغاصب مالكاً لرقبته فصار كالعبد المأذون فإنه غير مالك لرقبته وما في يده من مال التجارة إلا أنه قدم الإذن في التجارة لأنه مشروع من كل وجه والغصب ليس بمشروع؛ ع؛ كذا في النهاية ونظر في هذه المناسبة بأن الغصب عبارة عن إزالة اليد والإزالة ليست من أنواع التجارة والذي أرى أن وجه المناسبة ما ذكره صاحب غاية البيان حيث قال: المأذون يتصرف في الشيء بالإذن الشرعي والغاصب يتصرف لا بالإذن الشرعي فبينهما مناسبة المقابلة والكلام في الغصب من وجوه الأول في معناه لغة، والثاني في ركنه، والثالث في شرطه والرابع في صفته والخامس في حكمه، والسادس في أنواعه، والسابع في دليله والثامن في معناه عند الفقهاء فهو في اللغة عبارة أخذ عن الشيء على وجه الغلبة والقهر سوا كان متقوماً أو غيره يقال غصبت زوجة فلان وولده ويطلق على حمل الإنسان على فعل ما لا يرضاه يقال غصبني فلان على فعال كذا. وركنه إزالة اليد المحقة وإثبات اليد المبطلة، وشرطه كون الغاصب قابلاً للنقل وللتحويل وصفته أنه حرام محرم على الغاصب ذلك، وحكمه وجوب رد المغصوب إن كان قائماً ومثله إن كان هالكاً أو قيمته . وأنواعه وهو على نوعين: نوع يتعلق به المأثم وهو ما وقع عن علم أنه مال الغير، ونوع لا يتعلق به المأثم وهو ما وقع عن جهل كمن أتلف مال غيره وهو يظن أنه له ودليله قوله تعالى وكان وراءهم ملك يأخذ كل سفينة غصباً ﴾ [الكهف: ۷۹] ومعناه عند الفقهاء ما سيذكره المؤلف قال رحمه الله : (هو إزالة اليد المحقة بإثبات اليد المبطلة في مال متقوم محترم قابل للنقل) فقوله «هو إزالة اليد «المحقة اخرج زوائد المغصوب فإنها غير مضمونة لأنه ليس فيها
Book of Usurpation. Book of Usurpation. It is removing the rightful possession by establishing wrongful possession over a valuable, inviolable property that is transferable and usable as property. What is the Book of Usurpation? The chapter on usurpation is placed after permission to trade for two reasons: one is that usurpation is a type of trade, so much so that the acknowledgment of the authorized slave is valid regarding it but invalid regarding dower debt (mahr) because it is not a type of trade; the second, because the usurped property, as long as it remains in its specific form, the usurper does not own its corpus, so it is like the authorized slave who does not own his own corpus, and what is in his hand is trade property. However, permission to trade was placed first because it is lawful in every respect while usurpation is not lawful; as in al-Nihaya. And he considered the appropriateness in that usurpation is an expression of removal of the hand, and removal is not a type of trade. What I see is that the appropriateness is what the author of Ghayat al-Bayan mentioned: the authorized slave acts with legal permission, the usurper acts without legal permission, so between them there is an appropriateness of opposition. The discussion on usurpation has several aspects: first its linguistic meaning, second its pillar (rukn), third its condition (shart), fourth its description (sifah), fifth its ruling (hukm), sixth its types (anwa'), seventh its proof (dalil), and eighth its technical meaning among jurists. Linguistically, it is taking something by force and coercion, whether it is valuable or not; one says 'he usurped so-and-so's wife and children,' and it is used for compelling a person to do something he does not like; one says 'so-and-so compelled me to do such-and-such.' Its pillar is removing the rightful possession and establishing wrongful possession; its condition is that the usurper be capable of transfer and conversion; its description is that it is prohibited and forbidden for the usurper; its ruling is the obligation to return the usurped property if it still exists, its equivalent if it is destroyed, or its value. Its types: a type that incurs sin, which occurs with knowledge that it is another's property; and a type that does not incur sin, which occurs out of ignorance, like one who destroys another's property thinking it to be his own. Its proof is the statement of Allah Almighty: 'And there was behind them a king who would take every ship by usurpation' [al-Kahf: 79]. Its technical meaning among jurists is what the author will mention; he, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is removing rightful possession by establishing wrongful possession over a valuable, inviolable property that is transferable.' So his statement 'removing the rightful possession' excludes increments of the usurped property, for they are not guaranteed because they are not...
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Usurpation (Kitab al-Ghasb) from the sharh. It begins by explaining why this book follows the Book of the Authorized Slave: there is a connection that usurpation is a type of trade in some sense, or more precisely, there is a contrast — the authorized slave acts with permission, the usurper acts without permission. Then it outlines eight aspects of ghasb: linguistic meaning, pillar, condition, description, ruling, types, proof, and technical meaning. The linguistic definition: taking by force and coercion, including non-property like wife and children. The pillar (rukn) is the removal of rightful possession and establishment of wrongful possession. The condition is that the usurper be capable of transfer. The description is that it is haram. The ruling is the obligation to return the property if it exists, its mithl (equivalent) if destroyed and it is a mithli item, or its qimah (value) if not mithli. There are two types: with sin (knowingly) and without sin (ignorantly). The proof is the Qur'anic verse about the king taking ships by force (al-Kahf: 79). The technical definition is given by the author (Ibn Nujaym): 'Removing the rightful possession by establishing wrongful possession over a valuable, inviolable property that is transferable.' The commentary then explains that the phrase 'removing rightful possession' excludes the increments of the usurped property (e.g., fruits from a usurped tree) because they are not guaranteed — this is a specific Hanafi nuance.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Removing rightful possession and establishing wrongful possession over a valuable, inviolable property that is transferable and usable, for the purpose of taking it by force.
اليد المحقة (al-yad al-muhaqqa): Rightful possession — possession that is legally justified, such as ownership or trusteeship.
اليد المبطلة (al-yad al-mubtila): Wrongful possession — possession that has no legal basis, typically by force or fraud.
مال متقوم محترم (mal mutaqawwim muhtaram): Valuable and inviolable property — property that has legal monetary value and is protected by law (e.g., not wine or pigs).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the rukn (pillar) of ghasb?
A) Taking property by force.
B) Removal of rightful possession and establishment of wrongful possession.
C) The usurper's ability to transfer.
D) The property being valuable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ghasb hinges on two actions: removing rightful possession and establishing wrongful possession over valuable, inviolable, transferable property, making it haram and requiring restitution.

Riding an Animal Without Permission is Ghasb, Sitting on a Carpet is Not
Defines ghasb for riding vs sitting, with fatwas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that riding an animal without permission constitutes ghasb only if the animal is moved from its place, explain why sitting on a carpet does not amount to ghasb, and apply this principle to subsidiary cases involving liability.

Core Idea
Ghasb requires a physical transfer or displacement; sitting on a carpet does not transfer possession.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentator explains the boundary of ghasb (unlawful appropriation). The key distinction is between acts that physically transfer or displace property and those that merely use it without taking possession. Riding an animal without permission is ghasb only if the rider moves the animal; sitting on a carpet does not touch the owner's dominion. The fatwas that follow illustrate this principle in various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core rulings
Read the opening line: riding an animal (without permission) is ghasb; sitting on a carpet is not. Note the contrast.
The Arabic uses al-haml (carrying/riding) vs al-julus (sitting).
2
Examine the reasoning for each ruling
For riding: liability attaches only if the animal is moved from its place. The commentator clarifies that mere mounting without moving is not ghasb. For sitting: the carpet remains under the owner's hand because the owner spread it; sitting does not transfer possession.
The reasoning (talil) for sitting: 'the owner's action remains, so the effect of his hand remains'.
3
Review the subsidiary fatwas
Work through each fatwa: rider who doesn't move and another slaughters – liability on the slaughterer; boy who takes axe – no liability on owner; slave-girl sent to trader – liability on trader's wife; returning slave-girl – trader's word accepted unless he took physical control.
These fatwas illustrate the principle that physical transfer or control is key.
4
Understand the sharh chain
The matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) stated only 'لا الجلوس على البساط' – sitting on a carpet is not ghasb. The commentator expands this with reasoning and supporting fatwas. Note his comment that the matn could have been clearer by adding 'using, carrying, moving'.
This shows how later Hanafi scholars refined and explained earlier texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب
والحمل على الدابة غصب لا الجلوس على البساط ويجب رد عينه في مكان غصبه أو بنفس الحمل حولها عن مكانها أو لا. قال في فتاوى أبي الليث ركب دابة بغير إذن مالكها ثم نزل عنها وتكرها في مكانها ذكر في آخر كتاب اللقطة أنه يضمن، والصحيح أنه لا يضمن حتى يحولها، وفي الغياثية هو المختار وفي المنتقى لا ضمان عليه. رجل تعدى على ظهر دابة ولم يحولها عن موضعها وجاء رجل آخر وعقرها فالضمان على الذي عقرها. وفي أجناس الناطفي : رجل يكسر الحطب فجاء غلام وقال اعطني القدوم حتى أكسر أنا مكانك فأبى صاحب الحطب فأخذ الغلام القدوم فكسر فضرب فوقع بعض المكسور على عين الغلام لا يكون على صاحب الحطب شيء، ولو وجه جارية إلى النخاس ليبيعها فبعثتها امرأة النخاس في حاجتها فهربت فالضمان على المرأة. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث: جارية جاءت إلى النخاس وطلبت البيع ثم ذهبت ولا يدري أين ذهبت وقال النخاس رددتها على مولاها فالقول له والمعنى أن النخاس لم يأخذ الجارية ومعنى الرد أمرها بالذهاب إلى منزل السيد فلو أخذها النخاس أو ذهب بها إلى منزل مولاها فلا يصدق في قوله رددتها، فلو قال رحمه الله وبالاستخدام له والحمل والتحويل لكان أولى لما علمت. قال رحمه الله : (لا الجلوس على البساط) لأن الجلوس على بساط الغير ليس بتصرف فيه ولهذا لا يرجح به المتعلق به عند التنازع ما لم يصر في يده والبسط فعل المالك فبقي أثر يد المالك فيه ما بقي فعله لعدم ما يزيله من النقل والتحويل .
Book of Ghasb: Carrying an animal (without permission) is ghasb; sitting on a carpet is not. And it is obligatory to return the item in the place of ghasb, or if one carries the animal and moves it from its place, or not. He said in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A person rode an animal without owner's permission, then dismounted and left it in its place; it is mentioned at the end of Kitab al-Luqta that he is liable; but the correct view is that he is not liable until he moves it; and in al-Ghiyathiyyah it is the chosen view, and in al-Muntaqa there is no liability. A man trespassed on the back of an animal without moving it, then another man came and slaughtered it; liability is on the one who slaughtered it. And in Ajnas al-Natifi: A man was breaking firewood; a boy came and said, 'Give me the axe so I can break instead of you.' The woodowner refused; the boy took the axe and broke [wood]; then some broken piece fell on the boy's eye; there is no liability on the woodowner. And if he sent a slave-girl to the slave trader to sell her, and the trader's wife sent her on an errand and she ran away, liability is on the woman. And in Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A slave-girl came to the trader and requested to be sold, then went away and it is not known where she went; the trader said, 'I returned her to her master'; the trader's word is accepted, and the meaning is that the trader did not take the slave-girl, and the meaning of return is ordering her to go to the master's house. If the trader took her or went with her to the master's house, he is not believed in his claim of having returned her. If the author had said 'and via using him, carrying, and moving' it would have been better for what you know. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (Not sitting on the carpet) because sitting on someone else's carpet is not a disposal (tasarruf) in it, and therefore the one sitting is not given precedence in case of dispute as long as it is not in his hand; and the spreading is the owner's action, so the effect of the owner's hand remains in it as long as his action remains, due to the absence of anything that removes it, like transfer and movement.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Ghasb defines what constitutes ghasb (unlawful appropriation). The author states that carrying an animal by riding it without permission is ghasb, but merely sitting on a carpet is not, because sitting does not involve a tangible act of taking possession. He then supports these positions with fatwas from earlier reliable scholars. For riding, the correct Hanafi view is that liability (daman) does not attach unless the rider actually moves the animal from its place. The various scenarios illustrate the nuanced application: if someone rides without moving, then another slaughters, the slaughters is liable; if a boy takes an axe despite refusal and gets hurt, the owner is not liable. The commentator notes that the original matn could have been clearer by explicitly mentioning 'using, carrying, and moving' as defining ghasb. The explanation for sitting clarifies that since the owner's act of spreading the carpet leaves his hand on it, sitting does not transfer possession.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful taking or using of another's property without consent, with a connotation of usurpation.
الحمل (al-haml): Carrying or riding an animal – considered an act of ghasb if done without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa cited, when does a rider who rides an animal without permission become liable (daman)?
A) Immediately upon riding
B) Only if he moves the animal from its place
C) Only if the animal is injured
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Ghasb requires a physical act of taking or moving property; sitting on a carpet without permission does not constitute ghasb because the owner's hand remains on it.

Return of the Usurped Item in Its Place
Establishes primary obligation and substitute rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary obligation in ghasb (return of the specific item in the place of usurpation) and explain the subsidiary rules when the item perishes or changes, applying the Hanafi position on the place of payment of value.

Core Idea
The usurper must return the very item at the place of ghasb; only when impossible does a substitute (mithl or qima) suffice.

This lesson explores the foundational ruling of ghasb (usurpation) in Hanafi fiqh: the obligation to return the usurped item exactly where it was taken. The passage cites prophetic hadith and rational reasoning to establish this primary duty, then details the transition to paying value or a like when the item is lost. Understanding these rules is crucial for both the usurper's liability and the owner's right to complete restitution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary obligation and its evidence
Read the opening sentence: 'It is obligatory to return the item in the place of its ghasb.' Note the two hadith proofs: 'The hand is responsible for what it takes until it returns' and 'It is not permissible to take a brother's wealth... if he takes it, he must return it.'
The imperative 'tarudd' in the hadith indicates wujub (obligation).
2
Understand the rational reasoning
The commentary explains that taking the item deprives the owner of possession (yad), which is essential for benefiting from ownership. Undoing the harm requires restoring possession at the place where it was lost, because wealth values differ by location.
This is a maqasid-based argument: the most just restoration.
3
Recognize return as the primary obligation
The text emphasizes that return of the specific item ('ayn) is the original obligation. Bringing a substitute (mithl or qima) while able to return the item is invalid. Returning without the owner's knowledge suffices, confirming that the duty is towards the item itself.
Ibra' (release) from liability while the item exists is valid, but ibra' from the item itself is not.
4
Note the shift to substitute when item is lost
If the item perishes or is destroyed, the obligation shifts to mithl (like) for fungible goods (mithliyyat) or qima (value) for non-fungibles (qimiyyat). The substitute must be paid in the place of ghasb.
The text gives examples: wheat, slaves, animals, and the exception of dirhams and dinars.
5
Apply the place-of-payment rule with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For items that have moved, the default is to pay value where the ghasb occurred. Abu Yusuf provides nuances: for wheat taken from Mecca to Baghdad, value in Mecca; for a slave, if owner is from Mecca, value in Mecca, otherwise return the slave. The mufta bihi is the primary obligation; these details guide the substitute.
This shows the school's practical flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإذن
قال رحمه الله : ( ويجب رد عينه في مكان غصبه لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام على اليد ما أخذت حتى ترد أي على صاحب اليد وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا يحل لأحد أن يأخذ مال أخيه لاعباً ولا جاداً وإن أخذه فليرده عليه والمعنى أنه لا يزيد أن يأخذه سرقة ولكن يريد إدخال الغيظ عليه، ولأنه بالأخذ فوت عليه اليد وهي مقصودة لأن المالك يتوصل بها إلى تحصيل ثمرات الملك من الانتفاع والتصرف ولهذا شرعت الكتابة مع أنها لا تفيد سوى اليد فيجب عليه نسخ فعله دفعاً للضرر عنه، وأتم وجوهه ردِ عينه في مكان غصبه لأن المال يختلف باختلاف الأماكن ورد العين هو الموجب الأصلي لأنه أعدل ورد القيمة أو المثل مخلص فيصار إليه عند تعذر رد العين، ولهذا لو أتى بالمثل أو القيمة عند القدرة على العين لا يعتد به ولو رد العين من غير علم المالك برئ منها ولو لم يكن هو الموجب الأصلي لما برئ إلا إذا علم وقبضه كما في قبض المثل أو القيمة وقبل الموجب الأصلي المثل أو القيمة ورد العين مخلص، ولهذا لو أبرأه عن حين قيام العين يصح حتى لا يجب عليه الضمان بالهلاك والإبراء عن العين لا يصح. ولو كان للغاصب نصاب ينتقص به كما ينتقص بالدين فدل على أن الواجب المثل أو القيمة ووجوب رده في مكان غصبه مقيد بما إذا لم يتعين بزيادة أو نقصان كما سيأتي، وكذلك هو الضمان
يجب أداء القيمة في مكان غصبه ففي الخانية : رجل غصب عبداً حسن الصوت فتغير صوته عند الغاصب كان له النقصان ولو كان العبد مغنياً فنسي ذلك عند الغاصب لا يضمن الغاصب، وفي المنتقى : غصب من آخر دواب بالكوفة فالمغصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء أخذها وإن شاء قيمتها بالكوفة قال وكذا الخادم وكذا ماله جمل ومؤنة إلا الدراهم والدنانير فإنه يأخذها حيث وجدها وإن اختلف السعر لأنها أثمان، وليس له أن يأخذ القيمة . .. وإن كان المغصوب مثلياً وقد هلك في يد الغاصب، فإن كان السعر في المكان الذي التقيا فيه مثل السعر في مكان الغصب أو أكثر برئ برد المثل، وإن كان في المكان الذي التقيا فيه أقل فهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذ قيمة العين حيث غصبه، وإن شاء انتظر. وفي الخانية : فإن كانت القيمة في المكانين سواء كان للمغصوب منه أن يطالبه بالثمن. وفيها عن أبي يوسف : رجل غصب حنطة بمكة وحملها إلى بغداد قال عليه قيمتها بمكة، ولو غصب غلاماً بمكة فجاء به إلى بغداد فإن كان صاحبه من أهل مكة عليه قيمته، وإن كان من غير أهل مكة أخذ غلامه وفي الينابيع قال ابن سماعة : سمعت أبا يوسف في رجل غصب عبداً فذهب به إلى قرية فلقيه المغصوب منه فخاصمه فهي بالخيار إن شاء أخذ عبده بعينه، وإن شاء أخذ قيمته يوم غصبه .اهـ. فلو زاد المؤلف ومكان غصبه حيث لا يتغير ولا يقل لكان أولى.
And permission? He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (It is obligatory to return the item in the place of its ghasb) because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'The hand is responsible for what it takes until it returns,' meaning the owner of the hand, and his saying (peace be upon him): 'It is not permissible for anyone to take his brother's wealth, whether in jest or in earnest; if he takes it, he must return it to him.' The meaning is that he did not take it as theft but intended to cause him annoyance. And because by taking he deprives him of possession (yad), which is intended since the owner attains the fruits of ownership, like benefit and disposal, through it; hence writing was legislated even though it only gives possession. So he must undo his action to remove harm, and the most complete way is to return the item in the place of ghasb because wealth differs by location, and return of the item is the primary obligation because it is most just. Return of value or like is a substitute, resorted to when return of the item is impossible. Therefore, if he brings the like or value while able to return the item, it is not accepted. If he returns the item without the owner's knowledge, he is freed from liability; if it were not the primary obligation, he would not be freed unless the owner knew and took possession, as with taking possession of the like or value. And before the primary obligation, the like or value is a substitute, and return of the item is a release. Therefore, if the owner releases him from [liability] while the item exists, it is valid such that no compensation is due upon destruction; but release from the item itself is not valid. And if the usurper has a nisab that is reduced as it is reduced by debt, it indicates that the obligation is the like or value, and the obligation to return it in the place of ghasb is conditional on it not being specified by increase or decrease, as will come. And similarly is the compensation. It is obligatory to pay the value in the place of ghasb; in al-Khaniyya: A man usurped a slave with a good voice and his voice changed while with the usurper; he is entitled to the deficiency. If the slave was a singer and forgot while with the usurper, the usurper is not liable. In al-Muntaqa: Someone usurped animals from another in Kufa; the victim has the choice: if he wishes he may take them, if he wishes he may take their value in Kufa. He said: similarly, a servant, and similarly, anything with transport and expense, except dirhams and dinars: he takes them wherever he finds them even if the price differs, because they are prices. And he may not take the value. And if the usurped property is a mithli (fungible) and has perished in the usurper's hands, and the price in the place where they met is like the price in the place of ghasb or higher, he is freed by returning the like. If it is lower in the place where they met, then the victim has the choice: if he wishes he may take the value of the item where he usurped it, and if he wishes he may wait. And in al-Khaniyya: If the value in the two places is equal, the victim may demand the price. And in it from Abu Yusuf: A man usurped wheat in Mecca and carried it to Baghdad; he said: he owes its value in Mecca. And if he usurped a slave boy in Mecca and brought him to Baghdad, then if his owner is from the people of Mecca, he owes his value; if he is not from the people of Mecca, he takes his slave. And in al-Yanabi', Ibn Sami'a said: I heard Abu Yusuf regarding a man who usurped a slave and went with him to a village; the victim met him and disputed; he has the choice: if he wishes he may take his slave himself, and if he wishes he may take his value on the day of ghasb. So if the author had added 'and the place of ghasb where it does not change or decrease,' it would have been better.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage establishes the primary obligation in ghasb: the usurper must return the very item he took, and he must do so at the place where the usurpation occurred. The author supports this with two hadiths: the principle that 'the hand is responsible until it returns,' and the command to return a brother's wealth taken in jest or earnestly. The reasoning is that taking the item deprives the owner of possession (yad), which is essential for realizing the benefits of ownership. The most complete way to undo the harm is to return the item itself where it was taken, because the value of property differs by location. Returning an equivalent (mithl) or value (qima) is only a substitute when the original is unavailable. The text then provides several subsidiary rulings: returning without the owner's knowledge suffices; release (ibra) from liability while the item exists is valid, but release from the item itself is not. The discussion also covers scenarios where the item has changed or perished, and the place of payment of value. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the primary obligation is the item itself, and if destroyed, the like (mithl) for fungibles and value for non-fungibles, with specific rules on location and timing.

Key Terms
رد العين (radd al-'ayn): Returning the specific item that was usurped, as the primary obligation.
المثل (al-mithl): An item equivalent in kind and quality (like) owed when the usurped item is destroyed and it is mithli (fungible).
القيمة (al-qima): Monetary value of the usurped item, owed when it is qimi (non-fungible) or mithli is unavailable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, if the usurped item is still in existence, can the usurper pay its value instead and be discharged?
A) Yes, if the owner agrees
B) Yes, in all cases
C) No, the item must be returned
D) Only if the item has been damaged

Key Takeaway
The primary obligation in ghasb is to return the exact item in the place where it was taken; only if that is impossible does a substitute (mithl or qima) suffice, and it too must be discharged in the place of ghasb.

Obligation of Like or Value Upon Destruction: Definition, Exceptions, and Valuation Timing
Establishes obligation of mithl for fungibles and intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of mithl for fungibles, identify exceptions, and explain the three Hanafi positions on valuation timing of extinct mithli.

Core Idea
When a fungible (mithli) is destroyed, the usurper owes its like (mithl); if extinct, the three imams differ on valuation day.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the obligation when usurped property is destroyed. It defines what counts as mithli and when value is owed instead. The intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation timing is a classic example of how the madhhab accommodates different perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling: mithl for mithli, qima for qimi
The passage states that if the usurped item is mithli (fungible), the usurper must pay its like (mithl). For non-fungibles, value is owed.
2
Define mithli according to the text
The text gives examples: measured/weighed items, roughly equal countable items, eggs, current coins. But exceptions exist: broken halwa and preserved oil are treated as qimi.
Also note the definition of extinction: not found in the market where sold, even if found in houses.
3
Analyze the valuation timing dispute
When the mithli becomes extinct (no longer available in the market), value must be assessed. The three Hanafi imams differ: Abu Hanifa says day of suit, Abu Yusuf day of ghasb, Muhammad day of extinction. Each has a proof.
Abu Hanifa: mithl liability remains until judge decrees value; Abu Yusuf: extinct mithli becomes qimi; Muhammad: inability on day of extinction.
4
Understand the definition of extinction
Extinction means the item is not found in the market where it is sold, even if found in houses.
This distinguishes market availability from personal possession.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The amount of compensation depends on price fluctuations at the different dates.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view (day of suit).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (أو مثله أن هلك وهو مثلي يعني يجب عليه مثل المغصوب إن هلك عنده لقوله تعالى فمن اعتدى عليكم فاعتدوا عليه بمثل ما اعتدى عليكم [البقرة : ١٩٤] ولأن حق المالك ثابت في الصورة والمعنى وقد أمكن اعتبارهما بإيجاب المثل فكان أعدل وأتم فكان إيجابه أولى من القيمة. وأطلق في المثل فشمل الناطف المبذر والدهن المربى. وفي التتارخانية بر قوم ومشايخنا استثنوا من الموزونات الناطف المبذر والدهن المربى فقالوا : لواجب القيمة فيهما وفي السير الكبير ومن أتلف على آخر جبنه فعليه أنه قيمة الجبن مع مثلي ،موزون والمراد بالمثلي المكيل والموزون الذي ليس في تبعيضه ضرر والعدد المتقارب والبيض والفلوس الرائجة وما أشبه ذلك من العددي الذي لا يتفاوت. قال رحمه الله : (وإن انصرم المثلي فقيمته الخصومة) يعني إذا انقطع المثلي عن أيدي الناس يوم يجب على الغاصب قيمته يوم الخصومة وهذا قول الإمام. وقال الثاني: يوم الغصب. وقال محمد: يوم الانقطاع لأن المثل هو الواجب والقيمة إنما يصار إليها للعجز عنه والعجز في يوم الانقطاع فيعتبر فيه وللثاني أن المثل لما انقطع التحق بالقيمي وفيه يعتبر القيمة يوم الغصب وللإمام أن المثل هو الواجب بالغصب وهو باق في ذمته ما لم يقض القاضي بالقيمة ولهذا لو صبر إلى أن يعود المثل كان له ذلك وحد الانقطاع أن لا يوجد في السوق الذي يباع فيه وإن كان يوجد في البيوت؛ ذكره في النهاية. وقال في النهاية فإن قلت :
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (Or its like if it has perished and it is mithli) meaning he must give the like of the usurped property if it perishes in his possession, due to His saying: 'Then whoever has assaulted you, assault him in the like manner as he assaulted you' [2:194], and because the owner's right is established in form and meaning, and it is possible to consider both by imposing the like, so it is more just and complete, and imposing it is better than value. And he made an absolute statement about the like, so it includes the broken halwa and preserved oil. In al-Tatarakhaniyya: Some people and our shaykhs excepted from weighed items the broken halwa and preserved oil, saying: value is obligatory for them. And in al-Siyar al-Kabir: Whoever destroys someone else's cheese owes the value of the cheese, even though it is mithli (weighed). The meaning of mithli is measured or weighed items whose division does not cause harm, and roughly equal countable items, eggs, current coins, and similar countable items that do not vary. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And if the mithli becomes extinct, then its value is on the day of suit) meaning if the mithli disappears from people's hands, the usurper owes its value on the day of suit. This is the Imam's (Abu Hanifa) saying. The second (Abu Yusuf) said: on the day of ghasb. Muhammad said: on the day of extinction, because the like is the obligation, and value is only resorted to due to inability, and inability is on the day of extinction, so it is considered then. And for the second, when the like becomes extinct it joins the category of qimi (non-fungible), and for that value is considered on the day of ghasb. And for the Imam, the like is the obligation from the ghasb, and it remains in his liability unless the judge decrees value, and therefore if he waits until the like returns, he may do so. The definition of extinction is that it is not found in the market where it is sold, even if found in houses; mentioned in al-Nihaya. And in al-Nihaya he said: 'If you say...'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation when the usurped item is destroyed. If the item is mithli (a fungible good like measured or weighed items, eggs, coins), the usurper owes its like (mithl). The Quranic verse 'assault in like manner' and the principle of justice support this. The definition of mithli is given: items that can be substituted without significant difference, excluding items like broken halwa and preserved oil where value is owed. Then it addresses the case where the mithli becomes extinct (no longer available in the market). There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the timings of valuation. Abu Hanifa says the value is assessed on the day of suit (day of litigation). Abu Yusuf says the day of ghasb. Muhammad says the day of extinction. Each has their reasoning: Abu Hanifa considers that the mithl remains in liability until the judge replaces it with value; Abu Yusuf treats the extinct mithli as a qimi; Muhammad considers the inability arises at extinction. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated but often Abu Hanifa's view is preferred. The text also defines the point of extinction: when it is not found in the market where it is sold, even if available in houses.

Key Terms
المثلي (al-mithli): A fungible item that can be replaced by an equivalent in kind and measure without significant difference.
انصرم (insarama): Extinction, meaning the mithli becomes unavailable in the market where it is sold.
القيمي (al-qimi): A non-fungible item that cannot be replaced by like; value is assessed.
الخصومة (al-khusuma): Lawsuit, litigation; the day of suit for valuation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in the text, when is a mithli considered extinct?
A) When it cannot be found anywhere in the world
B) When it is not found in the market where it is sold, even if found in houses
C) When it is not found in the buyer's house
D) When the seller declares it unavailable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes mithl for fungibles; when extinct, valuation timing follows Abu Hanifa's view (day of suit) as the mufta bihi.

Valuation of Usurped Property: Mithli and Qimi
Explanation of compensation rules for usurped property with intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi obligation for usurped fungibles (mithli) and non-fungibles (qimi), explain the three Hanafi positions on valuation date for mithli when unavailable, and compare the Hanafi view with that of Imam Malik on qimi.

Core Idea
For fungibles, return the like; for non-fungibles, pay the value at usurpation. The valuation date for unavailable fungibles is disputed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the central rules of compensation for usurped property. You already know that usurpation (ghasb) is a prohibited act. Here, we focus on what must be paid when the property is destroyed: the like for fungibles (mithli) or value for non-fungibles (qimi). The author presents the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about the valuation date when a fungible is no longer available, and finally defends the Hanafi position that non-fungibles are compensated by value against Imam Malik's opposing view. By the end, you will navigate these rulings and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish mithli from qimi
Identify the two categories of property: fungibles (mithli) are items that can be replaced by their like in kind and measure (e.g., grains, eggs), while non-fungibles (qimi) are items with inherent variation (e.g., animals, woven goods). The legal obligation differs: for mithli, the like (mithl) must be returned; for qimi, the value must be paid.
Hanafis define mithli as measured (kayl) or weighed (wazn) items whose division does not cause harm, plus roughly equal countable items.
2
State the ruling for mithli and the Quranic evidence
If a usurped fungible perishes, the usurper must return its like (mithl). This is derived from the Quranic command (2:194): 'Whoever transgresses against you, transgress against him with the like of his transgression.' The imperative indicates obligation, and the phrase 'bi mithli' mandates the like.
The verse is qati' al-thubut (certain transmission) but zanni al-dalala (the meaning is interpretative). Hanafi usul treats such commands as obligating wujub, not fard.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation date when mithli is unavailable
When the mithli can no longer be found in the market (inqita'), the obligation shifts to value. The three Hanafi masters disagree on the valuation date: Abu Hanifa – day of lawsuit; Abu Yusuf – day of usurpation; Muhammad – day of disappearance. Understand each reasoning.
Abu Hanifa: the mithl remains a debt until the judge decrees value. Abu Yusuf: unavailable mithli becomes like qimi, so value at tort. Muhammad: inability to return occurs at disappearance.
4
Analyse the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam Malik on qimi
For non-fungibles (qimi), the Hanafi school holds value at the day of usurpation by consensus. Imam Malik disagrees, arguing that even non-fungibles must be replaced by their like, citing the hadith of Aisha breaking and replacing a bowl. The Hanafi response distinguishes between obligation and generosity, and cites the hadith of the freed slave where 'value' is explicitly mentioned.
The practical consequence: Hanafi pays market value; Maliki must procure a similar item.
5
Apply the rulings to subsidiary cases
Review the branch rulings: changes in value, sale by usurper, killing of a slave, etc. Note that all follow the same principles: for mithli, the mithl is primary; for qimi, value at usurpation is fixed regardless of later fluctuation.
Special rules apply to currency (dirhams and dinars) which are always taken wherever found.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بإيجاب المثل فكان أعدل وأتم فكان إيجابه أولى من القيمة. وأطلق في المثل فشمل الناطف المبذر والدهن المربى. وفي التتارخانية بر قوم ومشايخنا استثنوا من الموزونات الناطف المبذر والدهن المربى فقالوا : لواجب القيمة فيهما وفي السير الكبير ومن أتلف على آخر جبنه فعليه أنه قيمة الجبن مع مثلي ،موزون والمراد بالمثلي المكيل والموزون الذي ليس في تبعيضه ضرر والعدد المتقارب والبيض والفلوس الرائجة وما أشبه ذلك من العددي الذي لا يتفاوت. قال رحمه الله : (وإن انصرم المثلي فقيمته الخصومة) يعني إذا انقطع المثلي عن أيدي الناس يوم يجب على الغاصب قيمته يوم الخصومة وهذا قول الإمام. وقال الثاني: يوم الغصب. وقال محمد: يوم الانقطاع لأن المثل هو الواجب والقيمة إنما يصار إليها للعجز عنه والعجز في يوم الانقطاع فيعتبر فيه وللثاني أن المثل لما انقطع التحق بالقيمي وفيه يعتبر القيمة يوم الغصب وللإمام أن المثل هو الواجب بالغصب وهو باق في ذمته ما لم يقض القاضي بالقيمة ولهذا لو صبر إلى أن يعود المثل كان له ذلك وحد الانقطاع أن لا يوجد في السوق الذي يباع فيه وإن كان يوجد في البيوت؛ ذكره في النهاية. وقال في النهاية فإن قلت :

ولم قدم قول أبي يوسف في التعليل ولم يوسطه كما هو حقه؟ قلت: لعله أن يكون هو المختار لأنه أعدل الأقوال .

حجرة
عائشة

قال رحمه الله : (وما لا مثل له فقيمته يوم غصبه وهذا بالإجماع وهو المذروع والحيوان والمعدودات المتفاوتة، والوزني الذي يضره التبعيض لأنه تعذر اعتبار المثل صورة ومعنى وهو الكامل فوجب اعتبار المثل معنى وهو القيمة لأنها تقوم مقامه ويحصل بها مثله واسمها ينبئ عنه. وقال الإمام مالك : يضمن مثله صورة لما روي عن أنس رضي الله عنه قال : كنت في الله رضي عنها قبل أن يضرب الحجاب فأتى بقصعة من ثريد بعض أرواجه عليه الصلاة والسلام فضربت عائشة القصعة بيدها فكسرتها وجاءت بقصعة مثل تلك القصعة في يدها فاستحسن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذلك منها الحديث ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في عبد بين رجلين يعتق أحدهما نصيبه فإن كان موسراً ضمن نصيب الآخر، وإن كان معسراً سعى العبد في قيمة نصيب شريكه. وهذا نص صريح في اعتبار القيمة فيما لا مثل له والآية شاهدة لنا لأنه هو المثل المتعارف بين الناس، وفعل عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها كان على طريق المروءة ومكارم الأخلاف لا على طريق الواجب إذ كانت القصعتان للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال صاحب النهاية : وتحقيقه أن معناه الشيء الذي لا يضمن بمثله من جنسه لأن الذي لا مثل له في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى .اهـ. فعلى هذا كان على المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى أن يغير العبارة فيقول وما لا مثل له من جنسه. وأطلق في قوله يوم غصبه فشمل ما إذا زادت قيمته بعده أو نقصت أو استمرت على حالة واحدة وفي شرح الطحاوي: ولو غصب من رجل عبداً أو جارية ثمنها ألف درهم فازدادت قيمته أو نقصت ثم هلك عنده ضمن قيمته يوم غصبه بالإجماع ولو لم يهلك ورده على صاحبه، فإن كان النقصان في القدر ضمن قيمة النقصان، وإن كان النقصان في السعر لا يضمن. وشمل ما إذا هلك أو استهلكه بعد زيادة القيمة أو نقصانها أو استمرارها على حالة واحدة وأما إذا هلك أو استهلكه في يد الغاصب أو المشري من الغاصب. وفي شرح الطحاوي ولو هلك بعد الزيادة نحو أن يبيعه ويسلمه إلى المشتري فهلك في يد المشتري فالمغصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الغاصب قيمته يوم الغصب وجاز البيع والثمن للغاصب، وإن شاء ضمن المشتري قيمته وقت القبض وبطل البيع ويرجع المشتري على الغاصب بالثمن، ولو زادت قيمة العبد فقتله الغاصب ضمن عاقلته قيمة العبد يوم الغصب زائدة في ثلاث سنين وليس له أن يضمن الغاصب قيمته وقت التسليم على قول الإمام، وفي قولهما له أن يضمن الغاصب قيمته يوم الغصب حالاً وإن شاء ضمن العاقلة قيمته يوم القتل زائدة في ثلاث سنين. ولو كان المغصوب حيواناً سوى بني آدم فقتله الغاصب بعد الزيادة عند الإمام لا يضمن إلا قيمة الغصب، وعندهما المغصوب منه بالخيار وفي الفتاوى العتابية ولو زاد العبد ثم قتل
يوم
Book of Usurpation. Its like if it perishes and it is fungible, and if the fungible becomes unavailable, its value is on the day of lawsuit, and what has no like, its value is on the day of its usurpation. In al-Khaniyya: A man usurped a slave with a good voice and his voice changed at the usurper, he is liable for the decrease. If the slave was a singer and forgot that at the usurper, the usurper is not liable. In al-Muntaqa: He usurped animals in Kufa, the victim has the option: if he wishes he takes them, if he wishes their value in Kufa. He said: likewise a servant, and likewise what has transportation and maintenance except dirhams and dinars, for he takes them wherever he finds them even if prices differ because they are prices. He may not take the value. If the usurped was fungible and perished in the usurper's hand, if the price in the place where they meet is like the price in the place of usurpation or more, he is discharged by returning the like; if in the place where they meet it is less, he has the option: if he wishes he takes the value of the property where he usurped it, if he wishes he waits. In al-Khaniyya: if the value in the two places is equal, the victim may demand the price from him. In it from Abu Yusuf: A man usurped wheat in Makka and carried it to Baghdad, he said: he owes its value in Makka. If he usurped a boy in Makka and brought him to Baghdad, if his owner is from Makka, he owes his value; if from elsewhere, he takes his boy. In al-Yanabi', Ibn Sama'a said: I heard Abu Yusuf regarding a man who usurped a slave and went to a village, the victim met him and argued with him: he has the option: if he wishes he takes his slave in kind, if he wishes he takes his value on the day of usurpation. If the author had added 'and the place of his usurpation where it neither changes nor decreases' it would be better. He (the author) said: ('Or its like if it perishes and it is fungible' meaning he must give the like of the usurped if it perishes with him, due to His statement: 'Whoever transgresses against you, transgress against him with the like of his transgression' [2:194], and because the owner's right is established in form and meaning, and it was possible to consider both by imposing the like, so it is more just and complete, so imposing it is better than value. He made the like absolute, so it includes liquid sweet and clarified butter. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: Our scholars excepted from weighed items liquid sweet and clarified butter, saying: value is obligatory in them. In al-Siyar al-Kabir: Whoever destroys another's cheese owes the value of cheese, even though cheese is fungible, weighed. What is meant by fungible is measured and weighed items whose division causes no harm, and roughly equal countable items, eggs, current small coins, and similar countable items that do not vary. He (the author) said: ('If the fungible becomes unavailable, its value is at the lawsuit') meaning if the fungible disappears from people's hands, the day the usurper must pay its value is the day of lawsuit. This is the statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). The Second (Abu Yusuf) said: the day of usurpation. Muhammad said: the day of disappearance, because the like is what is owed, and value is resorted to only due to inability, and inability occurs on the day of disappearance, so it is considered. For the Second (Abu Yusuf): once the like disappears, it becomes like a non-fungible, and in that case value is considered on the day of usurpation. For the Imam (Abu Hanifa): the like is owed by usurpation and remains a debt until the judge rules for value, and that is why if he waits until the like returns, he may do so. The limit of disappearance is that it is not found in the market where it is sold, even if it is found in houses; mentioned in al-Nihaya. And in al-Nihaya he said: If you say: why did he present Abu Yusuf's statement in the reasoning and not place it in the middle as is its due? I say: perhaps it is the chosen view because it is the most balanced of the opinions. The chamber of Aisha. He (the author) said: ('And what has no like, its value is on the day of its usurpation') and this is by consensus. It includes woven items, animals, varying countable items, and weighed items whose division causes harm, because considering the like in form and meaning is impossible, and considering the like in meaning is obligatory, which is value, because it stands in its place and its like is achieved, and its name indicates. Imam Malik said: he is liable for its like in form, due to what is narrated from Anas: I was in the house of Aisha before the hijab was imposed, a bowl of tharid was brought from one of the wives of the Prophet, Aisha struck the bowl with her hand and broke it, and came with a bowl like that bowl in her hand, and the Messenger of Allah approved of that from her. And our proof is his statement regarding a slave between two men, one of whom frees his share: if he is wealthy, he guarantees the other's share; if poor, the slave works to pay the value of the partner's share. This is a clear text regarding considering value for what has no like. The verse is evidence for us because it is the customary like among people. Aisha's action was out of generosity and noble character, not obligatory, since both bowls belonged to the Prophet. The author of al-Nihaya said: Its verification is that it means something for which its like from its genus is not guaranteed, because that which has no like in reality is only Allah. Therefore, the author should change the expression and say: 'and what has no like from its genus.' He made absolute in his saying 'on the day of its usurpation' so it includes if its value increases after, decreases, or remains constant. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If someone usurps a male or female slave worth one thousand dirhams, its value increases or decreases, then perishes with him, he guarantees its value on the day of usurpation by consensus. If it does not perish and he returns it, if the decrease is in quality, he guarantees the value of the decrease; if the decrease is in price, he does not guarantee. It includes if it perishes or is consumed after increase or decrease or remaining constant. As for if it perishes or is consumed in the hand of the usurper or the buyer from the usurper. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If it perishes after increase, such as by selling and delivering to the buyer and it perishes in the buyer's hand, the victim has the option: if he wishes he makes the usurper liable for its value on the day of usurpation, and the sale is valid and the price belongs to the usurper; if he wishes he makes the buyer liable for its value at the time of receipt, and the sale is void, and the buyer recovers the price from the usurper. If the slave's value increases and the usurper kills him, his 'aqila guarantees the slave's value on the day of usurpation increased, payable over three years, and he cannot make the usurper liable for its value at the time of delivery according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he may make the usurper liable for its value on the day of usurpation immediately, or if he wishes make the 'aqila liable for its value on the day of killing increased over three years. If the usurped was an animal besides humans and the usurper kills it after the increase, according to Imam he is only liable for the value at the time of usurpation, and according to the two the victim has the option. In al-Fatawa al-'Attabiyya: If the slave increased then was killed... day.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq commentates on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the valuation date for usurped property. The author begins by presenting the matn: for a fungible (mithli) that perishes, the obligation is to return its mithl; if the mithl becomes unavailable, the value is assessed on the day of lawsuit (yawm al-khusuma). For non-fungibles (qimi), the value is on the day of usurpation (yawm al-ghasb). He then provides subsidiary rulings from various Hanafi sources.
The core legal issue is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on when the value is due if the fungible is no longer available. Imam Abu Hanifa holds it is the day of lawsuit because the mithl remains a debt until the judge decrees otherwise; the creditor may wait for the mithl to reappear. Abu Yusuf holds it is the day of usurpation because once the mithl is unavailable, the item becomes like a qimi, where value is assessed at the time of the tort. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is the day the mithl becomes unavailable (yawm al-inqita'), as inability to return the mithl arises then. The author notes that in al-Nihaya, the most balanced view is attributed to Abu Yusuf.
For non-fungibles (qimi), the author states there is consensus (within the Hanafi school) that value is assessed on the day of usurpation, citing the hadith of the two slaves freed by one owner. He then rebuts Imam Malik's view that even non-fungibles should be compensated by their like, using the hadith of Aisha breaking and replacing the bowl. The Hanafi position distinguishes between fungibles and non-fungibles based on the hadith of the slave, which explicitly mentions value.
The passage then details numerous branches: changes in value, sale by the usurper, increase before destruction, killing by the usurper, etc., all consistently applying the rule that for qimi, value is at the day of usurpation, and for mithli, the mithl is owed until impossibility.

Key Terms
المثلي (al-mithli): A fungible item that can be replaced by its like in kind and measure without significant variation, such as grains, oils, and eggs.
القيمي (al-qimi): A non-fungible item that cannot be replaced by its like due to inherent variation, such as animals and woven items.
الخصومة (al-khusuma): The lawsuit; the day the case is adjudicated, which is the valuation date for mithli that has become unavailable according to Abu Hanifa.
الانقطاع (al-inqita'): The disappearance of the fungible from the market where it is sold, even if found in private homes; the trigger for shifting to value according to Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the valuation date for a usurped fungible (mithli) that has become unavailable?
A) Value is assessed on the day of usurpation.
B) Value is assessed on the day of disappearance of the mithl.
C) Value is assessed on the day of the lawsuit.
D) The obligor must wait indefinitely until the mithl returns.

Key Takeaway
For fungibles, the like is owed; for non-fungibles, value at usurpation. The valuation date for unavailable fungibles is disputed among the Hanafi imams, with Abu Hanifa's lawsuit day, Abu Yusuf's usurpation day, and Muhammad's disappearance day.

Compensation for Non-Equivalent Items: Hanafi Rule and Maliki Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on compensation for non-equivalent usurped items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for compensating usurped items without an equivalent, identify the Maliki counter-argument, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
When an exact equivalent is impossible, compensation is by value, not replacement in kind.

This lesson explores a key dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding the compensation for usurped property that cannot be replaced exactly. The Hanafi school requires payment of market value on the day of usurpation, citing a hadith about a co-owned slave. Imam Malik, however, argues for replacement in kind, relying on an incident from Aisha. We examine both proofs and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi rule
State the rule: for items without an equivalent, compensation is by value on the day of ghasb.
The rule is wajib, not optional.
2
List the categories
Which items fall under this rule? (Produce, animals, diverse countable, weighed items harmed by division)
These are qimi items.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The hadith of the co-owned slave: 'if he is wealthy, he guarantees the share of his partner; if poor, the slave works to pay the value.'
Explicit mention of value.
4
Understand the Maliki objection
Imam Malik argues for replacement in kind, using the story of Aisha replacing a broken bowl with a similar one.
Maliki proof is an action of a Companion.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: Aisha's act was voluntary (muru'a), not obligatory, and the hadith is explicit about value.
The stronger textual proof prevails.
6
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: monetary compensation vs. physical replacement.
Important for real-world application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجرة عائشة قال رحمه الله : (وما لا مثل له فقيمته يوم غصبه وهذا بالإجماع وهو المذروع والحيوان والمعدودات المتفاوتة، والوزني الذي يضره التبعيض لأنه تعذر اعتبار المثل صورة ومعنى وهو الكامل فوجب اعتبار المثل معنى وهو القيمة لأنها تقوم مقامه ويحصل بها مثله واسمها ينبئ عنه. وقال الإمام مالك : يضمن مثله صورة لما روي عن أنس رضي الله عنه قال : كنت في الله رضي عنها قبل أن يضرب الحجاب فأتى بقصعة من ثريد بعض أرواجه عليه الصلاة والسلام فضربت عائشة القصعة بيدها فكسرتها وجاءت بقصعة مثل تلك القصعة في يدها فاستحسن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذلك منها الحديث ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في عبد بين رجلين يعتق أحدهما نصيبه فإن كان موسراً ضمن نصيب الآخر، وإن كان معسراً سعى العبد في قيمة نصيب شريكه. وهذا نص صريح في اعتبار القيمة فيما لا مثل له والآية شاهدة لنا لأنه هو المثل المتعارف بين الناس، وفعل عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها كان على طريق المروءة ومكارم الأخلاف لا على طريق الواجب إذ كانت القصعتان للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال صاحب النهاية : وتحقيقه أن معناه الشيء الذي لا يضمن بمثله من جنسه لأن الذي لا مثل له في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى .اهـ. فعلى هذا كان على المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى أن يغير العبارة فيقول وما لا مثل له من جنسه.
[Chamber of Aisha] He (the author) said: (And that which has no equivalent, its value [is due] on the day of its usurpation, and this is by consensus. It includes measured produce, animals, qualitatively different countable items, and weighed items that suffer from division. Because considering an equivalent in form and meaning is impossible, and it is complete [in value], so considering the equivalent in meaning—which is value—becomes obligatory, because it stands in its place and its equivalent is attained. Its name indicates it. Imam Malik said: He guarantees its equivalent in form, based on what was narrated from Anas (may Allah be pleased with him) that he was in [the chamber of] Aisha before the hijab was prescribed, and a bowl of tharid was brought from some of the wives of the Prophet; Aisha struck the bowl with her hand and broke it, and brought a bowl like that bowl in her hand, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) approved of that from her. As for us, we have his saying (peace be upon him) regarding a slave between two men, one of whom frees his share: if he is wealthy, he guarantees the share of his partner; if he is poor, the slave works to pay the value of the partner's share. This is explicit text in considering value for that which has no equivalent, and the verse testifies for us because it is the mutually recognized equivalent among people. The action of Aisha was out of chivalry and noble character, not by way of obligation, since the two bowls belonged to the Prophet. The author of al-Nihaya said: Its verification is that the meaning is the thing that is not guaranteed by its like from its genus, because that which has no equivalent in reality is Allah the Exalted. So according to this, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) should have changed the expression and said: 'that which has no equivalent from its genus.'
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the compensation for usurped property that has no exact equivalent (mithl). The Hanafi ruling is that such items—like foodstuffs, animals, and diverse countable goods—are compensated by their market value on the day of usurpation. This is based on the hadith of the co-owned slave: if one partner frees his share and is wealthy, he must pay the value of the other's share; if poor, the slave works for it. This proves value, not a physical replacement. Imam Malik argues for replacement in kind, citing the incident where Aisha broke a bowl and replaced it with a similar one, which the Prophet approved. The Hanafi response interprets Aisha's act as voluntary (muru'a), not obligatory, since the bowls belonged to the Prophet and she did not own them. Furthermore, the hadith about the slave explicitly uses value. The author of al-Nihaya clarifies that 'no equivalent' means no exact match within its genus—only Allah has no equivalent at all.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl): An exact equivalent in form and substance from the same genus.
قيمة (qima): Market value, used when mithl is unavailable.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking property without right and with force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is a usurped item without an exact equivalent compensated?
A) By paying its market value on the day of usurpation.
B) By returning a similar item from the same genus.
C) By paying twice its value.
D) By returning the original item or its equivalent.

Key Takeaway
For items without an exact equivalent, the Hanafi school requires payment of market value on the day of usurpation, based on explicit proof from the Sunnah.

Compensation for Value Change After Ghasb
Detailed rules for compensation when value fluctuates after usurpation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that compensation for usurped property is fixed at the value on the day of ghasb, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two disciples in cases of killing.

Core Idea
Compensation for usurped property is fixed at the value on the day of usurpation, regardless of later changes.

When a usurped item changes in value before its return or destruction, the Hanafi school establishes clear rules for compensation based on the value at the moment of usurpation. This lesson unpacks the key scenarios—perishing, return with defects, sale to a third party, and killing—along with the differences between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Recognize that the general principle is compensation measured at the value on the day of ghasb (qimat yawm al-ghasb). This is the benchmark for all scenarios.
This is stated explicitly in Sharh al-Tahawi.
2
Analyze the scenarios
Work through the four scenarios: (1) item perishes – pay value at ghasb; (2) item returned with physical defect – pay difference in value; (3) market price drop – no compensation; (4) sale to a third party – victim has a choice.
Pay attention to the distinction between nuqsan fi al-qadr and nuqsan fi al-sa'r.
3
Examine the killing scenario
When the usurper kills a slave after increase, compare Abu Hanifa's view (only 'aqila pays value at ghasb in installments) with the disciples' view (victim can choose immediate payment from usurper or increased value from 'aqila).
The 'aqila pays over three years according to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply to animals
Understand that for non-human animals, Abu Hanifa limits compensation to value at ghasb, while the disciples give the victim a choice. This extends the same ikhtilaf.
The text references 'animal other than humans' (hayawan siwa bani adam).
5
Recognize practical implications
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: the difference affects who pays (usurper vs. 'aqila) and when (immediately vs. installments). Later fatwa may prefer the disciples' view for greater victim protection.
The mufta bihi may vary; both views are valid within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في قوله يوم غصبه فشمل ما إذا زادت قيمته بعده أو نقصت أو استمرت على حالة واحدة وفي شرح الطحاوي: ولو غصب من رجل عبداً أو جارية ثمنها ألف درهم فازدادت قيمته أو نقصت ثم هلك عنده ضمن قيمته يوم غصبه بالإجماع ولو لم يهلك ورده على صاحبه، فإن كان النقصان في القدر ضمن قيمة النقصان، وإن كان النقصان في السعر لا يضمن. وشمل ما إذا هلك أو استهلكه بعد زيادة القيمة أو نقصانها أو استمرارها على حالة واحدة وأما إذا هلك أو استهلكه في يد الغاصب أو المشري من الغاصب. وفي شرح الطحاوي ولو هلك بعد الزيادة نحو أن يبيعه ويسلمه إلى المشتري فهلك في يد المشتري فالمغصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الغاصب قيمته يوم الغصب وجاز البيع والثمن للغاصب، وإن شاء ضمن المشتري قيمته وقت القبض وبطل البيع ويرجع المشتري على الغاصب بالثمن، ولو زادت قيمة العبد فقتله الغاصب ضمن عاقلته قيمة العبد يوم الغصب زائدة في ثلاث سنين وليس له أن يضمن الغاصب قيمته وقت التسليم على قول الإمام، وفي قولهما له أن يضمن الغاصب قيمته يوم الغصب حالاً وإن شاء ضمن العاقلة قيمته يوم القتل زائدة في ثلاث سنين. ولو كان المغصوب حيواناً سوى بني آدم فقتله الغاصب بعد الزيادة عند الإمام لا يضمن إلا قيمة الغصب، وعندهما المغصوب منه بالخيار وفي الفتاوى العتابية ولو زاد العبد ثم قتل يوم غصبه
And he gave absolute expression in his saying 'on the day of its usurpation', so it includes if its value increased after it, or decreased, or remained at one state. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If a person usurped a male or female slave whose price was a thousand dirhams, then its value increased or decreased, then it perished in his possession, he guarantees its value on the day of its usurpation by consensus. And if it did not perish and he returned it to its owner, then if the deficiency was in quantity, he guarantees the value of the deficiency; and if the deficiency was in price, he does not guarantee. And it includes if it perished or was consumed after increase or decrease or remaining at one state. As for if it perished or was consumed in the hand of the usurper or the buyer from the usurper. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If it perished after increase, such as when he sold it and delivered it to the buyer, then it perished in the buyer's hand, then the owner of the usurped property has the choice: if he wishes, he can make the usurper guarantee its value on the day of usurpation, and the sale is valid and the price belongs to the usurper; and if he wishes, he can make the buyer guarantee its value at the time of receipt, and the sale is void, and the buyer recovers the price from the usurper. And if the value of the slave increased and the usurper killed him, his 'aqila guarantees the value of the slave on the day of usurpation increased over three years, and he does not have the right to make the usurper guarantee its value at the time of delivery according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], he has the right to make the usurper guarantee its value on the day of usurpation immediately, and if he wishes, he can make the 'aqila guarantee its value on the day of killing increased over three years. And if the usurped property was an animal other than humans, and the usurper killed it after increase, according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] he only guarantees the value at the time of ghasb; according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], the owner has a choice. And in al-Fatawa al-'Attabiyya: If the slave increased in value then he killed [him] on the day of his ghasb...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling for compensation when the value of the usurped item changes over time. The general principle is that the value on the day of ghasb is the benchmark. If the item perishes, the usurper pays that value regardless of later increases or decreases. If the item is returned and there is a physical defect (nuqsan fi al-qadr), the usurper pays the difference; if it's a market price drop, no compensation is due. Special scenarios include sale to a third party: the victim can choose to claim from the usurper (value at ghasb) or from the buyer (value at receipt). In cases of killing a slave, the 'aqila (blood relatives) pay in installments, while the immediate liability differs between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples. For animals, Abu Hanifa limits compensation to the value at ghasb, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad give the victim a choice. The passage ends with a reference to al-Fatawa al-'Attabiyya on a similar case.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqila): The paternal relatives who pay diyah in case of unintentional killing.
قيمة يوم الغصب (qimat yawm al-ghasb): The market value at the exact time of usurpation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurped item is returned with a physical defect (nuqsan fi al-qadr), what is the ruling regarding compensation?
A) No compensation is required.
B) The usurper must pay the difference in value between the item's condition at ghasb and its defective condition upon return.
C) The usurper must pay the current market value of the item.
D) The usurper must replace the item with an identical one.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compensation for usurped property is calculated based on the value at the moment of usurpation, with special options in cases of sale or killing.
